Commentary
This passage presents the proof that an infinite series of simultaneous originated events is impossible. The commentator first states that the ordered events are either simultaneous (invalid due to the proof of application) or not. For simultaneous existence, the set itself would be originated and require a condition, which would both be inside the set (as one of the events) and outside it (as its condition), a contradiction. (2) That the argument applies to succession—he responds that in succession only one event exists at a time, so no set problem. (3) That the condition could be outside the set, avoiding interior contradiction—he responds that all conditions are included. Finally, he discusses the difference between a non-existent thing and non-existence, and the need for a locus, deferring to later discussions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the condition for the set can be completely outside the set, thereby avoiding the contradiction.
Correction: The commentator shows that if we consider all conditions, any such condition is still part of the set of conditions for the events, so it is not truly outside.

Checkpoint
What is the commentator's response to the objection that the condition could be outside the set?
A) He accepts the objection and abandons the proof.
B) He says the set includes all conditions, so the condition is already inside.
C) He argues that the condition must be outside because it is prior.
D) He ignores the objection.

Key Takeaway
The condition for the set cannot truly be outside because all conditions are included in the set; further, a non-existent thing is distinct from non-existence, a nuance for later discussions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every event in your life depends on an infinite chain of conditions, where does your own ultimate dependence lie? Does this passage force you to confront the reality that without the Necessary Being, you are merely a contingent link? Will you today trace one condition of your existence back to its ultimate source and acknowledge your absolute need for Allah?
?
The passage distinguishes between a non-existent thing (ʿadamī) and pure non-existence (ʿadam). Are you living as if your potential non-existence is a mere concept, or do you truly realize that at every moment you are being given existence by the Eternal? How would your actions change if you fully internalized that you are a 'non-existent thing' sustained only by His will?

Critique of Dispositional Possibility: The Negation of the Free Agent
Al-Jurjani criticizes the proof of isti'dadi as founded on the negation of the free agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Jurjani argues that the proof from dispositional possibility is based on a corrupt principle that denies God's free agency, and how the philosophers' affirmation of choice fails to refute this.

Core Idea
The proof of dispositional possibility rests on denying that God is a truly free agent.

Al-Jurjani begins his commentary by exposing the foundational flaw in the proof of dispositional possibility. He asserts that it is built on the corrupt principle of denying the free agent and affirming necessitation. In this lesson, we will unpack what is meant by 'free agent' and why the philosophers' own claim that God acts by choice does not salvage their argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the corrupt principle
Read the first line where al-Jurjani states that the proof is based on a corrupt principle: the negation of the free agent and the assertion of necessitation. Notice how he directly links the proof to the philosophers' foundational error.
2
Understand the definition of 'free agent'
Al-Jurjani explains that the free agent is one who can act or refrain and specifies each by His will. Consider why this definition is crucial—it means true freedom requires the ability to choose otherwise.
The philosophers' God is not free in this sense because His will is necessary.
3
See why the philosophers' affirmation of choice is insufficient
The commentator notes that even though philosophers say God acts by choice, their system makes the will (eternal care) necessary to His essence, so the antecedent of the conditional 'if He wills, He does' is necessarily realized. That turns choice into compulsion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا ( الاستدلال الذي هو بالإمكان الاستعدادي مبني على أصلهم الفاسد، وهو نفي القادر المختار)، والقول بالإيجاب؛
قوله : (وهو نفي القادر المختار بمعنى من يصح عنه الفعل والترك، يخصص كل منهما بإرادته فلا يرد أن الحكماء قائلون باختياره تعالى بمعنى : إِن شاء ،فعل، وإن لم يشأ لم يفعل، إلا أن مقدم الشرطية الأولى لازم الوقوع ؛ لكون المشيئة أعني العناية الأزلية لازمة لذاته . قوله : (بمجرد إرادته) يعني أن المخصص لوقوع بعض الأشياء في وقت دون وقت هو الإرادة، سواء قلنا بقدم تعلقها، أو بحدوثها كما مر سابقا تحقيقه .
يمتنع
قوله : ( ومنهم من اختار إلخ ) وهو المحقق الطوسي .
قوله : ( أن الإمكان الذي استدل به وهو إمكان وجود الحادث بعد عدمه. قوله : (أمر عقلي) لأنه هو الإمكان الذاتي مقيساً إلى الوجود المسبوق بالعدم.
قوله : ( لكنه يتعلق بشيء خارجي أي بشيء موجود في الخارج، لأن إمكان وجود الشيء بعد العدم يقتضي إمكان تبدله من حال إلى حال؛ بناء على زيادة الوجود على الماهية والمعدوم اتصافه بتبدل الأحوال، فالحادث لا يتصف به باعتبار ذاته بل موضوعه التغير من حال إلى حال . وإنما يجري عليه باعتبار وجوده فيه فيقال : البياض يمكن أن يوجد في الجسم، وهذا لا ينافي اتصافه بالإمكان الذاتي المطلق في نفسه؛ لأنه يتصف به الماهية في الذهن إذا لوحظ بالقياس إلى الوجود والعدم، بخلاف القديم، فإنه لكونه موجوداً دائماً يتصف بإمكان وجوده بالنظر إلى ذاته دائماً. وهذا معنى قوله : ولتعلقه بذلك الشيء يدل على وجود إلخ. فالإمكان کالعمى والتقدم في أنه ليس شيء منهما موجوداً في الخارج، لكنه يستدعي محلاً موجوداً في الخارج. وبهذا البيان تم المقصود إلا أن في كلامه ترك ما يعني وهو إثبات أنه متعلق بأمر خارجي. وأما قوله فمن حيث تعلقه بالشيء الخارجي إلخ فهو عديل لقوله في آخر الجواب :
في
•
تحقق له في الأعيان كيف وإنها نسبة بين الحادث والفيضان عن العلة ولا يتصور تحقق النسبة الأعيان بدون تحقق المنتسبين فيها . وبالجملة، إذا تحقق شرط من شروط الوجود ترجح على العدم بالنظر إليه، وإذا تحقق شرط آخر يكون أرجح بالنسبة إلى الأول ، وهكذا. فإن أريد بالقرب وبالبعد هذا المعنى فهو لا يستدعي محلا موجودا في الخارج، بل يتصف به ذلك الممكن حال عدمه في الخارج إذا وجد في الذهن وأما إذا لم يوجد فيه أيضاً فحينئذ لا موصوف، ولا اتصاف . وإن أريد أمر آخر فلا دليل على ثبوته .
And this inference based on dispositional possibility is founded on their corrupt principle, namely the negation of the free agent, and the assertion of necessitation. His saying: 'And it is the negation of the free agent' meaning one from whom action and refraining are possible, who specifies each by His will; so it is not objected that the philosophers affirm His choice in the sense that if He wills He does, and if He does not will He does not, except that the antecedent of the first conditional is necessarily realized because the will, i.e., eternal care, is necessary to His essence. His saying: 'by His mere will' means that what specifies the occurrence of some things at a time rather than another is the will, whether we say its relation is eternal or originated, as previously investigated. It is impossible. His saying: 'And some chose, etc.' — that is, al-Muhaqqiq al-Tusi. His saying: 'That the possibility by which he argued is the possibility of the existence of the originated after its non-existence.' His saying: 'It is a rational thing' because it is essential possibility compared to existence preceded by non-existence. His saying: 'But it relates to an external thing' i.e., to something existent in the external world, because the possibility of a thing's existence after non-existence implies the possibility of its change from state to state, based on the superaddition of existence to essence and the fact that non-existence is described by change of states; so the originated is not described by it in terms of its own essence but its subject is change from state to state. It is only attributed to it in view of its existence in it; it is said: whiteness can exist in the body, and this does not contradict its being described by absolute essential possibility in itself, because the essence is described by it in the mind when considered in relation to existence and non-existence, unlike the eternal, for since it exists perpetually it is described by the possibility of its existence in view of its essence perpetually. This is the meaning of his saying: 'And because it relates to that thing indicates existence, etc.' So possibility is like blindness and priority in that none of them exists externally, but they require an existent locus. With this explanation the purpose is fulfilled, except that in his words there is a missing element, namely the proof that it relates to an external thing. As for his saying 'from the standpoint of its relation to the external thing, etc.', it is an equivalent of his saying at the end of the answer: It is realized in concrete entities — rather, it is a relation between the originated and the emanation from the cause, and a relation cannot be realized in concrete entities without the relata being realized in them. In short, if a condition of existence is fulfilled, it becomes preponderant over non-existence with respect to it, and if another condition is fulfilled it becomes more preponderant than the first, and so on. If by nearness and remoteness this meaning is intended, then it does not require a locus existent in the external world; rather, that possible thing is described by it in the state of its non-existence in the external world if it exists in the mind; but if it does not exist in the mind either, then there is no subject and no description. And if something else is intended, there is no proof for its existence.
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage is a commentary by al-Jurjani on the matn. It begins by criticizing the proof of dispositional possibility, saying it is based on the philosophers' corrupt principle that denies the free agent (al-qadir al-mukhtar) and asserts necessitation (al-ijab). The commentator explains that when the matn says 'negation of the free agent,' it means one who can act or refrain and specifies each by His will; the philosophers' claim that God acts by choice does not refute this because for them the will, being eternal care, necessarily accompanies the essence, so the conditional 'if He wills, He does' necessarily occurs. Then the commentator discusses the view of some scholars, especially al-Tusi, who hold that the possibility used in the proof (imkan wujud al-hadith ba'd 'adamih) is a rational (mental) thing, not external. The explanation is that this possibility is essential possibility considered in relation to existence preceded by non-existence. It requires an external subject because the concept of changing from one state to another requires something that undergoes change. The commentator elaborates that the originated does not have change in its own essence; rather, change applies to its existence in the subject like whiteness in a body. The possibility is a relation between the originated and emanation from the cause, and this relation does not require an external locus; it can be predicated of the possible when it exists in the mind. If something else is intended, there is no proof. The passage thus presents a full objection-and-answer cycle: the objection that isti'dadi is not existent but mental, and the answer that it is a rational consideration without external existence.

Key Terms
القادر المختار
الإيجاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that philosophers who affirm God's choice are not denying free agency.
Correction: The commentator clarifies that their determinism makes the will necessary, so the affirmation of choice is verbal but not real.

Checkpoint
According to al-Jurjani, what is the corrupt principle upon which the proof of dispositional possibility is based?
A) The eternity of the world
B) The negation of the free agent and assertion of necessitation
C) The belief in essential possibility
D) The denial of causation

Key Takeaway
The proof from dispositional possibility is invalid because it assumes a deterministic framework that denies God's true free agency, and the philosophers' verbal affirmation of choice does not remedy this.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever behaved as if God's hands are tied by natural laws or your own circumstances? When you pray, do you truly expect God to act freely, or do you think He must follow certain 'rules'? Repent of any hidden doubt and ask Him to grant you certainty in His absolute power.
?
The philosophers claimed to affirm God's choice but reduced it to necessity. In your daily life, do you profess reliance on God while actually depending on secondary causes (money, health, skills)? What is one area where you can concretely trust God's free agency without backup plans?
?
Al-Jurjani exposed that a seemingly neutral philosophical proof actually denies God's free will. Are there ideas or beliefs in your mind that you consider 'neutral' but that subtly undermine God's attributes? Would you be willing to discard them if they contradict the Quran and Sunnah?

Imkan as a Rational Concept: Al-Tusi's View
Al-Jurjani presents and explains al-Tusi's view that imkan is rational and relates to an external thing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Tusi's view that the possibility used in the argument is a rational (mental) concept, not an external attribute, and how it relates to an external thing.

Core Idea
Possibility is a mental relation, not an external property.

After critiquing the proof of dispositional possibility, al-Jurjani presents an alternative view attributed to al-Muhaqqiq al-Tusi. This view holds that the possibility invoked in the argument is not a real attribute existing externally but a rational consideration. In this lesson, we explore this position and its explanation, including how possibility relates to external things.

Guided Path
1
Identify al-Tusi's view
Read the passage where al-Jurjani says 'some chose' and identifies al-Tusi. Note that the possibility in question is the possibility of the existence of the originated after its non-existence.
2
Understand why it is rational
Al-Jurjani explains that it is called a rational thing because it is essential possibility measured against existence preceded by non-existence. Think about why this measurement is mental.
Essential possibility itself is a mental consideration.
3
Grasp its relation to an external thing
Though it is rational, it relates to an external thing because the concept of change from state to state requires a subject that undergoes change. The commentator uses the example of whiteness being possible in a body.
The possibility is attributed to the existent, not to the non-existent.

Commentary
This passage is a commentary by al-Jurjani on the matn. It begins by criticizing the proof of dispositional possibility, saying it is based on the philosophers' corrupt principle that denies the free agent (al-qadir al-mukhtar) and asserts necessitation (al-ijab). The commentator explains that when the matn says 'negation of the free agent,' it means one who can act or refrain and specifies each by His will; the philosophers' claim that God acts by choice does not refute this because for them the will, being eternal care, necessarily accompanies the essence, so the conditional 'if He wills, He does' necessarily occurs. Then the commentator discusses the view of some scholars, especially al-Tusi, who hold that the possibility used in the proof (imkan wujud al-hadith ba'd 'adamih) is a rational (mental) thing, not external. The explanation is that this possibility is essential possibility considered in relation to existence preceded by non-existence. It requires an external subject because the concept of changing from one state to another requires something that undergoes change. The commentator elaborates that the originated does not have change in its own essence; rather, change applies to its existence in the subject like whiteness in a body. The possibility is a relation between the originated and emanation from the cause, and this relation does not require an external locus; it can be predicated of the possible when it exists in the mind. If something else is intended, there is no proof. The passage thus presents a full objection-and-answer cycle: the objection that isti'dadi is not existent but mental, and the answer that it is a rational consideration without external existence.

The Nature of Possibility as a Relation and Its Locus
Al-Jurjani explains that possibility is a relation and discusses its mental nature and the absence of need for an external locus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that possibility is a relation between the originated and emanation from the cause, and that this relation does not require an external locus, as it can be mentally considered.

Core Idea
Possibility is a mental relation, not a real entity needing external existence.

Al-Jurjani continues his explanation of al-Tusi's view by addressing the nature of possibility as a relation (nisba) between the originated thing and the emanation from the cause. He emphasizes that this relation does not require an independent existence in the external world; rather, it can be understood as a mental consideration. In this lesson, we examine this relational nature and the conclusion that if nearness and remoteness are interpreted as conditions, they do not require an external locus.

Guided Path
1
Understand possibility as a relation
Read where al-Jurjani says it is a relation between the originated and the emanation from the cause. Note that a relation cannot exist without its relata.
2
Consider the role of conditions
He explains that if conditions of existence are fulfilled, it becomes preponderant. If 'nearness' and 'remoteness' refer to such conditions, they do not need an external locus.
3
Conclusion about mental attribution
Al-Jurjani concludes that the possible thing can be described by this relation in the mind even during its non-existence in the external world. If it does not exist in the mind either, then there is no subject or description.
This solidifies the mental nature of possibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often plan and worry about future possibilities—wealth, marriage, success. If these are only mental relations without external reality, how much of your anxiety is rooted in illusions? Can you truly trust that only Allah's decree gives existence?
?
Regret is the ache of a 'what could have been.' But Al-Jurjani says that when the possible is not even in the mind, there is no subject and no description. Are you clinging to regrets that have no real existence? What would it free you to release them?
?
You describe yourself with labels: 'I am not good enough,' 'I am unlucky.' According to this lesson, such descriptions have no external locus. Do you confuse mental descriptions with reality? How can you replace them with descriptions based on Allah's promise?

Alternative Argument: Change in the Agent via Accidental Condition
Al-Jurjani presents an alternative argument about change and the free agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how some theologians argue that change required for origination can occur in the agent through an additional condition, without needing a material substrate.

Core Idea
Change in the agent by an accidental condition suffices for origination.

Al-Jurjani presents an alternative perspective that challenges the necessity of a receptive matter. The passage explores how the agent can become a cause by acquiring an additional condition, such as a specific position, without changing His essence. This argument preserves divine simplicity while explaining the diversity of creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Let the author's argument structure come through.
Notice the two main parts: the universal emanation premise and the alternative view.
2
Identify the universal emanation premise
The passage starts by stating that the source is universal emanation, meaning it gives existence equally to all possibles. Differentiation is only due to preparedness of receptacles.
This sets up the standard proof for a free agent.
3
Understand the alternative argument
Some argue that possibility (al-imkan) is mental, not external. The change needed for origination can be on the agent's side by an additional originated matter (e.g., a specific position), not by change in essence.
This avoids needing a material substrate with dispositional possibility.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this argument preserves the agent's immutability while explaining why some things exist at specific times. It shows the agent becomes a complete cause through an accidental condition.
This is an alternative to the standard proof requiring a receptive matter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بناءً على أن المبدأ عام الفيض بالنسبة إلى جميع الممكنات، فلا يختص إيجاده ببعض دون بعض إلا لاختلاف استعدادات القوابل وسيبين أن المبدأ مختار يفعل ما يشاء بمجرد إرادته. ومنهم من اختار أن الإمكان الذي استدل به لا وجود له في الخارج، وقال : الإمكان أمر عقلي لكنه يتعلق بشيء خارجي، فمن حيث تعلقه بالشيء الخارجي عليه ما سبق أنه يجوز أن يكون التغير في جانب الفاعل لا بأن يتبدل في ذاته أو صفاته مع الحقيقية، بل بأن يصير فاعلاً بانضمام أمر حادث إليه، كوضع معين مثلاً يكون معه علة تامة للحادث من غير أن يكون له مادة مستعدة .
Based on the principle that the source is universal emanation with respect to all possibles, so His bringing into existence is not specific to some to the exclusion of others except due to the difference in the preparednesses of receptacles; and it will be shown that the source is a free agent who does what He wills by His mere will. And some chose that the possibility by which one argued has no existence in the external world, and said: possibility is a rational thing, but it relates to an external thing; so from the standpoint of its relation to the external thing, what preceded applies: that the change may be on the side of the agent, not by change in His essence or essential attributes, but by His becoming an agent through the addition of an originated matter, such as a specific position, with which He becomes a complete cause for the originated event without having a receptive matter.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage presents an alternative perspective within the commentary. It begins by stating that the argument for dispositional possibility is based on the premise that the source (al-mabda') is universal emanation, meaning it gives existence equally to all possibles, and the only reason some things exist and others do not is the difference in the preparedness of the receptacles. The commentator then notes that it will be shown later that the source is a free agent who acts by mere will. Then, the text refers to the view of some (apparently al-Tusi and others) that the possibility used in the proof (imkan al-hadith) has no external existence; it is a rational (mental) thing but relates to an external thing. From that standpoint, the previous argument applies: the change required for origination may occur on the side of the agent, not by a change in His essence or essential attributes, but by His becoming an agent through the addition of an originated matter, such as a specific position (wad' mu'ayyan), with which He becomes a complete cause for the originated event without needing a receptive matter (madda musta'idda). Thus, the need for a material substrate with dispositional possibility is not necessary; the agent's own additional condition suffices.

عام الفيض
القوابل
القادر المختار
التغير في جانب الفاعل
العلة التامة
المادة المستعدة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that all change requires a material substratum.
Correction: This passage argues that change can occur in the agent through an accidental condition (like a specific position), without needing a receptive matter.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the alternative to requiring a receptive matter for the originated event?
A) The agent changes in essence.
B) The agent becomes a cause through an additional condition like a specific position.
C) The receptacles have different preparednesses.
D) The source is not universal emanation.

Key Takeaway
The passage presents an alternative argument that the change required for origination can occur in the agent through an additional condition, without needing a receptive matter.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you witness an event in your life, do you instinctively look for natural causes or do you see God's direct hand? This lesson argues that God can bring about change without any pre-existing matter, by His mere will and an additional condition of His choosing. Examine your heart: is your trust in God's power limited by the natural laws you observe? Commit to seeing every change as a direct act of God, whether or not you perceive a material cause.
?
The alternative view in this passage removes the necessity of a receptive matter for God's action. How does this impact your understanding of God's absolute freedom? Reflect on a situation where you felt trapped by circumstances—did you consider that God is not bound by any 'receptive matter'? He can create a way out where none exists. Will you turn to Him with the confidence that He is the free agent who acts by mere will?

The Proof from Successive Dispositions
First proof for duration via successive dispositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument for temporal duration based on successive dispositions on matter and why the author does not respond to it directly.

Core Idea
Successive dispositions require temporal priority, proving duration for the event.

This lesson introduces the first of two proofs for the existence of a duration (mudda) necessary for temporal origination. The argument relies on the observation that successive dispositions on matter are ordered, each prior to the next in a way that precludes coexistence, establishing a temporal priority that points to a time before the event.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the first proof
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Identify the two key claims: (1) successive dispositions are temporally prior, and (2) this priority implies a time before the event.
The proof is presented as the first of two aspects (fl-awwal).
2
Understand why the author does not answer this proof
The passage states that the author did not respond to this aspect because it relies on an infinite regress of dispositions, which is already known to be invalid.
This reveals the interconnected nature of theological arguments.
3
Reflect on the logical structure
Consider how this proof establishes temporal priority through a causal chain of dispositions. Ask: Does the priority necessarily imply a separate time, or could it be intrinsic to the event?
This connects to later discussions in the source.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المدة فلوجهين: الأول أن هذه الاستعدادات المتعاقبة على المادة بعضها مقدم على بعض تقدماً لا يجامع المتقدم فيه المتأخر، وهو التقدم الزماني فيكون المتقدم في زمان سابق على وجود الحادث، وهو المطلوب. وإنما لم يجب عن هذا الوجه لابتنائه على الاستعدادات المتعاقبة إلى غير النهاية، وقد عرفت بطلانها. وقد يجاب أيضاً بأن هذا التقدم ثابت بين أجزاء الزمان، وليس للزمان زمان . وربما تفصوا عن هذا الجواب بأن القبلية والبعدية اللتين لا يجامع فيهما القبل البعد عارضتان للزمان بالذات ولغيره بواسطته ، ألا ترى أنه إذا قيل: ومن حيث كونه قائماً بالعقل موجود في الخارج، وله إمكان آخر يعتبره العقل، وينقطع التسلسل بانقطاع اعتبار العقل . والمقصود دفع ما أورده الإمام من أن الإمكان لو كان موجوداً لكان واجباً أو ممكناً، والأول محال لكونه وصفاً لغيره، والثاني محال لأنه يلزم أن يكون للإمكان إمكان . قوله : ( وفيه بحث لأن تعلقه إلخ قد ظهر لك اندفاعه بما حررناه لك؛ لأن التعلق الذهني إنما هو للإمكان الذاتي المطلق، أعني سلب ضرورة الطرفين دون إمكان الحدوث أعني إمكان وجوده بعد العدم. قوله : (وأما المدة إلخ) لما كان المعتبر في الحدوث الزماني سبقه العدم على الوجود، أن يكون بالزمان؛ لجواز أن يكون بذاته كما ذهب إليه المتكلمون، كان المطلب نظرياً، فما قيل إنه بعد ملاحظة مفهوم الحدوث الزماني اقتضاؤه سبق المدة لا يحتاج إلى دليل وهم . وهي لا تستدعي الحادث. قوله : ( وقد يجاب إلخ) أي لا نسلم قولكم فيكون المتقدم في زمان سابق على وجود قوله : ( بأن القبلية والبعدية إلخ) فالتفريع المذكور ليس باعتبار أن التقدم الزماني مطلقاً يقتضي ذلك، بل لكونه في ما عدا الزمان . قوله : ( ولغيره بواسطته أي عارضتان لغير الزمان بواسطته فهو واسطة في العروض. قوله : أن هذه الاستعدادات إلخ فيه بحث لأن هذا الدليل لو تم لم يدل على وجود الزمان الذي هو المراد من مقالة الحكماء، كما سيشير إليه في آخر المقصد؛ إذ النزاع في سبق كل شيء بأمر موجود ، و وأما السبق بزمان موهوم فالمتكلمون قائلون به. قوله : ألا ترى أنه إذا قيل ولادة زيد فيه بحث لأن ما ذكر لو سلم لدل على أن القبلية والبعدية عرضان أوليان للزمان بمعنى عدم الواسطة في الإثبات والمطلوب عدم الواسطة في
As for the duration, it is [established] by two aspects: The first is that these successive dispositions upon matter, some of them are prior to others by a priority in which the prior does not co-exist with the posterior, and that is temporal priority, so the prior is in a time before the existence of the event, which is what is sought. And he did not answer this aspect because it is based on successive dispositions ad infinitum, and you have known their invalidity. And it may also be answered that this priority is established between parts of time, and time has no time. And perhaps they evade this answer by [saying] that priority and posteriority, in which the prior does not co-exist with the posterior, are accidental to time essentially and to other things by its mediation. Do you not see that when it is said: And by virtue of its being a mental state existent externally, and it has another imkan considered by the mind, and the regress is cut off by the cessation of the mind's consideration. And the purpose is to repel what the Imam [al-Razi] objected: that if imkan existed, it would be either necessary or possible; the first is impossible because it is an attribute of another, and the second is impossible because it would entail that imkan has an imkan. His statement: (And there is a discussion in it because its relation, etc. has become clear to you, its refutation by what we have written for you; because the mental relation is only for absolute essential imkan, i.e., the negation of the necessity of both sides, not for the imkan of origination, i.e., the imkan of its existence after non-existence.) His statement: (As for the duration, etc.) Since what is considered in temporal origination is the priority of non-existence over existence, that it be by time; because it is permissible that it be by essence as the mutakallimun held, the matter is theoretical. So what is said that after observing the concept of temporal origination, its requiring the priority of duration does not need a proof is a delusion. And it does not necessitate the event. His statement: (And it may be answered, etc.) meaning we do not accept your statement, so the prior is in a time before the existence of... his statement: (that priority and posteriority, etc.) So the aforementioned derivation is not because temporal priority absolutely necessitates that, but because it is in what is other than time. His statement: (and for other things by its mediation) i.e., they are accidental to non-time by its mediation, so it is a mediator in the accidentality. His statement: that these dispositions, etc. there is discussion in it because this proof, if valid, would not indicate the existence of time which is meant by the statement of the philosophers, as he will indicate at the end of the objective; for the dispute is about the priority of everything by an existing thing, and as for priority by an imaginary time, the mutakallimun affirm it. His statement: Do you not see that when it is said the birth of Zayd, there is discussion in it because what was mentioned, if granted, would indicate that priority and posteriority are primary accidents of time in the sense of no mediator in proof, while the sought is no mediator in...
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage is a proof-and-objection discussion concerning the duration (mudda) required for temporal origination. The author presents two aspects for duration: first, that successive dispositions on matter are temporally prior to each other, leading to the event's existence in a later time. This proof is not responded to because it relies on an infinite regress of dispositions, which is invalid. A second answer is that such priority exists between parts of time, but time itself has no time. Some evade this by arguing that priority and posteriority are accidental to time essentially and to other things by time's mediation. The passage then shifts to an objection from Imam al-Razi regarding imkan: if imkan were existent, it would be necessary or possible, both impossible. The commentary explains that this objection is answered by distinguishing absolute essential imkan from the imkan of origination. The discussion continues with comments on the duration proof: the required priority of non-existence over existence must be by time, but the mutakallimun allow priority by essence. The final part critiques the argument that priority and posteriority are primary accidents of time, stating that it does not prove the existence of time as the philosophers mean.

Key Terms
المدة
الاستعدادات المتعاقبة
التقدم الزماني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that temporal priority necessarily proves the existence of an objective time continuum independent of events.
Correction: The passage shows that the priority is between dispositions, and the existence of time as an entity is not directly proven; the author notes the argument's reliance on an infinite regress, which undermines it.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the first proof for temporal duration?
A) The priority of the agent over the act.
B) Successive dispositions on matter being temporally prior to each other.
C) The existence of a prepared material cause.
D) The eternal will of the agent.

Key Takeaway
The first proof for duration uses temporal priority of successive dispositions, but it is not defended because it relies on an invalid infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever built a belief or argument on an infinite chain of justifications, assuming it would eventually ground itself? In what area of your life do you need to stop relying on endless reasons and instead acknowledge a necessary stopping point?
?
Think of a conviction you hold about God or the universe that seems logically sound. What hidden assumption might it be resting on—something you've never examined—that could be as fragile as the infinite regress in this proof? How will you test that assumption today?

The Objection of Infinite Regress and the Alternative Answers
Responses to the infinite regress objection in the duration proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the first proof leads to an infinite regress, and the two alternative answers: that priority exists between parts of time, and the evasion that priority and posteriority are accidents of time mediated to others.

Core Idea
Infinite regress of dispositions is invalid; priority is either between time parts or accidental to time.

Having presented the first proof for duration, the author now addresses a potential objection based on infinite regress. Instead of a direct response, he offers an alternate answer: that temporal priority is only between parts of time, and then an evasion that such priority is an accident of time itself, not a separate time. This lesson unpacks these nuanced responses.

Guided Path
1
Read the alternative answers
Read the Arabic and translation for the second answer and the evasion. Notice the shift from rejecting infinite regress to reinterpreting the location of priority.
These answers are presented after noting the first proof is not answered.
2
Distinguish between the two responses
The first answer says priority is between parts of time, so no need for a time of time. The evasion says priority and posteriority are accidents that belong to time essentially and to other things only through time's mediation.
The evasion attempts to avoid the implication that time itself has a time.
3
Evaluate the strength of these answers
Consider whether these answers successfully establish the existence of a duration before the event, or merely redefine the problem. The commentary suggests they are attempts to counter the infinite regress objection.
This connects to broader debates in kalam about the nature of time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Imam al-Razi's Objection on the Existence of Imkan and the Response
Razi's objection on imkan and the distinguishing response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam al-Razi's objection that if imkan (possibility) were existent, it would lead to an infinite regress, and the response that distinguishes absolute essential imkan from imkan of origination.

Core Idea
Imkan is not an existing attribute; only absolute essential imkan is a mental relation, avoiding regress.

Imam al-Razi raises a powerful objection against the notion of imkan as a real attribute: if imkan existed, it would be either necessary or possible, both impossible. The response in this passage clarifies that the imkan at stake is only a mental consideration—absolute essential imkan (negation of necessity of both sides)—not the imkan of origination, which is the possibility of existence after non-existence. This distinction cuts off the regress.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic carefully. Identify the two horns of the dilemma: imkan as existent would be necessary or possible, both absurd.
The objection is attributed to Imam al-Razi (Imam).
2
Understand the two types of imkan
The response distinguishes absolute essential imkan (mental negation of necessity) from imkan of origination (possibility of existence after non-existence). The objection only applies to the latter, but the response redefines the relevant imkan as the former.
Absolute essential imkan is not a real existent; it is a mental relation.
3
See how the regress is cut off
Since absolute essential imkan is merely a mental consideration, it does not require another imkan, stopping the regress. The 'imkan of imkan' problem disappears.
This is a classic solution to the imkan regress in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المدة فلوجهين: الأول أن هذه الاستعدادات المتعاقبة على المادة بعضها مقدم على بعض تقدماً لا يجامع المتقدم فيه المتأخر، وهو التقدم الزماني فيكون المتقدم في زمان سابق على وجود الحادث، وهو المطلوب. وإنما لم يجب عن هذا الوجه لابتنائه على الاستعدادات المتعاقبة إلى غير النهاية، وقد عرفت بطلانها. وقد يجاب أيضاً بأن هذا التقدم ثابت بين أجزاء الزمان، وليس للزمان زمان . وربما تفصوا عن هذا الجواب بأن القبلية والبعدية اللتين لا يجامع فيهما القبل البعد عارضتان للزمان بالذات ولغيره بواسطته ، ألا ترى أنه إذا قيل: ومن حيث كونه قائماً بالعقل موجود في الخارج، وله إمكان آخر يعتبره العقل، وينقطع التسلسل بانقطاع اعتبار العقل . والمقصود دفع ما أورده الإمام من أن الإمكان لو كان موجوداً لكان واجباً أو ممكناً، والأول محال لكونه وصفاً لغيره، والثاني محال لأنه يلزم أن يكون للإمكان إمكان . قوله : ( وفيه بحث لأن تعلقه إلخ قد ظهر لك اندفاعه بما حررناه لك؛ لأن التعلق الذهني إنما هو للإمكان الذاتي المطلق، أعني سلب ضرورة الطرفين دون إمكان الحدوث أعني إمكان وجوده بعد العدم. قوله : (وأما المدة إلخ) لما كان المعتبر في الحدوث الزماني سبقه العدم على الوجود، أن يكون بالزمان؛ لجواز أن يكون بذاته كما ذهب إليه المتكلمون، كان المطلب نظرياً، فما قيل إنه بعد ملاحظة مفهوم الحدوث الزماني اقتضاؤه سبق المدة لا يحتاج إلى دليل وهم . وهي لا تستدعي الحادث. قوله : ( وقد يجاب إلخ) أي لا نسلم قولكم فيكون المتقدم في زمان سابق على وجود قوله : ( بأن القبلية والبعدية إلخ) فالتفريع المذكور ليس باعتبار أن التقدم الزماني مطلقاً يقتضي ذلك، بل لكونه في ما عدا الزمان . قوله : ( ولغيره بواسطته أي عارضتان لغير الزمان بواسطته فهو واسطة في العروض. قوله : أن هذه الاستعدادات إلخ فيه بحث لأن هذا الدليل لو تم لم يدل على وجود الزمان الذي هو المراد من مقالة الحكماء، كما سيشير إليه في آخر المقصد؛ إذ النزاع في سبق كل شيء بأمر موجود ، و وأما السبق بزمان موهوم فالمتكلمون قائلون به. قوله : ألا ترى أنه إذا قيل ولادة زيد فيه بحث لأن ما ذكر لو سلم لدل على أن القبلية والبعدية عرضان أوليان للزمان بمعنى عدم الواسطة في الإثبات والمطلوب عدم الواسطة في
As for the duration, it is [established] by two aspects: The first is that these successive dispositions upon matter, some of them are prior to others by a priority in which the prior does not co-exist with the posterior, and that is temporal priority, so the prior is in a time before the existence of the event, which is what is sought. And he did not answer this aspect because it is based on successive dispositions ad infinitum, and you have known their invalidity. And it may also be answered that this priority is established between parts of time, and time has no time. And perhaps they evade this answer by [saying] that priority and posteriority, in which the prior does not co-exist with the posterior, are accidental to time essentially and to other things by its mediation. Do you not see that when it is said: And by virtue of its being a mental state existent externally, and it has another imkan considered by the mind, and the regress is cut off by the cessation of the mind's consideration. And the purpose is to repel what the Imam [al-Razi] objected: that if imkan existed, it would be either necessary or possible; the first is impossible because it is an attribute of another, and the second is impossible because it would entail that imkan has an imkan. His statement: (And there is a discussion in it because its relation, etc. has become clear to you, its refutation by what we have written for you; because the mental relation is only for absolute essential imkan, i.e., the negation of the necessity of both sides, not for the imkan of origination, i.e., the imkan of its existence after non-existence.) His statement: (As for the duration, etc.) Since what is considered in temporal origination is the priority of non-existence over existence, that it be by time; because it is permissible that it be by essence as the mutakallimun held, the matter is theoretical. So what is said that after observing the concept of temporal origination, its requiring the priority of duration does not need a proof is a delusion. And it does not necessitate the event. His statement: (And it may be answered, etc.) meaning we do not accept your statement, so the prior is in a time before the existence of... his statement: (that priority and posteriority, etc.) So the aforementioned derivation is not because temporal priority absolutely necessitates that, but because it is in what is other than time. His statement: (and for other things by its mediation) i.e., they are accidental to non-time by its mediation, so it is a mediator in the accidentality. His statement: that these dispositions, etc. there is discussion in it because this proof, if valid, would not indicate the existence of time which is meant by the statement of the philosophers, as he will indicate at the end of the objective; for the dispute is about the priority of everything by an existing thing, and as for priority by an imaginary time, the mutakallimun affirm it. His statement: Do you not see that when it is said the birth of Zayd, there is discussion in it because what was mentioned, if granted, would indicate that priority and posteriority are primary accidents of time in the sense of no mediator in proof, while the sought is no mediator in...
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage is a proof-and-objection discussion concerning the duration (mudda) required for temporal origination. The author presents two aspects for duration: first, that successive dispositions on matter are temporally prior to each other, leading to the event's existence in a later time. This proof is not responded to because it relies on an infinite regress of dispositions, which is invalid. A second answer is that such priority exists between parts of time, but time itself has no time. Some evade this by arguing that priority and posteriority are accidental to time essentially and to other things by time's mediation. The passage then shifts to an objection from Imam al-Razi regarding imkan: if imkan were existent, it would be necessary or possible, both impossible. The commentary explains that this objection is answered by distinguishing absolute essential imkan from the imkan of origination. The discussion continues with comments on the duration proof: the required priority of non-existence over existence must be by time, but the mutakallimun allow priority by essence. The final part critiques the argument that priority and posteriority are primary accidents of time, stating that it does not prove the existence of time as the philosophers mean.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think imkan is a real attribute that exists in the world, leading to the regress problem.
Correction: The response clarifies that the imkan relevant to theological discussion is a mental concept (salb darurat al-tarafayn), not a real existent, thus avoiding the dilemma.

Checkpoint
According to the response, which type of imkan avoids the regress?
A) Imkan of origination
B) Absolute essential imkan (mental negation)
C) Both types
D) Neither type

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Razi's regress is avoided by distinguishing absolute essential imkan as a mental relation from real imkan of origination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there concepts you hold in your mind about God—like His attributes or actions—that you unconsciously treat as if they are objects or realities separate from Him? How does the distinction between mental imkan and real imkan expose a subtle form of misattribution? Pause and write down one such concept; then resolve to think of it only as a mental relation, not a separate entity.
?
You have just learned that absolute imkan is not an existing thing but a mental negation. How does this affect your understanding of God's power? If possibility is not a real waiting capacity in creatures, does it make God's creative act more immediate and awesome? Let this truth unsettle you: you never add anything to God's power; He simply wills and it is. Reflect on that for a minute and commit to remembering it in your next supplication.
?
Razi's objection shows how easy it is to fall into infinite regress by treating mental concepts as real. Have you ever built a chain of reasoning in your faith that seemed to go on forever—like questions about predestination that never end? What would it mean to you to stop the regress by accepting that some realities are simply mental constructs? Can you identify one such question in your own mind right now, and lay it to rest?

The First Face: Successive Dispositions and Temporal Priority
Commentary on the first face of the duration argument

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument for a necessary preceding duration and why it is considered reliant on an invalid infinite regress.

Core Idea
Temporal priority is not a second time; it inheres in the order of dispositions.

This lesson examines the first of two arguments for the existence of a duration (muddah) before an originated thing. The argument uses the concept of successive dispositions in matter, each prior to the next, implying a temporal order. However, this reasoning falters because it demands an infinite chain of dispositions, which has already been disproven.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice how the argument is structured as a conditional: if successive dispositions are temporally prior, then a prior time exists.
The passage ends mid-sentence; the example continues in the next lesson.
2
Identify the core claim
The first face claims that successive dispositions in matter are temporally prior to one another, so the originated thing is preceded by a time. This is the sought conclusion.
3
Understand why no direct response was given
The author states that this argument was not answered directly because it is based on an infinite regress of dispositions, which is known to be false.
This is a key point: the argument's weakness is its reliance on an infinite chain.
4
Examine the alternative answers
Two responses are mentioned: (1) temporal priority is between parts of time, but time itself is not in time; (2) some evade by saying priority/posteriority belong to time primarily and to others through it.
The passage begins an example to illustrate this evasion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المدة فلوجهين: الأول أن هذه الاستعدادات المتعاقبة على المادة بعضها مقدم على بعض تقدما لا يجامع المتقدم فيه المتأخر، وهو التقدم الزماني فيكون المتقدم في زمان سابق على وجود الحادث، وهو المطلوب. وإنما لم يجب عن هذا الوجه لابتنائه على الاستعدادات المتعاقبة إلى غير النهاية، وقد عرفت بطلانها. وقد يجاب أيضا بأن هذا التقدم ثابت بين أجزاء الزمان، وليس للزمان زمان. وربما تفصوا عن هذا الجواب بأن القبلية والبعدية اللتين لا يجامع فيهما القبل البعد عارضتان للزمان بالذات ولغيره بواسطته، ألا ترى أنه إذا قيل:
As for the duration (al-muddah), there are two faces. The first: these successive dispositions (al-isti'dadat al-muta'aqiba) in matter, some of them are prior to others with a priority in which the prior does not coexist with the posterior, which is temporal priority (al-taqaddum al-zamani). Thus the prior is in a time before the existence of the originated thing (al-hadith), which is what is sought. However, he did not respond to this face because it is based on successive dispositions ad infinitum, and you already know their falsehood. And it may also be answered that this priority is established between the parts of time, and time has no time. And perhaps they evade this answer by saying that priority and posteriority (al-qabliyya wa al-ba'diyya), in which the prior does not coexist with the posterior, are accidental to time primarily (bi al-dhat) and to others through its mediation (bi wasitatihi). Do you not see that when it is said:
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage presents the first of two faces (arguments) concerning the necessity of a duration preceding the originated thing. The argument claims that successive dispositions in matter are temporally prior to one another, and thus the originated thing is preceded by a time. The author notes that this argument is not refuted directly because it rests on an infinite regress of dispositions, which has already been shown invalid. An alternative answer is given: temporal priority holds between parts of time, but time itself is not in time. Some evade this by claiming that the attributes of priority and posteriority belong primarily to time and to others only through time. The passage ends with the beginning of an example, which continues into the next lesson.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Temporal priority always requires a distinct second time.
Correction: Here, the argument is about successive dispositions in matter itself, not about two separate times.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say that the first face (argument) was not directly answered?
A) Because it is based on an infinite regress of dispositions which is false
B) Because temporal priority is self-evident and needs no proof
C) Because the objectors conceded the point
D) Because the argument is too weak to require a response

Key Takeaway
The first face of the duration argument fails because it presupposes an infinite succession of dispositions, which is invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your own existence, do you ever feel like there must be an infinite chain of causes behind you? How does the invalidity of infinite regress in this lesson challenge that feeling, and what does it mean for your relationship with the Creator who is the First Cause?
?
The argument centers on 'successive dispositions'—preparations that lead to action. In your spiritual life, are there endless preparations (intentions, plans) that never lead to actual worship or change? If so, what is one disposition you have been stuck in, and what step will you take today to move from disposition to action?
?
The passage discusses time as an attribute that may be accidental. Do you think of time as a container for your life or as a sequence of events that you shape? How can viewing time as arising from the order of Allah's creation change the way you value each moment?

Priority of Non-Existence and the Thatness/Whyness Distinction
Argument that priority is known by thatness, stopping the question at parts of time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the priority of non-existence over existence for originated things is known in its thatness, not its whyness, and how this distinction stops the question of temporal precedence at the parts of time.

Core Idea
The priority of non-existence is self-evident in its thatness; asking why is unnecessary.

This passage continues the discussion of time and originated things. The author argues that the non-existence of a thing precedes its existence, but clarifies that this priority is not the non-existence itself. He then introduces a crucial distinction between knowing that something is prior (thatness) and knowing why it is prior (whyness). This distinction is essential to avoid an infinite regress of questioning about temporal priority.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the claim that non-existence is prior to existence and the clarification that priority is not the non-existence itself.
The opening phrase 'التقدم ليس نفس العدم' is key.
2
Understand the thatness vs whyness distinction
The author says the question about priority is about thatness (anniyya), not whyness (limmiyya). Reflect on the difference: 'that' something is prior vs 'why' it is prior.
This distinction stops the need for further explanation once we reach self-evident temporal relations.
3
Apply to the example of years
The priority of the past year over this year is known by the meaning of the words. The question 'why is the past year prior?' is not asked because the thatness is obvious.
This illustrates how the question ceases at the parts of time.
4
Recognize the limitation
The author notes that this does not prove the opponent's desired conclusion. Consider why the argument is limited.
The passage ends with 'ولا يخفى أنه لا يدل على مطلوبهم'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني: إن عدم الحادث متقدم على وجوده، والتقدم ليس نفس العدم ضرورة؛ إذ لا معنى لتقدم الشيء على نفسه. وبالجملة المطلوب بالسؤال هناك هو العلم بآنية التقدم لا لميته، وإلا فلا نسلم انقطاع السؤال عند الوصول إلى أجزاء الزمان، بل يصح أن يقال: لم تقدم هذا الجزء الذي يسمى بالعام الماضي على الذي يسمى بهذه السنة؛ إذ ليس عند العقل بالنظر إلى ذاته ما يمنع هذا السؤال. ثم إن تقدم العام الماضي على هذا العام معلوم الآن لكل أحد؛ لدلالة اللفظ على ذلك، دون سائر الحوادث. وهذا هو الفارق في انقطاع السؤال عند الوصول إلى أجزاء الزمان لا قبله، إذ كان المطلوب معرفة آنية التقدم لا لميته. ولا يخفى أنه لا يدل على مطلوبهم. وأما ما يقال من أن السبق الزماني لو كان عبارة عما ذكر من غير اعتبار أمر آخر معه أوجب أن يكون سبق العلة المعدة على معلولها سبقا زمانيا؛ لأن لها أيضا قبلية لا يجامع معها القبل البعد، وقد صرحوا بأنه سبق ذاتي، فمما لا يلتفت إليه؛ إذ لا محذور في اجتماع جهتي التقدم في العلة المعدة أو غيرها، ألا يرى أن العقل الأول متقدم على الثاني بالعلية وبالرتبة أيضا لقربه من المبدأ الأول.
The second face: The non-existence of the originated thing (al-hadith) is prior to its existence, and the priority is not the non-existence itself necessarily; for there is no meaning to something being prior to itself. In short, what is sought by the question there is knowledge of the thatness (anniyya) of the priority, not its whyness (limmiyya). Otherwise, we do not concede that the question ceases when reaching the parts of time; rather, it may validly be said: Why is this part called the past year prior to that which is called this year? For the intellect, considering its essence, does not prevent this question. Then, the priority of the past year over this year is known in its thatness to everyone, due to the indication of the word, unlike other events. This is the difference: the question ceases when reaching the parts of time but not before, because what is sought is knowledge of the thatness of priority, not its whyness. And it is clear that this does not prove what they want. As for what is said: if temporal priority (al-sabq al-zamani) were only what is mentioned without considering another matter along with it, it would necessitate that the priority of the preparing cause (al-illa al-mu'idda) over its effect would be temporal priority, because it also has a priority in which the prior does not coexist with the posterior, yet they have declared it to be essential priority (sabq dhati) – this is not to be heeded, for there is no harm in the combination of two aspects of priority in the preparing cause or other things. Do you not see that the First Intellect (al-ʿaql al-awwal) is prior to the Second Intellect (al-ʿaql al-thani) by causality and also by rank due to its closeness to the First Principle (al-mabda' al-awwal).
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage presents the second face (argument) for the necessity of time before an originated thing: the non-existence of the thing is prior to its existence, but this priority is not the non-existence itself (otherwise would be circular). The author then discusses the nature of the question about priority. He distinguishes between knowing the thatness (anniyya: that it is prior) and the whyness (limmiyya: why it is prior). Once we reach the parts of time (e.g., years), the question of whyness naturally stops because the priority is self-evident in the meaning of the terms. The author rejects an objection that this would make the priority of a preparing cause temporal; he notes that such a cause can have both causal priority and temporal priority without contradiction, as in the example of the First Intellect being prior to the Second Intellect both causally and in rank.

Key Terms

حادث
تقدم
آنية
لمية
سبق زماني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The priority of non-existence is the non-existence itself.
Correction: Priority is a relation distinct from the thing itself; it cannot be identical to non-existence because that would be self-priority.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the question of priority cease when reaching the parts of time (e.g., past year and this year)?
A) Because the parts of time are causally related
B) Because the priority is known in its thatness from the meaning of the words
C) Because the intellect cannot question the priority of time
D) Because the parts of time are not real

Key Takeaway
The priority of non-existence over existence is known in its thatness, not its whyness; this distinction stops unnecessary questioning about temporal precedence at the parts of time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own existence, do you ever demand a 'why' for the most basic facts of your life — like the fact that you are reading these words? How does the thatness/whyness distinction challenge your urge to explain everything?
?
Is there an area of your faith where you repeatedly ask 'why' about something that is already self-evident (like the priority of God's existence over creation)? What would it cost you to stop at the thatness and simply believe?
?
The author says that questioning can stop at the parts of time. Reflect: In your daily life, do you respect the limits of reason, or do you try to exhaustively explain everything, including matters that are known by their thatness alone? What might change if you accepted those limits?

Temporal and Essential Priority Combined in a Cause
Response to objection that preparing cause's priority would be temporal, with example of First Intellect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that a preparing cause can have both temporal and essential priority without contradiction, as illustrated by the First Intellect's priority over the Second Intellect.

Core Idea
A cause can have multiple aspects of priority; temporal and essential priority can coexist.

The previous argument about thatness and whyness might imply that all priority is merely temporal. An objector raises a concern: if temporal priority is only about the meaning of words, then the priority of a preparing cause would also be temporal, contradicting the view that it is essential. The author responds that there is no problem with a thing having both temporal and essential priority from different aspects, and provides the example of the First Intellect being prior to the Second Intellect by causality and by rank.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objector says: if temporal priority is just the relation of before and after as indicated by words, then the priority of a preparing cause (al-illa al-mu'idda) over its effect would also be temporal, because it also has a priority where the prior does not coexist with the posterior. But scholars say it is essential priority (sabq dhati).
Understand the objection: it tries to create a contradiction in the previous definition.
2
Understand the author's dismissal
The author dismisses the objection, stating that there is no harm (la mahdhur) in a thing having two aspects of priority. A cause can have both temporal and essential priority simultaneously.
This is the key response: combination is possible.
3
Examine the example
The First Intellect (al-ʿaql al-awwal) is prior to the Second Intellect (al-ʿaql al-thani) by causality (bil-illiyya) and also by rank (bil-rutba) due to its closeness to the First Principle. This shows two distinct types of priority in one thing.
Causality is often seen as essential priority, while rank is a form of temporal or at least non-essential priority.
4
Reflect on the implication
The example shows that a cause can have both types of priority without contradiction, so the objector's worry is unfounded.
This strengthens the earlier argument about temporal priority being known by thatness.

الوجه الثاني: إن عدم الحادث متقدم على وجوده، والتقدم ليس نفس العدم ضرورة؛ إذ لا معنى لتقدم الشيء على نفسه. وبالجملة المطلوب بالسؤال هناك هو العلم بآنية التقدم لا لميته، وإلا فلا نسلم انقطاع السؤال عند الوصول إلى أجزاء الزمان، بل يصح أن يقال: لم تقدم هذا الجزء الذي يسمى بالعام الماضي على الذي يسمى بهذه السنة؛ إذ ليس عند العقل بالنظر إلى ذاته ما يمنع هذا السؤال. ثم إن تقدم العام الماضي على هذا العام معلوم الآن لكل أحد؛ لدلالة اللفظ على ذلك، دون سائر الحوادث. وهذا هو الفارق في انقطاع السؤال عند الوصول إلى أجزاء الزمان لا قبله، إذ كان المطلوب معرفة آنية التقدم لا لميته. ولا يخفى أنه لا يدل على مطلوبهم. وأما ما يقال من أن السبق الزماني لو كان عبارة عما ذكر من غير اعتبار أمر آخر معه أوجب أن يكون سبق العلة المعدة على معلولها سبقا زمانيا؛ لأن لها أيضا قبلية لا يجامع معها القبل البعد، وقد صرحوا بأنه سبق ذاتي، فمما لا يلتفت إليه؛ إذ لا محذور في اجتماع جهتي التقدم في العلة المعدة أو غيرها، ألا يرى أن العقل الأول متقدم على الثاني بالعلية وبالرتبة أيضا لقربه من المبدأ الأول.
The second face: The non-existence of the originated thing (al-hadith) is prior to its existence, and the priority is not the non-existence itself necessarily; for there is no meaning to something being prior to itself. In short, what is sought by the question there is knowledge of the thatness (anniyya) of the priority, not its whyness (limmiyya). Otherwise, we do not concede that the question ceases when reaching the parts of time; rather, it may validly be said: Why is this part called the past year prior to that which is called this year? For the intellect, considering its essence, does not prevent this question. Then, the priority of the past year over this year is known in its thatness to everyone, due to the indication of the word, unlike other events. This is the difference: the question ceases when reaching the parts of time but not before, because what is sought is knowledge of the thatness of priority, not its whyness. And it is clear that this does not prove what they want. As for what is said: if temporal priority (al-sabq al-zamani) were only what is mentioned without considering another matter along with it, it would necessitate that the priority of the preparing cause (al-illa al-mu'idda) over its effect would be temporal priority, because it also has a priority in which the prior does not coexist with the posterior, yet they have declared it to be essential priority (sabq dhati) – this is not to be heeded, for there is no harm in the combination of two aspects of priority in the preparing cause or other things. Do you not see that the First Intellect (al-ʿaql al-awwal) is prior to the Second Intellect (al-ʿaql al-thani) by causality and also by rank due to its closeness to the First Principle (al-mabda' al-awwal).
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage presents the second face (argument) for the necessity of time before an originated thing: the non-existence of the thing is prior to its existence, but this priority is not the non-existence itself (otherwise would be circular). The author then discusses the nature of the question about priority. He distinguishes between knowing the thatness (anniyya: that it is prior) and the whyness (limmiyya: why it is prior). Once we reach the parts of time (e.g., years), the question of whyness naturally stops because the priority is self-evident in the meaning of the terms. The author rejects an objection that this would make the priority of a preparing cause temporal; he notes that such a cause can have both causal priority and temporal priority without contradiction, as in the example of the First Intellect being prior to the Second Intellect both causally and in rank.

The Substrate of Priority and Non-Priority
Objection and answer about the necessity of time as a real substrate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalām argument that correct judgments about priority and non-priority do not require an externally real substrate like time; a mental construct is sufficient.

Core Idea
Not every correct judgment needs an external reality; mental substrates suffice.

This lesson presents a classic objection: if priority and non-priority are mere privations, how can the mind make true judgments about them? The objector insists on a real substrate, time. The answer concedes the need for a substrate but denies it must be external. Through this exchange, we explore the distinction between external existence and mental consideration.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Start by reading the objection in Arabic and translation. Notice the claim that even if priority and non-priority are privations, the mind's correct judgment about them demands a real substrate.
The term 'خ' (هب) implies a hypothetical concession for argument's sake.
2
Identify the point of concession
The author grants the need for an essential substrate (maʿrūḍ dhātī), but asks if it must be externally real. Find the phrase that shows this concession.
3
Understand the key distinction
The answer distinguishes between external existence (wujūd fī al-khārij) and mental existence (amr ʿaqlī). The substrate can be mental, not necessarily time in the external world.
4
See the commentator's clarification
The commentary explains that the substrate is the non-existence of the event itself, not time. The desired conclusion (precedence by duration) is established regardless of whether time is externally real.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت : هب أن القبلية واللاقبلية عدميتان لكن الحكم باتصاف الأشياء بهما حكم صحيح تشهد به بديهة العقل فلا بد لها من معروض ذاتي هو الزمان . قلت : هذا مسلم لكن لا يلزم منه وجود ذلك المعروض في الخارج، بل جاز أن يكون أمراً عقلياً معروضاً في نفس الأمر لما هو اعتباري . قوله : (هذا) (مسلم أي أنه لا بد له من معروض ذاتي، لكنه لا يلزم منه وجود ذلك المعروض؛ لجواز أن يكون عدم ذلك الحادث، فلا حكمه بأنه هو الزمان . وبما ذكرنا ظهر اندفاع ما قيل بعد تسليم أن معروضه الذاتي هو الزمان ثبت المطلوب وهو مسبوقية الحادث بالمدة ولا يهمنا بيان كونه موجوداً في الخارج، فإنه مطلوب آخر مذكور في مقامه .
For if you say: Assume that priority (al-qabliyah) and non-priority (al-lāqabliyah) are privations, but the judgment that things are described by them is a correct judgment attested to by the primary intuition of the intellect, so they must have an essential substrate (maʿrūḍ dhātī) which is time. I say: This is granted, but from it does not necessitate the existence of that substrate in the external world; rather it is possible that it be a mental matter (amr ʿaqlī) subsisting in the very nature of the case for what is relative (iʿtibārī). His saying (This is granted) means that it necessarily has an essential substrate, but from it does not necessitate the existence of that substrate; because it is possible that it be the non-existence of that event, so do not judge that it is time. And by what we have mentioned, the refutation of what was said is clear: after granting that its essential substrate is time, the desired conclusion is established, which is the precedence of the event by a duration, and we are not concerned with demonstrating its existence in the external world, for that is another desired conclusion mentioned in its proper place.
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer concerning the necessity of time as a real substrate for the relations of priority and non-priority. The objector argues that even if priority and non-priority are privations, the mind still makes correct judgments about them, so they must have a real entity that underlies them, which is time. The responder (the author) concedes that they need a substrate but denies that it must be externally real; it could be a mental construct. The commentator then clarifies the answer: the substrate is the non-existence of the event, not necessarily time, and the fact that the event is preceded by a duration does not require that duration to be an external entity. The passage concludes that the desired conclusion (the occurrence of the event after a period of non-existence) is established regardless of whether the duration is external or mental.

The First School and the Core Answer
First school claim and its refutation via the particular existence distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first school's claim that all essences are made and the key rebuttal that what is made is particular existence, not essence.

Core Idea
The made is particular existence, not the essence itself.

This lesson introduces a major position on the making of essences: that all essences, both simple and composite, are made because they are possible. The response immediately corrects this by distinguishing between essence and existence. Read carefully to grasp how the distinction preserves the need for an agent without making essences themselves the objects of creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the claim
Read the first sentence stating that all essences are made, both simple and composite. Notice the reasoning: simple essences are possible and thus need an agent; composite essences are likewise because their parts are made.
The reasoning is based on possibility (imkan) and need (ihtiyaj).
2
Identify the answer
Read the answer that begins with 'The answer'. It states that the made is particular existence (al-wujud al-khas), i.e., identity (huwiyya), not the essence of existence. Consider what this implies: denying that essences are made does not deny making entirely.
The answer uses the term 'particular existence' to denote concrete instances.
3
Reflect on the logical move
Think about why this answer works: if the made is the particular existence, then essences as universals are not made, but the need for an agent is fulfilled through existence. This preserves causality without making essences dependent.
This distinction is fundamental in later discussions.

المرصد الرابع - المقصد الخامس : في أقسام الواحد
أحد المذاهب هو أن الماهيات كلها مجعولة، أما البسيطة فلأنها ممكنة؛ والممكن محتاج لذاته إلى فاعل وأما المركبة فكذلك أيضاً، أو لأن أجزاءها البسيطة مجعولة. ( والجواب : أن المجعول هو الوجود الخاص ) أي هويته ) لا ماهية الوجود ) ، فلا يلزم من ارتفاع المجعولية عن الماهيات بأسرها ارتفاع المجعولية رأساً، واستغناء الممكن عن الفاعل المؤثر. المذهب ( الثالث ( الماهية المركبة مجعولة، بخلاف ) الماهية ( البسيطة ؛ لأن شرط المجعولية الإمكان، وذلك لأن المجعولية فرع الاحتياج إلى

باعتبار ذاته أو باعتبار أجزائه .
قوله : (والجواب إلخ) حاصله منع الملازمة المدلول عليها بقوله : لأن كل ما فرض مجعولاً فهو ماهية لجواز أن تكون هوية أي ماهية شخصية لا ماهية كلية . وفيه : أن النزاع في أن الماهية بمعنى ما به الشيء هو كليا أو جزئياً جعولة أو لا، لا في الماهية الكلية. وأما على ما ذكرنا من التقرير، فحاصل الجواب منع الشرطية بناء على أن المجعول هوية الوجود، لا ماهية الوجود الصادقة عليه فضلاً عن مطلق الماهية ولا يلزم من صدق شيء على شيء أن يكون مجعولاً، وإلا يلزم أن تكون السلوب والعدمات الصادقة عليه مجعولة .
قوله : (أي هويته ) أي المراد بالوجود الخاص أشخاصه لا مفهومه الكلي .
قوله : ( الماهية المركبة مجعولة لئلا يلزم نفي المجعولية بالكلية، ولظهوره لم يتعرض له .
اعتبر بالقياس إلى الأجزاء المقدارية الغير المتشابهة، فلا يتم التقريب، ويناسب الجواب أيضاً. وفيه نظر إذ المقدر حينئذ أن ليس بعض الماهيات مجعولة، لأن نقيض الإيجاب الكلي الذي ادعى السلب الجزئي . وما ذكره إنما يتم لو كان المقدر السلب الكلي . اللهم إلا أن يبنى الكلام على أن بعض الماهيات إذا لم تكن مجعولة كان الجميع كذلك إذ لا فرق بين ماهية وماهية بعد كونها خاصة ممكنة تأمل .
