The Fourth Observation - The Fifth Objective: On the divisions of the one. One of the schools is that all essences (al-mahiyyat) are made (maj'ula); as for the simple (essence), because it is possible (mumkin); and the possible is in need by itself of an agent; and as for the composite, likewise, or because its simple parts are made. The answer: That which is made is the particular existence (al-wujud al-khas), i.e., its identity (huwiyya), not the essence of existence, so it does not follow from the removal of being-made from all essences the removal of being-made entirely, and the independence of the possible from the effective agent. The third school: The composite essence is made, unlike the simple essence, because the condition of being-made is possibility, and that is because being-made is a branch of need for the effective agent. His statement: 'And the possible is in need by itself of an agent' — therein that the necessary is that the simple is in need by itself of an agent, but what is claimed is that it is in need in its essence of an agent; because the dispute is whether essences in themselves are in need of an agent or not? So it is possible that it is in need by itself, not by another, of an agent in existence, and it is not in need in its essence of anything. Perhaps the author, because the well-known proof was apparently invalid, left it and argued with what is mentioned in the book. His statement: 'Or because its parts, etc.' — we do not mean by a thing being made except the relation of making (al-ja'l) to it, whether in consideration of its essence or in consideration of its parts. His statement: 'The answer, etc.' — its summary is the denial of the implication indicated by his statement: 'because everything supposed to be made is an essence' — it is possible that it is an identity, i.e., a personal essence (mahiyya shakhsiyya), not a universal essence. And therein: that the dispute is about the essence in the sense of 'that by which a thing is what it is', whether universal or particular, being made or not, not about the universal essence. And according to the formulation we have mentioned, the summary of the answer is the denial of the conditional premise based on that which is made is the identity of existence, not the essence of existence true of it, let alone absolute essence, and it does not follow from the truth of a thing about a thing that it is made; otherwise it would follow that privations (sulub) and non-existents (adamāt) true of it would be made. His statement: 'i.e., its identity' — meaning by 'particular existence' its individuals, not its universal concept. His statement: 'The composite essence is made lest it lead to the negation of being-made entirely, and due to its obviousness he did not discuss it.' Consider by comparison to the quantitative parts that are not similar; then the approximation is not complete, and the answer is also appropriate. And therein is consideration, for what is assumed then is that some essences are not made, because the contradictory of the universal affirmative that he claimed is the particular negative. What he mentioned would only be complete if what was assumed was the universal negative. Unless the discussion is built on the basis that if some essences are not made, then all are likewise, since there is no difference between one essence and another after they are particular possible. Contemplate. His statement: 'He is the particular existence, etc.' It was said: It follows that the essence is also made because making the universal existence is necessarily within the particular. And the answer: Being-made is need (al-ihtiyaj), and it does not follow from particular need universal need. And it may be answered that the investigation is about the essence insofar as it is essence, not about the mixed essence, as will be known from the exposition (al-tahrir).
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage defines the third position on the making of essences and provides a detailed response. It first states a school (madhhab) that all essences are made, whether simple or composite, because they are possible and need an agent. The answer immediately refutes this: what is made is the particular existence (al-wujud al-khas), i.e., the concrete identity (huwiyya), not the essence of existence itself. Thus, denying that essences are made does not negate making entirely or imply that the possible is independent of an agent. The third position is then given: composite essences are made, but simple essences are not, because making requires possibility, and composite essences have parts that are themselves simple and made. The commentary then analyzes the phrase 'the possible is in need by itself of an agent'. It distinguishes between being essentially in need (ihtiyaj dhati) versus being in need by itself (bi dhatihi) for existence, clarifying the exact point of contention. It then explains the response in more detail: the universal essence is not made, only the identity (which is a particular instance). The objection that the universal existence is included in the particular is answered by noting that need (ihtiyaj) is specific to the particular and does not transfer to the universal. The investigation is about the essence in itself, not the mixed essence found in external reality. Throughout, the passage maintains the crucial distinction between the essence as a concept and its concrete instantiation in existence.

The Third School: Composite Essences Are Made
Third school position and detailed commentary on need, identity, and the answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third school's position that composite essences are made while simple essences are not, and the detailed analysis of need and existence that underlies it.

Core Idea
Composite essences are made, simple essences are not, because making requires possibility and composition.

This lesson presents the third school's position on the making of essences: composite essences are made, but simple essences are not. The passage then delves into a nuanced discussion about the nature of need (ihtiyaj) and the distinction between universal and particular existence. Pay close attention to how the author defends this position against objections.

Guided Path
1
Read the third school's statement
Read the sentence stating that the composite essence is made, unlike the simple essence, because the condition of being-made is possibility. Note that this is a middle position between the first school and the answer.
The third school agrees with the answer that simple essences are not made, but claims composite ones are.
2
Analyze the commentary on need
Read the commentary on the phrase 'the possible is in need by itself of an agent'. This clarifies the kind of need at stake: is it essential need or need for existence? The author distinguishes between being essentially in need and being in need by itself in existence.
This distinction is crucial for understanding the disagreement.
3
Understand the answer's implications
Read the further commentary on the answer, which denies the implication that if something is made then its essence is made. The author argues that the made is the identity, not the universal, and that need is particular.
The objection about universal existence being included in particular is answered by noting that need is specific to the particular.
4
Reflect on the overall argument
Think about how the third school's position fits with the answer. The third school accepts that simple essences are not made, but claims composite ones are because they have parts that are simple and made. The answer's distinction applies to simple essences, but composite ones are made in a different sense.
The discussion is subtle and involves the nature of composition.

المرصد الرابع - المقصد الخامس : في أقسام الواحد
أحد المذاهب هو أن الماهيات كلها مجعولة، أما البسيطة فلأنها ممكنة؛ والممكن محتاج لذاته إلى فاعل وأما المركبة فكذلك أيضاً، أو لأن أجزاءها البسيطة مجعولة. ( والجواب : أن المجعول هو الوجود الخاص ) أي هويته ) لا ماهية الوجود ) ، فلا يلزم من ارتفاع المجعولية عن الماهيات بأسرها ارتفاع المجعولية رأساً، واستغناء الممكن عن الفاعل المؤثر. المذهب ( الثالث ( الماهية المركبة مجعولة، بخلاف ) الماهية ( البسيطة ؛ لأن شرط المجعولية الإمكان، وذلك لأن المجعولية فرع الاحتياج إلى
قوله : ( والممكن محتاج لذاته إلى فاعل فيه أن اللازم أن يكون البسيط لذاته محتاجاً إلى فاعل، والمدعى أن يكون في ذاته محتاجاً إلى فاعل؛ لأن النزاع في أن الماهيات هل هي في نفسها محتاجة إلى فاعل أم لا؟ فيجوز أن تكون لذاته لا لغيره محتاجا إلى فاعل في الوجود، ولا يكون محتاجاً في ذاته إلى شيء، لعل المصنف لأجل كون الاستدلال المشهور ظاهر البطلان تركه، واستدل بما هو المذكور في الكتاب .
قوله : ( أو لأن أجزاءها إلخ) ولا نعني بكون الشيء مجعولاً إلا تعلق الجعل به، سواء كان
باعتبار ذاته أو باعتبار أجزائه .
قوله : (والجواب إلخ) حاصله منع الملازمة المدلول عليها بقوله : لأن كل ما فرض مجعولاً فهو ماهية لجواز أن تكون هوية أي ماهية شخصية لا ماهية كلية . وفيه : أن النزاع في أن الماهية بمعنى ما به الشيء هو كليا أو جزئياً جعولة أو لا، لا في الماهية الكلية. وأما على ما ذكرنا من التقرير، فحاصل الجواب منع الشرطية بناء على أن المجعول هوية الوجود، لا ماهية الوجود الصادقة عليه فضلاً عن مطلق الماهية ولا يلزم من صدق شيء على شيء أن يكون مجعولاً، وإلا يلزم أن تكون السلوب والعدمات الصادقة عليه مجعولة .
قوله : (أي هويته ) أي المراد بالوجود الخاص أشخاصه لا مفهومه الكلي .
قوله : ( الماهية المركبة مجعولة لئلا يلزم نفي المجعولية بالكلية، ولظهوره لم يتعرض له .
اعتبر بالقياس إلى الأجزاء المقدارية الغير المتشابهة، فلا يتم التقريب، ويناسب الجواب أيضاً. وفيه نظر إذ المقدر حينئذ أن ليس بعض الماهيات مجعولة، لأن نقيض الإيجاب الكلي الذي ادعى السلب الجزئي . وما ذكره إنما يتم لو كان المقدر السلب الكلي . اللهم إلا أن يبنى الكلام على أن بعض الماهيات إذا لم تكن مجعولة كان الجميع كذلك إذ لا فرق بين ماهية وماهية بعد كونها خاصة ممكنة تأمل .
قوله : (هو الوجود الخاص إلخ) قيل : يلزم أن تكون الماهية أيضاً مجعولةً لأن جعل وجود العام ضروري في ضمن الخاص والجواب: أن المجعولية هو الاحتياج، ولا يلزم من الاحتياج الخاص الاحتياج العام. وقد يجاب بأن البحث في الماهية من حيث هي هي : لا في الماهية المخلوطة؛ كما سيعلم من التحرير .
The Fourth Observation - The Fifth Objective: On the divisions of the one. One of the schools is that all essences (al-mahiyyat) are made (maj'ula); as for the simple (essence), because it is possible (mumkin); and the possible is in need by itself of an agent; and as for the composite, likewise, or because its simple parts are made. The answer: That which is made is the particular existence (al-wujud al-khas), i.e., its identity (huwiyya), not the essence of existence, so it does not follow from the removal of being-made from all essences the removal of being-made entirely, and the independence of the possible from the effective agent. The third school: The composite essence is made, unlike the simple essence, because the condition of being-made is possibility, and that is because being-made is a branch of need for the effective agent. His statement: 'And the possible is in need by itself of an agent' — therein that the necessary is that the simple is in need by itself of an agent, but what is claimed is that it is in need in its essence of an agent; because the dispute is whether essences in themselves are in need of an agent or not? So it is possible that it is in need by itself, not by another, of an agent in existence, and it is not in need in its essence of anything. Perhaps the author, because the well-known proof was apparently invalid, left it and argued with what is mentioned in the book. His statement: 'Or because its parts, etc.' — we do not mean by a thing being made except the relation of making (al-ja'l) to it, whether in consideration of its essence or in consideration of its parts. His statement: 'The answer, etc.' — its summary is the denial of the implication indicated by his statement: 'because everything supposed to be made is an essence' — it is possible that it is an identity, i.e., a personal essence (mahiyya shakhsiyya), not a universal essence. And therein: that the dispute is about the essence in the sense of 'that by which a thing is what it is', whether universal or particular, being made or not, not about the universal essence. And according to the formulation we have mentioned, the summary of the answer is the denial of the conditional premise based on that which is made is the identity of existence, not the essence of existence true of it, let alone absolute essence, and it does not follow from the truth of a thing about a thing that it is made; otherwise it would follow that privations (sulub) and non-existents (adamāt) true of it would be made. His statement: 'i.e., its identity' — meaning by 'particular existence' its individuals, not its universal concept. His statement: 'The composite essence is made lest it lead to the negation of being-made entirely, and due to its obviousness he did not discuss it.' Consider by comparison to the quantitative parts that are not similar; then the approximation is not complete, and the answer is also appropriate. And therein is consideration, for what is assumed then is that some essences are not made, because the contradictory of the universal affirmative that he claimed is the particular negative. What he mentioned would only be complete if what was assumed was the universal negative. Unless the discussion is built on the basis that if some essences are not made, then all are likewise, since there is no difference between one essence and another after they are particular possible. Contemplate. His statement: 'He is the particular existence, etc.' It was said: It follows that the essence is also made because making the universal existence is necessarily within the particular. And the answer: Being-made is need (al-ihtiyaj), and it does not follow from particular need universal need. And it may be answered that the investigation is about the essence insofar as it is essence, not about the mixed essence, as will be known from the exposition (al-tahrir).
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage defines the third position on the making of essences and provides a detailed response. It first states a school (madhhab) that all essences are made, whether simple or composite, because they are possible and need an agent. The answer immediately refutes this: what is made is the particular existence (al-wujud al-khas), i.e., the concrete identity (huwiyya), not the essence of existence itself. Thus, denying that essences are made does not negate making entirely or imply that the possible is independent of an agent. The third position is then given: composite essences are made, but simple essences are not, because making requires possibility, and composite essences have parts that are themselves simple and made. The commentary then analyzes the phrase 'the possible is in need by itself of an agent'. It distinguishes between being essentially in need (ihtiyaj dhati) versus being in need by itself (bi dhatihi) for existence, clarifying the exact point of contention. It then explains the response in more detail: the universal essence is not made, only the identity (which is a particular instance). The objection that the universal existence is included in the particular is answered by noting that need (ihtiyaj) is specific to the particular and does not transfer to the universal. The investigation is about the essence in itself, not the mixed essence found in external reality. Throughout, the passage maintains the crucial distinction between the essence as a concept and its concrete instantiation in existence.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if composite essences are made, then simple essences must also be made because they are parts.
Correction: The third school specifically states that simple essences are not made; the making of composites does not require making of simples as objects of making, but rather the composite is made due to its composition and possibility.

Checkpoint
According to the third school, why is the composite essence made but not the simple essence?
A) Because the composite essence is possible, while the simple is necessary.
B) Because the condition of being-made is possibility, and the composite essence has the additional condition of parts.
C) Because the simple essence is eternal, while the composite is temporal.
D) Because the composite essence is dependent on its parts, which are themselves made.

Key Takeaway
The third school holds composite essences are made due to possibility and composition, while simple essences are not; the answer's distinction between identity and universal essence preserves this position.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, do you treat your own existence as something that is 'made' by God, or do you act as if you are self-sufficient? Reflect on a moment today when you felt independent. How does the lesson's distinction between essence and identity challenge that feeling?
?
The passage argues that simple essences are not in need of a maker. Does this mean that your simple nature (your 'whatness') is not directly created? How does that affect your sense of responsibility for who you are?
?
If composite things require an agent, and you are a composite of body and soul, then you are utterly dependent. How does this truth confront your pride today? What would change if you truly lived as a 'made' thing?

Causality and Created Things

The Self-Evident Conception of Need
Al-Ījī declares the conception of need as necessary and explains its inclusion in General Matters.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the conception of a thing's need for another is considered necessary and self-evident in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
The need for a cause is a self-evident truth, not an acquired concept.

This lesson introduces the fifth observatory on cause and effect from al-Ījī's work. The first goal states that the conception of a thing's need for another is necessary (ḍarūrī), meaning it is innate and does not require reasoning. We will explore why this is foundational for discussions of causality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to understand the author’s flow.
Notice how the passage ties causality to the broader category of General Matters.
2
Identify the key claim about need
Find the statement that the conception of need is necessary and obtained without acquisition. Why is this important?
3
Reflect on why it is self-evident
Think about every person’s natural understanding that if something exists, it may need something else. The commentator emphasizes that no reasoning is needed.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الخامس في العلة والمعلول ] لما كانت العلية والمعلولية من العوارض الشاملة للموجودات على سبيل التقابل كالإمكان والوجوب ( أورد مباحثهما في الأمور العامة، وفيه مقاصد ) عشرة : [ المقصد الأول : تصور احتياج الشيء إلى غيره ضروري ] تصور احتياج الشيء إلى غيره ضروري، حاصل بلا اكتساب، فإن كل أحد قوله : ( لما كانت إلخ) يعني أنه لما كانت حال العلية والمعلولية في عدم شمول كل واحد منهما لجميع الموجودات بناء على أن برهان التطبيق قام على وجوب الانتهاء في طرف العلية والمعلولية، فلا بد من علة لا تكون معلولاً، ومن معلول لا يكون علة، وشمولهما لجميع الموجودات على سبيل التقابل كحال الوجوب الذاتي والإمكان الخاص أورد مباحثها في الأمور العامة. وفيه إشارة إلى أن ما فعله الإمام في كتابه : «الملخص) والمباحث المشرقية حيث جعل الوجوب والإمكان من الأمور العامة دون العلية والمعلولية تحكم وما قيل: إن مراده أن إيراد مباحثهما في الأمور العامة مبني على التفسير الثاني للأمور العامة لا على تفسير المصنف؛ لأنه يلزم أن يكون مباحث العلية مذكوراً استطراداً فليس بشيء، أما أولاً، فلان بناء إيراد المصنف علي تفسير لم يذكره مما لا معنى له وقراءة أورد على المجهول يجعل كلام الشارح قدس سره لغوا . وأما ثانيا : فلان لزوم الاستطراد ممنوع ولو سلم فهو لازم في الوجوب أيضاً، كما ذكره الشارح قدس سره سابقاً. وأما ثالثاً : فلان التفسير الثاني، وهو ما يشمل المفهومات بأسرها، لا الموجودات فقط. وأما رابعاً : فلانه حينئذ يصير قوله : كالوجوب والإمكان مستدركاً. قوله : ( تصور احتياج الشيء ولو بالوجه .
The Fifth Observatory on Cause and Effect. Since causality and being caused are among the universal accidents covering existents in the manner of opposition, like possibility and necessity, (he presented their investigations in General Matters, and it contains ten Goals). [The First Goal: The conception of a thing's need for another is necessary.] The conception of a thing's need for another is necessary, obtained without acquisition, for everyone... His statement: (Since causality etc.) means: Since the state of causality and being caused, in that each of them does not cover all existents – based on the fact that the proof of application (burhān al-taṭbīq) established the necessity of termination on the side of cause and effect – so there must be a cause that is not an effect, and an effect that is not a cause, and their covering all existents in the manner of opposition is like the state of essential necessity and specific possibility, he presented their investigations in General Matters. And in it there is an indication that what the Imam did in his book 'al-Mulakhkhaṣ' and 'al-Mabāḥith al-Mashriqiyya', where he made necessity and possibility from General Matters without causality and being caused, is arbitrary. And what was said: that his intention is that presenting their investigations in General Matters is based on the second interpretation of General Matters, not on the author's interpretation – because it would necessitate that the investigations of causality be mentioned as a digression – is nothing. As for first, because basing the author's presentation on an interpretation he did not mention is meaningless, and reading 'ūrida' in the passive would make the commentator's speech, may his secret be sanctified, vain. As for second, because the necessity of digression is denied, and even if granted, it would also be necessary for necessity, as the commentator, may his secret be sanctified, mentioned previously. As for third, because the second interpretation is that which encompasses concepts entirely, not existents only. As for fourth, because then his statement 'like possibility and necessity' would become redundant. His statement: (The conception of a thing's need for another, even if only in some respect).
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage opens the fifth observatory on cause and effect (al-ʿilla wa al-maʿlūl). It first states that causality and being caused are universal accidents that cover all existents in a reciprocal manner, similar to how possibility and necessity are opposed. Therefore, the author, al-Ījī, includes their discussion under the heading of General Matters (al-umūr al-ʿāmmah). The first goal within this observatory is announced: the conception of a thing's need for another is necessary (ḍarūrī), meaning it is a self-evident idea not acquired through reasoning. The commentator then explains the reasoning: causality and being caused do not each cover all existents (since there must be a first cause that is not an effect), but together they cover all existents reciprocally, just as essential necessity and specific possibility do. The commentator then criticizes the approach of Imam al-Rāzī, who placed necessity and possibility in General Matters but did not include causality. This is deemed arbitrary. The commentator refutes the objection that the author's placement might rely on a different interpretation of 'General Matters' (i.e., including concepts, not just existents). He gives four arguments: first, the author did not mention such an interpretation; second, the claim of digression is false; third, the second interpretation would include concepts only, not existents; fourth, it would make the comparison to necessity and possibility redundant. The passage ends with a note that the conception of need is even in some respect (wa law bi-l-wajh).

Key Terms
علة
معلول
علية
معلولية
الأمور العامة
برهان التطبيق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the nature of the conception of a thing's need for another?
A) It is acquired through reasoning
B) It is necessary and self-evident
C) It is based on divine revelation
D) It is only metaphorical

Key Takeaway
The conception of need is a self-evident truth that does not require reasoning; it is the foundation for all causal arguments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the conception of need is self-evident, why do you sometimes act as if you are self-sufficient? When have you felt a deep need for something beyond yourself, and how did you respond to that innate awareness? Commit today to acknowledging one real need you have been ignoring.
?
The text says everyone inherently knows that things need causes. Do you truly live with that conviction, or do you often ignore the implications of your own need for a Creator? What one change would you make today to consciously depend on Allah in a specific area of your life?
?
If the concept of need is necessary and not acquired, then your awareness of your dependence is always with you. Are you suppressing it or embracing it? What is one concrete step you will take this week to turn to Allah in humility, using that innate awareness as a means of drawing closer to Him?

Causality and Created Things

Defending the Inclusion of Causality in General Matters
Commentator critiques al-Rāzī and defends inclusion of causality in General Matters with four arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the arguments for including causality in General Matters and the refutation of Imam al-Rāzī's alternative approach.

Core Idea
Causality belongs in General Matters just as necessity and possibility do.

After establishing the self-evidence of need, the commentator defends al-Ījī's decision to discuss causality under General Matters. He critiques Imam al-Rāzī for excluding causality and refutes objections that it is a digression. This lesson unpacks those four arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the argumentative style.
This passage is a refutation, so pay attention to the structure.
2
Identify the criticism of al-Rāzī
Find where the commentator says al-Rāzī's action is 'arbitrary' (تحكم). What is the issue?
3
Understand the four arguments
The commentator gives four points against the alternative interpretation. List them in your mind.
Each argument starts with 'أما'.
4
Note the final clarification
The passage ends with 'even if only in some respect', qualifying the conception of need.
This shows the conception is relational, not absolute.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Correction: The commentator argues that if causality is a digression, so is necessity, and the placement is based on a proper understanding of General Matters as covering reciprocal accidents.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what is wrong with al-Rāzī's exclusion of causality from General Matters?
A) He forgot to include it
B) It is arbitrary (taḥakkum)
C) He used the wrong interpretation of General Matters
D) He should have included only necessity

Key Takeaway
The inclusion of causality in General Matters is defended by showing that the alternative interpretations are untenable.

The Necessity of Conceiving Dependence and the Definition of Cause
Al-Jurjani argues that the conception of need is necessary and defines cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Jurjani holds the conception of a thing's need for another to be necessary and how he defines a cause.

Core Idea
The idea that things need other things is already known to everyone.

Al-Jurjani begins the fifth observation by establishing a foundational epistemological point: the conception of a thing's dependence on another is not acquired through learning; it is a necessary conception that every rational person already possesses. From this self-evident truth, he then defines what a cause is. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage slowly, then follow the literal English translation. Notice how the author states his claim and then expands with examples.
The passage includes commentary interpolations; focus on the core matn (the first few lines).
2
Identify the claim about necessity
Al-Jurjani asserts that the conception of a thing's need is 'necessary' (daruri) and 'attained without acquisition' (bila iktisab). Find the justification he gives: everyone knows their needs and independences.
The phrase 'the conception that precedes necessary affirmation is more appropriate to be necessary' reinforces the point.
3
Understand the definition of cause
From the necessity of need, the author defines a cause (ʿilla) as 'that which is needed for the existence of something'. Note the clarifications: it includes composite causes (like matter, form, agent) and excludes causality in non-existence.
The mention of 'independence' (istighna') is either incidental or because its necessity implies the necessity of need.

Source Text

المرصد الخامس - المقصد الأول : تصور احتياج الشيء إلى غيره ضروري

The fifth observation - The first objective: The conception of a thing's need for another is necessary. The conception of a thing's need for another is necessary, attained without acquisition, for everyone has a need for things and independence from things. And the conception that precedes necessary affirmation is more appropriate to be necessary. So that which is needed for the existence of something is called a cause for it, and that is from... His statement: (Everyone is able to acquire first. His statement: (Absolutely) means the necessary with respect to everyone, even the imbecile and children. His statement: (So that which is needed, whether by itself or with respect to its parts, to include the complete cause composed of matter, form, and agent, for it is needed with respect to the agent; but its essence, meaning the totality, is in need of the totality of matter and form which is identical to the effect, as the need of the whole for its part. As his statement: (for the existence of something) he indicated by that that causality in non-existence is merely a rational consideration, whose return is the non-existence of the causality of existence for existence. It will come later. His statement: (and his independence from things) he mentioned independence either as incidental, or because it is non-existence and its necessity entails the necessity of the need which is our subject.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage presents the first objective of the fifth observation. It states that the conception of a thing's need for another is a necessary conception (tasawwur daruri), obtained without acquisition (iktisab), because everyone has a basic awareness that some things are needed and others are not. The conception that precedes the necessary affirmation (tasdiq) is even more appropriate to be necessary. Then it defines a cause (ʿilla) as that which something needs for its existence. The commentary then expands: the necessity is absolute, covering even the intellectually impaired and children. The phrase 'that which is needed' includes both the simple and the composite cause (e.g., matter, form, agent), clarifying that the composite cause is needed with respect to its agent part, but the whole is not needed in the same way—rather, the whole is in need of its parts. The phrase 'for the existence of something' indicates that causality in non-existence is only a rational notion, not real. The mention of 'independence' is either incidental or because it is non-existence and its necessity implies the necessity of need.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the necessity of the conception means the concept itself is innate or prior to all thought.
Correction: Al-Jurjani clarifies it is a necessary conception, but not necessarily innate; it arises naturally from experience, yet is still not acquired through deliberate learning.

Key Takeaway
The conception of a thing's need for another is self-evident, and a cause is defined as that which something depends on for its existence.

Objection and Resolution on Composite Causes
Al-Jurjani's objection and resolution on composite causality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a composite cause cannot be strictly defined as 'that which is needed' and how al-Jurjani resolves the objection by distinguishing between single and multiple needs.

Core Idea
A composite cause is needed through its parts, not as a single whole.

Al-Jurjani tackles a subtle objection: if the cause is composite, then the effect's parts are part of the cause, but the whole is not needed by the parts. This seems to break the definition of cause as 'that which is needed'. In this lesson, we follow the author's careful analysis and his resolution using the distinction between single and multiple needs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation to understand the objection and resolution flow.
The Arabic is dense; note the use of technical terms like 'علة تامة' and 'احتياج'.
2
Identify the objection
Find the claim that if the effect is composite, its parts are part of the cause, so the cause is not needed by the effect; rather the whole needs its parts.
This challenges the core definition of cause as 'that which is needed'.
3
Examine the first resolution and number analogy
Notice how al-Jurjani first suggests that the part of the cause is each component (matter/form) individually, not the totality. Then see the analogy with numbers: the parts of ten are units, not numbers like two.
This analogy helps clarify that 'part of cause' can be multiple items.
4
Understand the final resolution
Al-Jurjani ends by broadening the meaning of 'need' to include multiple needs, and 'that which is needed' to refer to each part individually. This preserves the definition.
The resolution hinges on the generality of the term 'احتياج'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (فالمحتاج إليه في وجود شيء يسمى علة قيل : المعلول إذا كان مركباً فجميع أجزائه التي هي عينه يكون جزءاً من العلة التامة، والجزء لا يكون محتاجاً إلى الكل، بل الأمر بالعكس فإطلاق لفظ العلة عليها اصطلاح ،آخر لا بمعنى المحتاج إليه، كيف والاحتياج يستلزم التقدم والعلة التامة في الصورة المذكورة لا تتقدم على المعلول، لا زماناً ولا ذاتاً؟ كما سيصرح به وقد يقال : جزء العلة التامة كل واحد من المادة ،والصورة لا مجموعها . وإلا لزم كون المعلول عين العلة لأن جزء العلة علة ونظيره ما ذكره المحققون من أن أجزاء العدد الذي . يتوهم تركبه الأعداد من هي الوحدات لا تلك الأعداد، مثلاً الاثنان ليس جزءاً من العشرة، وقد سبق تحقيقه. والإنصاف أن كلا من المادة والصورة كما أنه داخل في قوام العلة التامة للمعلول المركب، كذلك مجموعهما، والاثنان إنما لا يكون جزءاً من العشرة ؛ بناء على ما سبق من إمكان تصورها بالكنه بدون تصوره، وإمكان تصور العلة التامة بالكنه بدون تصور هذا المجموع، وكذا لزوم كون جزء العلة علة ممنوع وأما كون جزء العلة علة فإنما هو على تقدير التسليم في جزء العلة بمعنى المحتاج إليه. وقد يقال : المراد بالاحتياج المأخوذ في تفسير العلة أعم من الاحتياج الواحد والاحتياجات المتعددة والثانية موجودة في العلة التامة باعتبار أجزائها المنفردة، والتقدم وكذا لزوم كون جزئها علة إنما يلزم في المعنى الأول ، ولك أن تقول : المراد بالمحتاج إليه أعم من أن يكون هو نفسه كذلك، أو كل واحد من أجزائه المتعددة، نظيره ما ذكره الشارح في حواشي الطوالع من أن معنى قولهم : الحد التام تعريف بالداخل دخول جزء من
•
أجزائه .
His statement: (So that which is needed for the existence of something is called a cause) he said: if the effect is composite, then all its parts which are identical to it become part of the complete cause, and the part is not in need of the whole; rather, the opposite is the case, so applying the term 'cause' to it is another convention, not in the sense of that which is needed. How so, for need entails priority, and the complete cause in the mentioned form does not precede the effect, neither temporally nor essentially, as he will clarify. And it may be said: the part of the complete cause is each one of matter and form, not their totality. Otherwise, it would necessitate that the effect is identical to the cause because a part of the cause is a cause. An analogous example is what the researchers mentioned: the parts of a number that is imagined to be composed of numbers are the units, not those numbers; for example, two is not a part of ten, and this has been verified previously. The fairest view is that each of matter and form is included in the constitution of the complete cause for the composite effect, and so is their totality. And two is not a part of ten based on what preceded: the possibility of conceiving it in essence without conceiving it, and the possibility of conceiving the complete cause in essence without conceiving this totality. Likewise, the necessity that a part of the cause is a cause is denied. As for a part of the cause being a cause, it is only on the supposition of accepting 'part of the cause' in the sense of that which is needed. And it may be said: what is meant by need taken in the definition of cause is more general than single need and multiple needs, and the second is present in the complete cause with respect to its individual parts. Priority and likewise the necessity that its part is a cause only apply in the first sense. But you may say: what is meant by that which is needed is more general than it being itself like that, or each one of its multiple parts. An analogy is what the commentator mentioned in his glosses on al-Tawaliʿ: that the meaning of their statement 'the complete definition is a definition by the internal, the entry of a part of its parts.'
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its resolution regarding the definition of cause as 'that which is needed' when the cause is composite. The objection states that if the effect is composite, its parts are identical to it and become parts of the complete cause. But a part is not in need of the whole; rather, the whole is in need of its parts. Therefore, applying the term 'cause' to the composite is only a convention, not in the sense of that which is needed, because need implies priority, while the complete cause does not precede the effect temporally or essentially. The resolution proceeds in several steps. First, it suggests that the part of the complete cause is each individual component (matter and form), not their totality. Second, it rejects the implication that the effect would be identical to the cause. Third, it uses the analogy of numbers: the parts of ten are the units, not two (which is itself a number). Fourth, it argues that the need in the definition is ambiguous between single need and multiple needs; the composite cause is needed in the latter sense. Finally, it concludes that 'that which is needed' can refer either to the whole itself or to each of its parts individually, citing a gloss on al-Tawaliʿ about complete definition.

جزء العلة
احتياج

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The composite cause cannot be a proper cause because the whole is not needed by its parts.
Correction: The author shows that need can be distributed across parts, so the composite cause is needed in the sense of multiple needs.

Checkpoint
How does the commentator resolve the objection that a composite cause cannot be 'that which is needed'?
A) By denying that composite causes exist.
B) By arguing that the need is for the whole, not the parts.
C) By distinguishing between single need and multiple needs.

Key Takeaway
The resolution lies in recognizing that need can be multiple, and 'that which is needed' can apply to each part individually.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

In your own life, are there ‘wholes’—like your identity or your worship—that you treat as self-sufficient, forgetting the many parts that constitute them? What would change if you saw yourself as dependent on each component?
?
The resolution shows that need can be multiple and not imply temporal priority. How does this affect your understanding of Allah’s attributes as causes of creation? Do you sometimes assume that Allah’s creative act must be simple and instantaneous, ignoring the multiple ‘needs’ in His decree? How will you adjust your thinking to embrace this complexity?

Conceiving Need and Defining Cause
Al-Jurjani explains the necessity of conceiving need and defines cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the conception of a thing's need is necessary and how Al-Jurjani defines cause as that which is needed.

Core Idea
The conception of need is necessary and prior to judgment; cause is what a thing depends on.

Al-Jurjani begins a discussion on causality by establishing that the conception of a thing's need for another is a necessary concept. He argues that this conception is even prior to necessary judgments and then defines the cause as that which is needed in existence. This passage sets the foundation for understanding causal relationships in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation to grasp the overall flow.
Pay attention to the technical terms like احتياج and علة.
2
Identify the main claim about necessity
Notice that Al-Jurjani states that the conception of a thing's need is 'ضروري' (necessary) and that this conception is prior to necessary judgment.
The word 'أولى' indicates that the conception is more appropriate to be necessary.
3
Understand the definition of cause
See how Al-Jurjani defines cause as 'المحتاج إليه في وجود شيء' (that which is needed in the existence of a thing). He expands this to include complete causes composed of matter, form, and agent.
The mention of complete cause shows the scope of the definition.
4
Reflect on the mention of independence
Consider why Al-Jurjani brings up independence (استغناء). He suggests it is either parenthetical or because its necessity implies the necessity of need.
This links back to the main topic of need.

The fifth observation - first goal: The conception of a thing's need for another is necessary. Its need for matters and its independence from matters, and the conception prior to the necessary judgment absolutely is more appropriate to be necessary. So that which is needed in the existence of a thing is called a cause for it. And that from his saying: 'Everyone is able to acquire first.' Objection: Composite Effect and the Part-Whole Problem
Al-Jurjani presents the objection about composite effects and gives an initial response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that if a composite effect's parts are part of its cause, then the part cannot need the whole, and the initial response that the part of the cause is each individual constituent (matter and form) rather than their aggregate.

Core Idea
A part cannot need the whole, so how can a composite effect's parts be in its cause?

This lesson presents a critical objection to the definition of cause as 'that which is needed' when the effect is composite. The objector argues that if all parts of the effect become parts of the cause, then a part would not need the whole, contradicting the definition. Al-Jurjani then offers an initial response: the part of the complete cause is each individual component (matter and form) separately, not their totality, illustrating this with the analogy of numbers.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Notice how the objector claims that for a composite effect, its parts become parts of the complete cause, leading to the problem that a part does not need the whole.
2
Identify the initial response
Al-Jurjani says that the part of the complete cause is each one of matter and form, not their aggregate. Understand why this avoids the problem.
3
Study the number analogy
He compares to the parts of a number being units, not the numbers. Reflect on how this supports his point.

قوله : ( على التصديق الضروري مطلقاً) أي بالنسبة إلى الكل حتى البله والصبيان، فلا یرد جواز كسبية أطراف البديهي، ويحتمل أن يكون مطلقاً قيداً للتصور أي بالكنه، أو بوجه ما فإنه كاف في المطلوب . قوله: (فالمحتاج إليه في وجود شيء يسمى علة قيل : المعلول إذا كان مركباً فجميع أجزائه التي هي عينه يكون جزءاً من العلة التامة، والجزء لا يكون محتاجاً إلى الكل، بل الأمر بالعكس فإطلاق لفظ العلة عليها اصطلاح ،آخر لا بمعنى المحتاج إليه، كيف والاحتياج يستلزم التقدم والعلة التامة في الصورة المذكورة لا تتقدم على المعلول، لا زماناً ولا ذاتاً؟ كما سيصرح به وقد يقال : جزء العلة التامة كل واحد من المادة ،والصورة لا مجموعها . وإلا لزم كون المعلول عين العلة لأن جزء العلة علة ونظيره ما ذكره المحققون من أن أجزاء العدد الذي . يتوهم تركبه الأعداد من هي الوحدات لا تلك الأعداد، مثلاً الاثنان ليس جزءاً من العشرة، وقد سبق تحقيقه. والإنصاف أن كلا من المادة والصورة كما أنه داخل في قوام العلة التامة للمعلول المركب، كذلك مجموعهما، والاثنان إنما لا يكون جزءاً من العشرة ؛ بناء على ما سبق من إمكان تصورها بالكنه بدون تصوره، وإمكان تصور العلة التامة بالكنه بدون تصور هذا المجموع، وكذا لزوم كون جزء العلة علة ممنوع وأما كون جزء العلة علة فإنما هو على تقدير التسليم في جزء العلة بمعنى المحتاج إليه. وقد يقال : المراد بالاحتياج المأخوذ في تفسير العلة أعم من الاحتياج الواحد والاحتياجات المتعددة والثانية موجودة في العلة التامة باعتبار أجزائها المنفردة، والتقدم وكذا لزوم كون جزئها علة إنما يلزم في المعنى الأول ، ولك أن تقول : المراد بالمحتاج إليه أعم من أن يكون هو نفسه كذلك، أو كل واحد من أجزائه المتعددة، نظيره ما ذكره الشارح في حواشي الطوالع من أن معنى قولهم : الحد التام تعريف بالداخل دخول جزء من أجزائه .
His saying: 'Upon the necessary judgment absolutely' meaning relative to everyone, even the idiotic and children, so the possibility of the [acquisition] of the parts of the self-evident is not refuted. And it is possible that 'absolutely' is a qualification for the conception, i.e., by essence or in some way, for it suffices for the desired. His saying: 'So that which is needed in the existence of something is called a cause.' It is said: If the effect is composite, then all its parts which are identical to it become a part of the complete cause, and a part is not in need of the whole, rather the opposite. So the application of the term 'cause' to it is a different convention, not in the sense of that which is needed. How so, since need entails priority, and the complete cause in the mentioned form does not precede the effect, neither in time nor in essence? As he will clarify. And it may be said: The part of the complete cause is each one of matter and form, not their aggregate. Otherwise, it would necessitate that the effect is identical to the cause because a part of the cause is a cause. An analogy is what the investigators mentioned: that the parts of a number which is imagined to be composed of numbers are the units, not those numbers. For example, two is not a part of ten. This has been verified earlier. And the fair view is that each of matter and form, just as it is included in the constitution of the complete cause for the composite effect, so too is their aggregate. And two is not a part of ten only because of the earlier [proof] of the possibility of conceiving it by essence without conceiving its aggregate, and the possibility of conceiving the complete cause by essence without conceiving this aggregate. Likewise, the necessity that a part of the cause is a cause is denied. As for a part of the cause being a cause, it is only on the assumption of conceding that the part is in the sense of that which is needed. And it may be said: The need taken in the definition of cause is more general than a single need and multiple needs, and the second is present in the complete cause with respect to its individual parts. Priority and likewise the necessity that its part is a cause only follow in the first meaning. And you may say: That which is needed is more general than it being itself so, or each one of its multiple parts. An analogy is what the commentator mentioned in the glosses of al-Tawali': that the meaning of their saying 'the complete definition is a definition by the intrinsic, entering as a part of its parts.'
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against the definition of cause as that which is needed, specifically regarding composite effects. The objector argues that if the effect is composite, its parts become part of the complete cause, but a part is not in need of the whole, so the cause would not be needed. Moreover, the complete cause would not precede the effect. The commentator responds with several points: first, it may be said that each individual part (matter and form) is the part of the cause, not their aggregate. Otherwise, the effect would be identical to the cause. He gives an analogy: the parts of a number are the units, not the number itself. Second, the fair view is that both matter and form individually and their aggregate are part of the cause, but the objection based on priority is denied. Third, the need in the definition can be multiple needs, so the complete cause has multiple needs. Fourth, 'that which is needed' is more general, covering both the whole and each part. He concludes with an analogy from al-Tawali' about the complete definition.

معلول مركب
مادة
صورة
جزء العلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If the effect is composite, its parts become parts of the complete cause, making the part need the whole, which is impossible.
Correction: Al-Jurjani responds that the part of the complete cause is each individual component (matter and form) separately, not their aggregate, thereby avoiding the problem of a part needing the whole.

Checkpoint
According to the initial response in this passage, what is considered the part of the complete cause for a composite effect?
A) The aggregate of matter and form
B) Each of matter and form individually
C) The effect itself
D) The whole universe

Key Takeaway
The initial response to the composite effect objection is that the complete cause's parts are the individual constituents (matter and form), not their combination, avoiding the part-whole need contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever assume that a cause must precede its effect in time? How does this lesson challenge your assumptions about God's creative power, which is not bound by temporal priority? What does that mean for how you trust His actions in your life?
?
When you see composite things in the world—your body, your relationships, your challenges—do you tend to think their cause must contain all their parts? How does this lesson correct that? Can you apply this to stop attributing ultimate causation to secondary means and instead see them as tools of Allah?
?
The objection uses the part-whole relation to question causality. What 'parts' of your life do you rely on as if they are the cause of your well-being? How does realizing that the ultimate Cause is not composed of parts change your reliance on Allah alone? What step can you take today to shift that reliance?

Responses: Reconciling the Complete Cause with Composite Effects
Al-Jurjani provides comprehensive responses to the composite effect objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the subsequent responses to the composite effect objection, including the fair view that both individual parts and their aggregate are part of the cause, the generalization of need to multiple needs, and the generalization of 'that which is needed' to cover parts.

Core Idea
The complete cause can be composite without violating the definition of need.

Building on the initial response, Al-Jurjani provides further clarifications. He presents the fair view that both matter and form individually and their aggregate are part of the complete cause. He then addresses the issue of priority and the necessity that a part of the cause is itself a cause. Finally, he generalizes the concept of need to encompass multiple needs, and extends 'that which is needed' to include each part of a composite cause, drawing an analogy with complete definitions.

Guided Path
1
Understand the 'fair view'
Al-Jurjani states that both each part and their aggregate are part of the complete cause. Consider why this does not lead to the earlier problem.
He argues that the number analogy does not apply here because the cause can be conceived without conceiving the aggregate.
2
Analyze the denial of 'part of cause is cause'
He denies the necessity that a part of the cause is itself a cause unless we concede a specific meaning. Notice the distinction.
3
Generalize the concept of need
Need can be multiple, so the complete cause with its parts satisfies multiple needs. This resolves the priority objection.
4
Generalize 'that which is needed'
The needed can be the whole or each part. He concludes with an analogy from definitions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Reflect on your own spiritual journey: Are you relying on a single practice (like just praying) as the 'complete cause' of your faith, or do you acknowledge the multiple 'parts' (knowledge, action, sincerity, community) that together form the complete cause? What part are you neglecting? Commit to one action today to address that missing part.
?

In your life, do you treat the various means of drawing closer to Allah (e.g., reading Quran, charity, night prayer) as optional extras, or do you recognize each as an essential part of the complete cause of your spiritual growth? Which of these 'parts' have you been avoiding, and what will you do tomorrow to include it?
?

This lesson shows that multiple needs can be satisfied by multiple parts. Is there a good deed you have been postponing because you thought you needed a 'single complete cause'? What step can you take today, even if imperfect, as a valid part of the cause?

Classification of Cause and Definition of Form
Al-Jurjani classifies cause and defines form with a detailed objection and resolution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Jurjani classifies causes into complete and incomplete, and defines form as the intrinsic cause by which a thing is actual.

Core Idea
The form is the proximate cause of a thing's actuality, but it may require matter.

In this passage, Al-Jurjani lays out the foundational classification of causes in the context of the effect's need. He distinguishes between complete and incomplete causes, and within incomplete causes, he identifies the formal cause as the intrinsic part that makes the thing actual. This lesson explores that classification and the nuanced definition of form, including a key objection and its resolution.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once with the translation. Notice the classification structure and the definition of form. The passage is dense, so take your time.
The OCR has some ellipsis; the meaning is clear in context.
2
Identify the cause classification
Find the two main divisions: complete cause and incomplete cause. Then note that incomplete cause is further divided into intrinsic (part of the effect) and extrinsic.
The intrinsic part that makes the thing actual is called 'form'.
3
Understand the definition of form
Focus on the phrase 'if the thing is actual by it' and how it restricts form to being the proximate cause of actuality. See how the commentator explains this with the example of the bed's configuration.
The preposition 'bi' is interpreted as proximate causality.
4
Examine the objection and reply
Read the objection about the sword form existing in wood without the sword being actual. Understand why this does not refute the definition, as per the commentator's reply.
The reply refers to the specific sword-form requiring iron matter.
5
Resolve the parts-of-form issue
Notice how the commentator handles the problem of the last part of a composite form being excluded from both form and matter definitions, by distinguishing immediate from secondary need.
This preserves the classification's coherence.

المرصد الخامس - المقصد الأول : تصور احتياج الشيء إلى غيره ضروري الشيء ( المحتاج) يسمى (معلولاً، والعلة إما تامة كما سيأتي وإما ناقصة. والناقصة (إما جزء الشيء) الذي هو المعلول ( أو ) أمر ( خارج عنه ، والأول إن كان به الشيء بالفعل كالهيئة للسرير فهو (الصورة) لا يقال صورة السيف قد تحصل في الخشب، مع أن السيف ليس حاصلاً بالفعل؛ لأنا نقول : الصورة السيفية المعينة إذا . . . . قوله : ( إما تامة كما سيأتي ، أو ناقصة) يعني أن القسمة الأولى متروكة في الذكر اختصاراً، بقرينة قوله : ويسمى جميع ما يحتاج إليه الشيء علة تامة والكلام في أن العلة التامة إذا كانت مشتملة على المادة والصورة يصدق عليه تعريف العلة بمعنى المحتاج إليه أو لا تحقيقه. سيجيء قوله : ( إن كان به الشيء بالفعل الباء للملابسة أي ما يقارن لوجود وجود الشيء بمعنى أن لا يتوقف بعد وجوده على شيء آخر، فخرج مادة الأفلاك، وأجزاء الجزء الصوري لمادة المركب كصور الخشب للسرير فإنها أجزاء مادية بالنسبة إلى المركب وحمل الباء على السببية القريبة مع عدم صحته في ما به الشيء بالقوة يحتاج إلى القول بأن العلة التامة والفاعل سببان بعيدان بواسطة الصورة . قوله : ( لا يقال : إلخ) ليس مراده النقض بالصورة النوعية للسيف الحاصلة في الخشب، قوله : ( والعلة إما جزء الشيء المقسم في عبارة المتن هو العلة الناقصة، كما أشار إليه الشارح. ولا يرد مجموع المادة والصورة؛ لما عرفت من أنه معلول لا علة، ولو سلم فالوحدة النوعية باعتبار العلية معتبرة في المقسم قوله : ( والأول إن كان به الشيء بالفعل الباء للسببية القريبة، وتقديم الجار والمجرور للحصر، فالمستفاد منه أن الصورة هي السبب القريب لحصول الشيء بالفعل البتة، حتى لو جاز وجودها بدون المادة لكان مستلزما لحصول المركب بالفعل ،البتة، فيخرج المادة التي يلازمها الصورة، كالمادة الفلكية؛ فإن وجود الفلك وإن كان معها بالفعل، لكن لا بها. ويخرج أيضاً كل من جزئي الصورة المركبة إذا ثبت أما جزؤها الأول فظاهر وأما جزؤها الثاني؛ فلأن لجزئها الأول مدخلاً قريباً في وجوب حصول المركب بالفعل، وقد اعتبرنا الحصر، فإن قلت: إذا خرج من تعريف الصورة جزؤها الأخير، ولا شك في عدم دخوله في تعريف المادة، مع دخوله في المقسم، بطل الانحصار قلت : المقسم علة الشيء بلا واسطة أعني المحتاج إليه أولا وبالذات والمعلول إنما يحتاج أولاً وبالذات إلى كل من المادة ،والصورة وأما الاحتياج إلى جزئها، فإنما ثانيا وبالعرض، وبهذا التقدير يظهر اندفاع الاعتراض بصدق تعريف المادة على غير الأخير من أجزاء الصورة؛ وذلك لأن ما عبارة عن العلة بلا واسطة ويظهر أيضاً جواز إخراج كل من جزئي الصورة بهذا الطريق أيضاً، هذا غاية توجيه المقام، وإن اشتمل على نوع تكلف لتصحيح الكلام، مع أنه بعد محل الملام قوله: (لأنا نقول الصورة السيفية (المعينة أي تعيناً نوعياً باعتبار حلولها في المادة هو الحديدية والمراد بحصولها بشخصها حصول شخص منها .
The fifth observation - first goal: The conception of a thing's need for another is necessary. The thing that is needed is called an effect, and the cause is either complete, as will come, or incomplete. And the incomplete is either a part of the thing which is the effect, or an external matter. The first, if the thing is actual by it, like the configuration for the bed, then it is the form. It should not be said that the form of a sword may exist in wood while the sword is not actual; for we say: The specific sword-form... His saying: 'Either complete, as will come, or incomplete' means that the first division is left unmentioned for brevity, by the indication of his saying: 'And all that a thing needs is called a complete cause.' And the discussion is whether the complete cause, when it includes matter and form, applies to the definition of cause in the sense of that which is needed or not; its verification will come. His saying: 'If the thing is actual by it' – the preposition 'bi' is for accompaniment, i.e., that which accompanies the existence of the thing's existence in the sense that it does not depend after its existence on anything else. Thus the matter of the celestial spheres and the parts of the formal part of the matter of the composite, like the forms of wood for the bed, are excluded, for they are material parts relative to the composite. And interpreting 'bi' as proximate causality, while it is incorrect in what the thing is potential, requires saying that the complete cause and the agent are two remote causes through the form. His saying: 'It should not be said: ...etc.' – he does not mean a refutation by the specific form of the sword existing in wood. His saying: 'And the cause is either a part of the thing' – the divided term in the text is the incomplete cause, as the commentator indicated. And the aggregate of matter and form is not included, because you know it is an effect, not a cause. Even if conceded, the specific unity with respect to causality is considered in the divided term. His saying: 'And the first, if the thing is actual by it' – the preposition 'bi' is for proximate causality, and the fronting of the prepositional phrase is for restriction. So what is derived from it is that the form is the proximate cause of the thing's actuality absolutely, even if its existence without matter were possible, it would necessitate the actuality of the composite absolutely. Thus, the matter that accompanies the form, like celestial matter, is excluded; for the existence of the celestial sphere, although actual with it, is not by it. It also excludes each of the two parts of a composite form if established. As for its first part, it is obvious; as for its second, because its first part has a proximate role in the necessity of the composite's actuality, and we have considered restriction. If you say: If the last part of the form is excluded from the definition of form, and there is no doubt that it is not included in the definition of matter, while it is included in the divided term, then the division fails. I say: The divided term is the cause of the thing without mediation, i.e., that which is needed firstly and essentially. And the effect only needs firstly and essentially each of matter and form. As for the need for its parts, it is only secondarily and accidentally. By this estimation, the objection disappears that the definition of matter applies to other than the last parts of the form; because 'what' expresses the cause without mediation. It also appears that it is permissible to exclude each of the two parts of the form by this method. This is the ultimate direction of the passage, although it involves some laboriousness to correct the speech, though it is still a place of blame. His saying: 'For we say: The specific sword-form' – i.e., specific in kind by virtue of its indwelling in the iron matter, and by 'its existence in person' is meant the existence of an individual of it.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage classifies causes into complete and incomplete. The incomplete cause is either intrinsic (a part of the effect) or extrinsic. The intrinsic part, if it makes the thing actual, is called the form. The commentator then addresses a potential objection: the form of a sword can exist in wood without the sword being actual, so form does not guarantee actuality. He responds by referring to the specific sword-form. He then explains the classification further: the first division (complete vs. incomplete) is mentioned for brevity. He discusses whether a complete cause including matter and form fits the definition of cause as that which is needed. He then explains the phrase 'if the thing is actual by it' in detail: it means the form is the proximate cause of actuality, excluding matter that is merely concomitant. He also analyzes the parts of a composite form and resolves an objection about the last part of the form being excluded from both form and matter definitions, by clarifying the divided term is the immediate cause, and the effect's need is primarily for matter and form as wholes, not their parts.

Key Terms
علة تامة
علة ناقصة
صورة
مادة
معلول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That form alone always makes a thing actual, regardless of matter.
Correction: The form is the proximate cause of actuality but requires the appropriate matter; the example of sword form in wood is not a valid counterexample because the specific form is tied to iron matter.

Checkpoint
What is the definition of 'form' according to this passage?

B) That by which a thing is actual, like the configuration of a bed
C) The external cause of a thing
D) The complete cause of a thing

Key Takeaway
Form is the intrinsic incomplete cause that makes a thing actual, but it is not sufficient without matter; it is the proximate cause of actuality.

How will you remind yourself of this dependence daily?
?
The commentator acknowledges the explanation has 'some laboriousness'. This reflects the depth and precision required to understand matters of causality. Are you willing to exert similar effort to understand your faith deeply, or are you content with superficial understanding? What can you do today to dive deeper into one aspect of your aqeedah?

Definition of Material Cause and Its Various Names
The author defines material cause and enumerates its six names with explanations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of material cause, its scope including accidents, and its six alternative names with their specific considerations.

Core Idea
Material cause is the potential substrate that underlies change, with multiple names reflecting different perspectives.

In this passage, the author lays out the foundational concept of material cause, essential for understanding causality in Islamic philosophy and kalam. The definition is given with a concrete example, then the scope is extended beyond substances to accidents. Finally, six names are listed, each highlighting a unique aspect of material cause. This lesson will walk you through the definition, clarification, and the rich terminology associated with matter.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Let the author's own flow come through before you interpret.
Notice the use of the example (wood for a bed).
2
Understand the definition
Identify the definition: material cause is that in which a thing exists potentially. The example of wood for a bed illustrates this.
3
Note the expansion to accidents
The author clarifies that material cause is not limited to substances but also applies to accidents and their parts.
This broadens the typical understanding.
4
List the six names and their considerations
Go through each name: مادة (matter), طينة (clay), قابل (receptive), هيولى (hyle), عنصر (element), أسطقس (element). For each, understand the specific consideration mentioned.
The names عنصر and أسطقس can be reversed in interpretation.
5
Reflect on the commentator's note
The commentator explains that these names are applied to substantial matters, not accidental matters, to avoid objection.
This resolves a potential inconsistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كان الشيء به بالقوة، كالخشب له (أي للسرير) فهو المادة. وليس المراد بالعلة الصورية والمادة ما يختص بالجواهر من المادة والصورة الجوهريتين بل ما يعمهما وغيرهما من أجزاء الأعراض التي توجد بها الأعراض، إما بالفعل أو بالقوة. ولها (أي للمادة) أسماء متعددة، باعتبارات مختلفة: فمادة وطينة؛ إذ تتوارد عليها الصور المختلفة، وقابل وهيولى (من جهة استعدادها للصور)، وعنصر إذ منها يبتدأ التركيب، وأسطقس إذ إليها ينتهي التحليل. وقد يعكس ويفسر كل من العنصر والأسطقس بتفسير الآخر.

قوله: (ولها أسماء) أي يطلق على المادة هذه الأسماء، ولو باعتبار بعض أفرادها، وهي المواد الجوهرية، فلا يرد أن العلة المادية للأعراض لا يطلق عليها هذه الأسماء.
And if the thing is in potentiality by it, like the wood for it (i.e., for the bed), then it is the matter. And the intended meaning by formal cause and matter is not what is specific to substances from the matter and substantial form, but rather what encompasses them and other parts of accidents by which accidents exist, either actually or potentially. And for it (i.e., for matter) there are multiple names, according to different considerations: so matter (madda) and clay (tina) because different forms succeed upon it; and receptive (qabil) and hyle (hayula) from the aspect of its preparedness for forms; and element ('unsur) because from it composition begins; and element (ustuquss) because to it analysis ends. And sometimes each of 'unsur and ustuquss is reversed and interpreted with the interpretation of the other.

He said: (and for it names) meaning that these names are applied to matter, even if only with respect to some of its individuals, which are substantial matters, so it is not objected that the material cause of accidents is not called by these names.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage defines the material cause and enumerates its various names. The author begins by defining material cause as that in which a thing exists potentially, using the example of wood for a bed. He then clarifies that the terms 'formal cause' and 'matter' as used here are not restricted to substances and their substantial form and matter, but also include accidents and their parts. Thus, the material cause can be for accidents as well. The passage then lists six names for material cause, each reflecting a different consideration: madda (matter), tina (clay) for its receptivity to successive forms; qabil (receptive) and hayula (hyle) for its preparedness; 'unsur (element) for it being the starting point of composition; and ustuquss (element) for it being the endpoint of analysis. The commentator notes that these names are applied specifically to substantial matters, not to accidental matters, so no objection arises from that.

Internal and External Causes: Quiddity vs. Existence
The author classifies causes into internal (material & formal) and external (efficient & final) and distinguishes their roles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four causes, their classification into internal (material & formal) and external (efficient & final), and why the former are called causes of quiddity while the latter are causes of existence.

Core Idea
Causes of quiddity constitute essence; causes of existence bring it into being.

In this passage, the author systematically classifies the four causes into two pairs: those that are internal to the effect (matter and form) and those that are external (agent and end). This distinction is not merely taxonomic; it reveals different roles in causation—one pair constitutes the whatness, the other brings it into actuality. Let's explore how and why.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Then read the translation. Notice the parentheses indicating commentary inserted into the original text.
The passage uses a compressed style typical of kalam texts.
2
Identify the two main classifications
The author first introduces internal causes (material & formal) as causes of quiddity, then external causes (efficient & final) as causes of existence. Mark where each pair is defined.
3
Understand the naming rationale
Ask why the first pair is specifically called 'causes of quiddity', and the second pair 'causes of existence'. The passage explains: the first pair is inside the quiddity, the second pair is outside and brings existence.
Note that both pairs are actually causes of existence, but the names highlight their primary role.
4
Note the special conditions
The passage ends with two additional remarks: material and formal causes exist only for composite things; final causes exist only for voluntary agents. Reflect on why these restrictions matter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(وهاتان) أي الصورة والمادة (علتان للماهية) داخلتان في قوامها كما أنها علتان للوجود أيضاً، لتوقفه عليهما. (فيخصان باسم علة الماهية تمييزاً لهما عن الباقيتين المشاركتين إياهما في علية الوجود. (والثاني): أعني ما يكون خارجاً عن المعلول، (إما ما به الشيء كالنجار) له أي للسرير وهو الفاعل والمؤثر (وإما ما لأجله الشيء كالجلوس عليه) له وهو الغاية أي العلة الغائية. (وهاتان) العلتان أعني الفاعل والغاية يخصان باسم علة الوجود؛ لتوقفه عليهما دون الماهية. (والأوليان) وهما المادة والصورة لا توجدان إلا للمركب وهو ظاهر. (والغاية) لا تكون إلا لفاعل بالاختيار.
(And these two) i.e., form and matter, (are two causes of the quiddity) entering into its constitution, just as they are also two causes of existence, because it depends on them. (So they are specified with the name 'cause of quiddity' to distinguish them from the other two that share with them in causality of existence. (And the second) i.e., what is outside the effect, (either that by which the thing exists like the carpenter) for it i.e., for the bed, and he is the agent and effecter, (or that for which the thing exists like sitting upon it) for it, and it is the end, i.e., the final cause. (And these two) causes, i.e., the agent and the end, are specified with the name 'cause of existence' because existence depends on them and not the quiddity. (And the first two) i.e., matter and form, exist only for the composite, and that is apparent. (And the end) exists only for a voluntary agent.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage classifies the four causes into two categories: internal causes (material and formal) and external causes (efficient and final). It explains that material and formal causes are causes of the quiddity (mahiyya) because they constitute the essence of the thing, and they are also causes of existence because the thing's existence depends on them. However, they are specifically called 'causes of quiddity' to distinguish them from the other two. The efficient cause (al-fa'il) and final cause (al-ghaya) are external to the effect; the efficient cause is that by which the thing exists, exemplified by the carpenter for the bed, and the final cause is that for which the thing exists, exemplified by sitting on the bed. These two are specifically called 'causes of existence' because existence depends on them, not the quiddity. The passage also notes that material and formal causes exist only for composites, and the final cause applies only to voluntary agents (i.e., agents with will).

Key Terms
علة الماهية
علة الوجود
العلة الصورية
العلة المادية
العلة الفاعلة
العلة الغائية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that efficient and final causes are also part of the quiddity or essence of the thing.
Correction: The author explicitly states that efficient and final causes are outside the effect (خارج عن المعلول), thus they are not internal to the quiddity. Only material and formal causes constitute the quiddity.

Checkpoint

C) Material and formal
D) Formal and final

Key Takeaway
The four causes are divided into internal (material & formal), which constitute the quiddity, and external (efficient & final), which bring about existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

But have you ever defined yourself by external causes—your job, your achievements, or others' expectations—rather than by your essential nature as a servant of Allah? How can you shift your self-understanding from external to internal causes?
