لا أولاً وبالذات .
The fifth observatory - the first objective: The conception of a thing's need for another is necessary. (If it is said: You have left out a division) of the incomplete cause, which is the condition, for it is among the things that a thing needs for its existence, and it is also a part of the complete cause, so the external cause is not limited to the agent and the end. We say: It is a part (of the agent in reality; because what is meant by the agent is the one independent in agency and effect, and it is not so) from his statement: (the condition) i.e., that on which a thing depends in its existence and is not among the mentioned divisions, so addressing the removal of the obstacle in addition to the answer is a preparation for the question and answer to come. His statement: (for it is among what it needs etc.) the first is to establish the principle of causality and the second to establish that it is incomplete. His statement: (It is a part of the agent in reality) is attached to 'part', i.e., a part in reality even if it is not an apparent part, or to 'agent', i.e., a part of what is actually an agent, for the agent in reality is what is characterized by actual agency, and as for the essence of the agent, it is its nature to act. His statement: (He is the independent in agency, whether he is independent by himself, or by the entry of another thing.) So what is meant by 'that by which the thing [acts]' is what is independent in causality and effect, as is understood, whether by itself or by the addition of something to it. So the mention of this division includes the mention of three things: the independent agent by himself, and the essence of the agent, according to the conceptual consideration in the chapter of definition. And it should be known that his statement 'unlike in the chapter of definition' is not an explanation of the author's words but is a digression that occurred during the explanation of the conclusion. Otherwise, the author has mentioned previously that the meaning of the priority of the definition over the defined is the priority of each part of its parts over it, not that the definition itself is prior to the defined by the conceptual distinction of generality and specificity, even if the judge al-Urmawi said so, so let it be considered. His statement: (And also a part of the complete cause) this is an emphasis on his statement that it is among the things that a thing needs in its existence. It is said: you may take it as an establishment based on the fact that the word 'also' indicates that it is like other parts, no degradation to it so that it is not considered and not counted as a cause, and you are aware that the comparison indicates degradation in the compared. His statement: (because what is meant by the agent is the independent in agency) It is said: this is not useful because the questioner's aim is that the condition itself, for example, is included in the divided [category]; because the effect needs it, and it cannot be predicated that it is a part of the effect nor that from which nor that for which, and there is no meaning to lack of division except that there exists something to which the divided applies and none of the divisions applies, and it does not benefit that it is a part of one of the divisions. And it was answered: that his intention is that the condition, for example, is a part of the agent, so the need for it is secondary and accidental, i.e., through the need of the independent agent for it. And the divided, as we indicated, is that which is needed first and essentially, namely the actual receiver and the independent agent, so there is no harm in the condition itself being excluded from the divisions. But something remains: it was necessary that the final cause not be mentioned then, because they stated that it influences the influence of the agent, not the existence of the effect, so the need for it is through the need of the actual agent for it, not first and essentially.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. The objector claims that the condition (شرط) has been omitted from the division of causes (agent, end, matter, form). The condition is also a part of the complete cause, so the external cause is not limited to agent and end. The defender (commentator) answers that the condition is a part of the agent in reality, because the agent is defined as what is independent in agency and effect. The condition is not independent but contributes to the agent's efficacy. The commentary then explains the definitions: the condition is what a thing depends on for its existence but is not among the mentioned divisions (matter, form, agent, end). The defender states that the condition is part of the agent in reality, meaning it is a real part even if not apparent. Further, the independent agent may be independent by itself or with the addition of another thing, so the mention of the division includes three things: the independent agent by itself, the essence of the agent, etc. There is a digression about the definition chapter. The objection is refined: the condition is needed by the effect, but it does not fit into any of the four causes. The answer clarifies that the need for the condition is secondary, through the agent's need for it. The divided category is what is needed first and essentially (the actual receiver and the independent agent). Finally, a lingering issue is raised: if this logic is applied, the final cause should not be mentioned because it influences the agent, not the effect directly. The passage thus defends the classification of causes by subsuming the condition under the agent.

Correction: The condition is not an independent cause; it is part of the agent's agency.

The Condition is Part of the Efficient Cause
Objection and answer about the condition as part of the efficient cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the condition (shart) is not considered a separate incomplete cause but is subsumed under the efficient cause in Islamic causal theory.

Core Idea
Conditions are not independent causes; they belong to the efficient cause.

In the classification of external causes, the efficient, material, formal, and final causes are typically enumerated. But where does the condition fit? Theologians argued that conditions are necessary for the efficient cause to act, so they are part of it. This lesson examines the objection that conditions were omitted and the answer that they are included within the efficient cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Start by reading the objection: the condition is a kind of incomplete cause that has been left out of the division.
Notice the structure: 'If it is said ... we say'.
2
Understand the answer
The answer is that the condition is part of the efficient cause in reality, because the efficient cause is defined as that which is independent in effecting, and it is not independent except with conditions.
The key term is 'mustaqill bi-al-fa'iliyyah' (independent in effecting).
3
Grasp the definition of efficient cause
The efficient cause is that which is independent in causation and influence, whether by itself or with the inclusion of another matter (like conditions).
This broadens the concept of efficient cause.
4
Follow the further objection and response
A further objection argues that this answer does not satisfy because the condition itself falls under the division (muqsam) but not under any of the enumerated categories. The response is that the condition is part of the efficient cause, so the need for it is secondary, through the need of the independent efficient cause.
This refines the relationship: primary need is for efficient cause; secondary need for conditions.
5
Reflect on the remaining issue
The passage ends by noting a potential asymmetry with the final cause, which also influences only through the efficient cause, yet it is listed as a separate division. This is left as a point for further thought.
Not all theologians resolved this consistently.

If it is said: You have left out a part of the incomplete cause, which is the condition (al-shart), for it is among the things that a thing needs in its existence, and also a part of the complete cause (al-illah al-tammah). So the external cause is not limited to the efficient cause (al-fa'il) and the final cause (al-ghayah). And the division, as we have indicated, is that which is needed primarily and essentially, which is the material cause (al-qabil bi-al-fi'l) and the independent efficient cause. Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer regarding the classification of external causes. The objection states that the condition (al-shart) has been omitted from the division of incomplete causes, since it is something on which the existence of a thing depends; therefore the external cause is not limited to the efficient and final causes. The answer is that the condition is in reality a part of the efficient cause, because what is meant by 'efficient cause' is that which is independent in effecting (al-mustaqill bi-al-fa'iliyyah), and it does not attain independence except with the gathering of conditions and removal of obstacles. The commentary then explains: the condition is a part of the efficient cause in reality, even if not apparently; the 'independent in efficacy' may be independent by itself or by the inclusion of another matter; thus the mention of the efficient cause includes three things: the efficient cause independent by itself, the essence of the efficient cause, and what is added to it (conditions, instruments, etc.). A further objection is raised: this answer is insufficient because the questioner's point is that the condition falls under the division (muqsam) but is not one of the enumerated divisions (efficient, material, final, formal). The response is that the condition is a part of the efficient cause, so the need for it is secondary, through the need of the independent efficient cause. The division (what is needed primarily) is the material cause (qabil bi-al-fi'l) and the independent efficient cause, so there is no harm in the condition not being a separate division. However, a remaining issue is that the final cause also influences only by influencing the efficient cause, so it too is needed secondarily, yet it was mentioned as a separate division; this asymmetry is noted but not resolved here.

Correction: The condition is not a separate cause; it is part of the efficient cause, since the efficient cause is defined as independent in effecting only when conditions are fulfilled.

The Removal of Obstacles as a Condition for the Efficient Cause
Objection that removal of obstacles cannot be a cause, answered as a condition for the efficient cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the removal of obstacles is a necessary condition for the efficient cause, not a positive cause itself.

Core Idea
The absence of an obstacle does not cause existence; it allows the efficient cause to act.

This passage tackles a subtle objection: how can non-existence (removal of an obstacle) be considered a part of causality? The author clarifies that the removal of obstacles is a condition, not a cause, and is part of the completion of the efficient or material cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without overanalyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the flow: initial statement, objection, answer, then analogy.
The Arabic contains some parenthetical marks; focus on the overall argument.
2
Identify the objection
Find the 'if you say' part. The objection claims that if removal of obstacles is a cause, then non-existence would be part of existence, which is impossible.
3
Understand the answer
The answer explains that removal of obstacles is a condition (shart) that the efficient cause needs, not a positive cause. It is part of the completion (tatimmah) of the cause.
The commentator emphasizes that conditions are incomplete causes.
4
Apply to the material cause
See how the same logic applies to the material cause: the potential (qabil) becomes actually potential only when conditions are met and obstacles removed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إلا باستجماع الشرائط، وارتفاع الموانع ) ، فوجود الشرط وعدم المانع من تتمة الفاعل فلا حاجة إلى الإفراد بالذكر، وقد يجعلان من تتمة المادة لأن القابل إنما يكون قابلاً بالفعل عند حصول الشرائط، وارتفاع الموانع. ومنهم من جعل الأدوات من تتمة الفاعل، وما عداها من تتمة المادة. فإن قلت : لما جعل ارتفاع الموانع جزءاً للفاعل علة أو القابل، بل إذا جعل مما يحتاج إليه الشيء في وجوده، فعدم المانع جزء من الوجود، وأنه خلاف الضرورة ( الشاهدة بأن العدم لا يكون كذلك . ( قلنا : عدم المانع والشرائط، وعلى أن كلاً منها مما يحتاج إليه المعلول، وعلى أنها ناقصة. إنما المتروك تفصيله وبيان اشتماله على الأمور الثلاثة، وقس على هذا التقرير في جانب المادة: بأن المادة هو القابل، والقابل لا يكون قابلاً بالفعل إلا بحصول الشرائط، فالمراد بما به الشيء بالقوة الجزء الذي يكون به الشيء قابلاً له بالفعل، سواء كان بنفسه أو بانضمام أمر آخر إليه، فيكون ذكر هذا ذكراً للأمور الثلاثة، إنما المتروك التفصيل.
Except with the gathering of conditions and the removal of obstacles. So the existence of the condition and the absence of the obstacle are part of the completion of the efficient cause, so there is no need to mention them separately. And they may be made part of the completion of the material cause, because the potential (al-qabil) is only actually potential when the conditions are obtained and the obstacles removed. Some make the instruments part of the completion of the efficient cause, and what is other than them part of the completion of the material cause. If you say: When you made the removal of obstacles a part of the efficient cause as a cause or of the potential, but if you make it among what the thing needs in its existence, then the absence of the obstacle is a part of existence, and that contradicts the necessity that testifies that non-existence (al-'adam) is not like that. We say: The absence of the obstacle and the conditions [are conditions], and that each of them is something the effect needs, and that they are incomplete. Only their detail and the exposition of their inclusion of the three matters is omitted. And analogize this account regarding the material side: that the material (al-maddah) is the potential (al-qabil), and the potential is only actually potential with the obtaining of conditions. So what is meant by 'that by which the thing is in potentiality' is the part by which the thing is actually potential for it, whether by itself or by the addition of another matter. So this mention is a mention of the three matters; only detail is omitted.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer concerning the removal of obstacles (raf' al-mani') in the classification of causes. The initial statement asserts that the existence of the condition and the absence of the obstacle are part of the completion (tatimmah) of the efficient cause, so need not be listed separately; alternatively, they may be part of the completion of the material cause, since the material (qabil) becomes actually receptive only upon conditions and removal of obstacles. The objection then states: if you consider the removal of obstacles a part of the efficient cause or the material cause, but you also consider it something the thing needs in its existence, then the absence of the obstacle would be a part of existence, which contradicts the necessary truth that non-existence cannot be a cause. The answer is: the absence of the obstacle and the conditions are themselves things the effect needs, and they are incomplete causes; only the detailed exposition of how they include the three matters (efficient, material, final) is omitted. The same reasoning applies on the material side: the matter (al-maddah) is the potential recipient (al-qabil), and it becomes actually potential only with the obtaining of conditions. Therefore, what is meant by 'that by which the thing is in potentiality' is the part that makes the thing actually potential, whether by itself or with something else. So this mention includes the three matters; only detail is omitted.

مادة

تتمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The removal of an obstacle (a non-existence) cannot be involved in causality at all because it is nothing.
Correction: The removal of an obstacle is not a positive cause but a necessary condition for the efficient cause to act. It is part of the completion (tatimmah) of the cause.

Checkpoint
How does the answer address the objection that making the removal of an obstacle a cause contradicts the fact that non-existence cannot be a cause?
A) By denying that the removal of obstacles is a cause.
B) By explaining that the removal of obstacles is a condition (shart) that the efficient cause needs, not a positive cause.
C) By stating that non-existence can indeed be a cause.
D) By transferring the removal of obstacles to the material cause only.

Key Takeaway
The removal of obstacles is not a positive cause but a necessary condition for the efficient cause to bring about the effect.

?
The passage distinguishes between a condition and a cause. In your relationship with Allah, are there 'conditions' you focus on (e.g., avoiding major sins) while forgetting the efficient cause—sincere tawheed and reliance on Him? What practical change does this demand from you starting now?

Causes by Intermediary: Resolving an Objection on the Four Causes
Objection and resolution about conditions and obstacles as causes by intermediary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how conditions, instruments, and removal of obstacles are classified as causes by intermediary, preserving the exhaustiveness of the four causes.

Core Idea
Conditions are not separate causes but secondary causes by intermediary.

In the previous discussion, efficient and material causes were defined as independent efficient cause and actually potential material cause. But what about conditions, instruments, and removal of obstacles? They are needed for an effect, yet they don't fit as part, source, or purpose. This objection threatens the exhaustiveness of the four-cause classification. The resolution introduces the concept of 'cause by intermediary' (علة بالواسطة). Let's explore this crucial refinement.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objector admits the definitions of efficient and material causes but argues that conditions, instruments, etc., are needed yet not part of the effect, nor efficient, material, or final. This would make the division inexhaustive.
Notice how the objector frames the problem: something needed but not fitting any of the four types.
2
Understand the resolution: causes by intermediary
The resolution states that the effect primarily needs the independent efficient cause and the actual potential material cause. It needs the mentioned items only through those primary causes. Thus they are causes by intermediary (علل بالواسطة).
This preserves the division: the primary causes are the direct (without intermediary) causes, and the others are secondary.
3
Note the response about the final cause
A response points out that the final cause is a cause of the causality of the efficient cause, so it would also be by intermediary. This is a subtle point that may require further discussion.
This does not invalidate the resolution but shows the concept applies to all secondary causes.
4
Reflect on how this preserves the classification
By distinguishing between direct causes (efficient, material, formal, final) and indirect causes (by intermediary), the exhaustiveness of the four-cause division is maintained.
The divisional term (مقسم) refers to the cause without intermediary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبما ذكرنا اندفع ما قيل : سلمنا أن المراد بالفاعل هو المستقل بالفاعلية والمادة هو القابل بالفعل، لكن كل ما ذكرناه من الشرائط، والآلات، ورفع المانع، والمعد مما يحتاج إليه المعلول، ولا يصدق عليه أنه جزء منه ولا ما منه ولا ما لأجله، ولا نعني بعدم الحصر في الأقسام إلا وجود شيء يصدق عليه المقسم، ولا يصدق عليه شيء من الأقسام. وقد يقال : في توجيهه : بأن المراد أن المعلول يحتاج أولاً إلى الفاعل المستقل والقابل بالفعل، واحتياجه إلى ما ذكر إنما هو بواسطة احتياجهما إليه، فتكون تلك الأمور من العلل بالواسطة، والمقسم هو علة الشيء بلا واسطة ورد : بأنه يخرج عن قسمة العلة الغائية؛ لأنها علة العلية الفاعل، فتكون علة بالواسطة . قوله : (لما جعل إلخ ) أشار بتقدير الشرط إلى أن الفاء في قوله : فعدم المانع للدلالة على أن منشأ السؤال ما تقدم كما أن مورده ذلك، وأن هذا السؤال لا تعقل له بالجواب عن بطلان الحصر؛ لأن اعترافه بأن رفع المانع ليس مما يحتاج إليه اعتراف بعدم بطلان الحصر به قوله : ( وأنه خلاف الضرورة إلخ ) فإنا إذا علمنا وجود حادث طلبنا بالبديهة علته، بل هذا مركوز في طبائع الحيوانات العجم . قوله: (أي باستجماع الشرائط، وارتفاع الموانع ارتفاع الموانع عند المصنف من قبيل الشرائط، ولذا اكتفى في السؤال بذكر الشرائط، وأفرد بالذكر ارتفاع الموانع هاهنا عطفاً للخاص على العام لخفاء أمره . بعيد قوله : ( وقد يجعلان من تتمة إلخ) لا شك أن جعل الأدوات من تتمة المادة فالأولى جعله من تتمة ،الفاعل، كما سنذكره إلا أن قوله : ومنهم من جعل إلخ ربما يشعر بترجيح الجعل الأول على الثاني . قوله : (فإن قلت إلخ) يمكن توجيه هذا السؤال بحيث يرجع إلى ما سيذكره الشارح بقوله : فإن قلت : جعل إلخ، وحينئذ لا يرد ما أشار إليه بقوله : تم التحقيق إلخ .
And by what we have mentioned, what was said is repelled: We grant that what is meant by the efficient cause is the independent in efficacy, and by the material is the actually potential, but all that we have mentioned of conditions, instruments, removal of obstacles, and preparers are among what the effect needs, and it is not true of them that they are a part of it, nor that from which, nor that for which. And we mean by the non-exhaustiveness of the divisions only the existence of something of which the division (al-muqsam) is true, and none of the divisions is true of it. And it may be said in its interpretation: That what is meant is that the effect needs first the independent efficient cause and the actually potential material cause; its need for what we mentioned is only through their need for them. So those matters are among the causes by intermediary (al-'ilal bi-al-wasitah), and the division (al-muqsam) is the cause of the thing without intermediary. It was responded: That this excludes the final cause from the division, because it is a cause of the causality of the efficient cause, so it is a cause by intermediary. His saying: (When you make, etc.) He indicated, by presupposing the condition, that the 'fa' in his saying 'so the absence of the obstacle' indicates that the origin of the question is what preceded, just as its import is that; and that this question cannot be rationally answered concerning the invalidity of the exhaustiveness, because his admission that the removal of the obstacle is not among what it needs is an admission of the non-invalidity of the exhaustiveness by it. His saying: (And that it contradicts necessity, etc.) For when we know the existence of an originated being, we demand by intuition its cause; indeed, this is ingrained in the natures of dumb animals. His saying: (i.e., with the gathering of conditions and the removal of obstacles. The removal of obstacles according to the author is of the type of conditions, so he sufficed in the question with mentioning conditions, and singled out the mention of removal of obstacles here as a conjunction of the specific to the general due to its subtlety. Remote. His saying: (And they may be made from the completion, etc.) There is no doubt that making the instruments from the completion of the material cause, the preferable is to make it from the completion of the efficient cause, as we will mention, except that his saying 'and some made, etc.' perhaps suggests a preference for the first making over the second. His saying: (If you say, etc.) It is possible to direct this question such that it refers to what the commentator (al-sharih) will mention in his saying 'if you say: you made, etc.', and in that case what he indicated by his saying 'the investigation is completed, etc.' is not refuted.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on causes, presenting a further objection and its resolution. The objection states: even if we accept that the efficient cause is independent and the material is actually potential, still the conditions, instruments, removal of obstacles, and preparers are things the effect needs, yet they are not part of the effect, nor efficient, nor material, nor final. Thus the classification of causes is incomplete. The proposed resolution is that the effect primarily needs the independent efficient cause and the actually potential material cause; its need for these other things is mediated through the need of those primary causes for them. Hence these are 'causes by intermediary' (al-'ilal bi-al-wasitah). The division (muqsam) is the cause without intermediary. A response is noted: this would exclude the final cause from the division, because the final cause is a cause of the causality of the efficient cause, so it too is a cause by intermediary. The rest of the passage is commentary on the wording of the base text, explaining phrases from the author: the 'fa' indicates the origin of the question; the admission that the removal of obstacles is not a need of the effect refutes the objection of non-exhaustiveness; the intuitive demand for a cause is ingrained even in animals. The commentator notes disagreements about classification: some make instruments part of the material cause, but the preferable view is to make them part of the efficient cause.

Objection and Answer on Non-Existence as Part of the Cause
Objection that non-existence cannot be part of the cause and its answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how non-existence (such as the removal of obstacles) can be considered a condition for an effect without turning non-existence into an existent thing.

Core Idea
Non-existence can be a condition but never a part of existence.

This passage presents a subtle objection: if we say the removal of an obstacle is part of the cause, then non-existence becomes part of existence, which seems impossible. The answer clarifies that such non-existence is merely a condition for the recipient's actuality, not an active part. Understanding this distinction is key to the broader theory of causality in Islamic thought.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully.
Read the first part of the passage where the objector argues that making 'removal of obstacles' part of the cause leads to absurdity.
Notice the phrase 'خلاف الضرورة' — violating necessity.
2
Understand the problem.
The problem is that non-existence (like absence of an obstacle) cannot be an existing part of the cause. Ask yourself: why would this be a problem?
Because non-existence by definition is not a thing.
3
Read the answer and its reasoning.
Read the answer starting with 'قلنا'. The author distinguishes between being a condition (something the effect needs) and being an active part.
Notice the terms 'عدم المانع والشرائط' and 'ناقصة'.
4
Apply to matter and recipient.
The passage extends the reasoning to matter and recipient, explaining that the recipient is only receptive when conditions are met.
This shows consistency across different causal contexts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت : لما جعل ارتفاع الموانع جزءاً للفاعل علة أو القابل، بل إذا جعل مما يحتاج إليه الشيء في وجوده، فعدم المانع جزء من الوجود، وأنه خلاف الضرورة ( الشاهدة بأن العدم لا يكون كذلك . ( قلنا : عدم المانع والشرائط، وعلى أن كلاً منها مما يحتاج إليه المعلول، وعلى أنها ناقصة. إنما المتروك تفصيله وبيان اشتماله على الأمور الثلاثة، وقس على هذا التقرير في جانب المادة: بأن المادة هو القابل، والقابل لا يكون قابلاً بالفعل إلا بحصول الشرائط، فالمراد بما به الشيء بالقوة الجزء الذي يكون به الشيء قابلاً له بالفعل، سواء كان بنفسه أو بانضمام أمر آخر إليه، فيكون ذكر هذا ذكراً للأمور الثلاثة، إنما المتروك التفصيل.
If you say: Why did you make the removal of obstacles part of the agent or the recipient? Rather, if you make it something that the thing needs in its existence, then non-existence of the obstacle is part of existence, and that contradicts the necessity (witnessing that non-existence is not like that). We say: Non-existence of the obstacle and conditions — and that each of them is something the effect needs, and that they are incomplete. Only the detail is omitted, and its indication that it includes the three things. And apply this explanation to the side of matter: that matter is the recipient, and the recipient is not actually receptive except by the obtainment of conditions. So what is meant by that by which the thing is in potentiality is the part by which the thing is actually receptive to it, whether by itself or by being joined with another thing. So this mention is a mention of the three things; only the detail is omitted.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage contains an objection-and-answer pair. The objector argues that if removal of obstacles is considered part of the cause (whether agent or matter), then non-existence becomes part of existence, which contradicts the evident necessity that non-existence is not like existence. The answer clarifies that the non-existence of the obstacle and the conditions are each things the effect needs, but they are incomplete; the full detail is omitted for brevity. The answer then explains that when we speak of matter as the recipient, it is only actually receptive when conditions are met. The phrase 'that by which the thing is in potentiality' refers to the part that makes the thing actually receptive, either by itself or with something else. This shows that the three things (agent, matter, and condition) are all included, but the detailed breakdown is left out.

Second Objection on the Division of Causes and Its Reply
Second objection that conditions/tools are not causes and the reply.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the second objection challenges the exhaustive division of causes and how the reply uses the distinction between immediate and mediate causes to defend it, while also revealing a nuance about the final cause.

Core Idea
Conditions and tools are mediate causes, not immediate parts, preserving the division.

This lesson examines a key objection to the traditional division of causes: that conditions, tools, and preparations are needed by an effect yet do not fall under any of the four causes (material, formal, efficient, final). The objector claims this makes the division incomplete. The reply clarifies that these are mediate causes, not immediate parts, and defends the division, though it raises a new issue about the final cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Pay attention to the flow of objection, proposed solution, and rejoinder.
The passage begins by stating the objection is already repelled by earlier arguments.
2
Identify the objector's argument
Find where the objector grants the definitions of agent and matter but argues that conditions, tools, etc. are needed yet not any part of the cause. Understand that he claims the division is not exhaustive.
The objector does not deny the need, but denies the categorization.
3
Understand the proposed solution (tawjih)
Notice the attempted defense: the effect first needs agent and matter directly; its need for conditions is mediated through them, making conditions mediate causes and the divided term (complete cause) immediate.
This would keep the division exhaustive if mediate causes are subsumed.
4
See why the solution is rejected
Read the rejoinder: the final cause is a cause of the agent's causality, making it a mediate cause as well. Thus the solution would exclude the final cause from the immediate division, which is unacceptable.
So the objection is not fully resolved by this tawjih; the earlier argument is relied upon.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبما ذكرنا اندفع ما قيل : سلمنا أن المراد بالفاعل هو المستقل بالفاعلية والمادة هو القابل بالفعل، لكن كل ما ذكرناه من الشرائط، والآلات، ورفع المانع، والمعد مما يحتاج إليه المعلول، ولا يصدق عليه أنه جزء منه ولا ما منه ولا ما لأجله، ولا نعني بعدم الحصر في الأقسام إلا وجود شيء يصدق عليه المقسم، ولا يصدق عليه شيء من الأقسام. وقد يقال : في توجيهه : بأن المراد أن المعلول يحتاج أولاً إلى الفاعل المستقل والقابل بالفعل، واحتياجه إلى ما ذكر إنما هو بواسطة احتياجهما إليه، فتكون تلك الأمور من العلل بالواسطة، والمقسم هو علة الشيء بلا واسطة ورد : بأنه يخرج عن قسمة العلة الغائية؛ لأنها علة العلية الفاعل، فتكون علة بالواسطة.
And by what we have mentioned, what was said is repelled: We accept that by 'agent' is meant the independent agent and by 'matter' is meant the actually receptive, but all that we have mentioned of conditions, tools, removal of obstacles, and preparation are things the effect needs, yet they are not truly called a part of it, nor that from which it is, nor that for the sake of which it is. And we do not mean by the lack of exhaustive division except that there exists something to which the divided term applies but to which none of the divisions apply. And it may be said in its direction: that the meaning is that the effect first needs the independent agent and the actually receptive, and its need for what was mentioned is only through its need for them, so those things are mediate causes, and the divided term is the immediate cause. And it is replied: that this excludes the final cause from the division because it is the cause of the agent's causality, so it is a mediate cause.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection and its refutation. The objector grants that the agent is the independent cause and matter is the actually receptive, but argues that conditions, tools, removal of obstacles, and preparation are needed by the effect yet are not any of the four causes (material, formal, efficient, final). The objector means that there is a division that is not exhaustive: something falls under the divided term but not under any division. The proposed solution is that the effect first needs the independent agent and actually receptive matter, and the need for the other things is mediated through these two, making them mediate causes, while the divided term (complete cause) is immediate. This solution is rejected because it would exclude the final cause, which is a cause of the agent's causality, making it also mediate. The passage thus defends the traditional division of causes.

Correction: They are mediate causes, not immediate parts. The passage shows they are needed and are considered causes in a mediated sense.

Key Takeaway
Conditions and tools are mediate causes, not immediate parts, preserving the exhaustive division of causes, though the final cause's mediate status requires careful handling.

Non-Existence Cannot Be a Source of Existence
Argument and verification on causal roles of non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why non-existence cannot be an efficient cause and how it can function as an indicator or condition in causal reasoning according to Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Non-existence cannot create; it can only reveal or condition.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif addresses a subtle point in causal theory: can non-existence have any role in bringing about existence? The author argues that non-existence, having no reality, cannot be a principle of existence, but it can serve as a revealer of existential conditions. He then provides a nuanced verification that the intellect permits non-existence as a condition but not as an efficient cause, categorizing three ways something can contribute to causation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the strong initial assertion: 'The non-existent has no realization...'
2
Identify the assertion about non-existence
The passage begins by denying that non-existence can be a principle of existence. What are the three characteristics it lacks?
It has no realization, no distinction, no subsistence.
3
Understand the examples of revealer
Examine the two examples: absence of a door and absence of a pillar. How does non-existence act as a 'kāshif' (revealer) of an existential condition?
The absence reveals something about existence; it does not cause it.
4
Analyze the threefold categorization
The verification section presents three ways something can have input in existence: through existence only, non-existence only, or both. Identify each type and its example.
Agent/condition/matter/form (existence only), obstacle (non-existence only), prepared thing (both).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العدمي، لا تحقق له في نفس الأمر، ولا تميز له، ولا ثبوت فكيف يكون مبدأ لوجود الغير؟ نعم : إنه ) أي عدم المانع (قد يكون كاشفاً عن شرط وجودي، كعدم الباب المانع للدخول، فإنه ) أي عدم الباب ) كاشف عن وجود فضاء له قوام يمكن النفوذ فيه، وكعدم العمود المانع لسقوط السقف فإنه كاشف عن وجود مسافة يمكن تحرك السقف فيه، أي في الأمر الممتد الذي هو المسافة ( للسقوط، إلا أنه ربما لا يعلم ) الشرط الوجودي المعتبر في علة الوجود ) إلا بلازم عدمي، فيعبر عنه بذلك ) اللازم كما في المثالين المذكورين ( فيسبق إلى الأوهام أنه ) أي ذلك العدمي (مؤثر) في الوجود، ومعتبر في علته وليس كذلك. فظهر أن الأمور الداخلة في في العلة التامة كلها وجودية فتكون هي أيضاً موجودة بوجود أجزائها بأسرها. ثم التحقيق: أن بديهة العقل لا تجوز كون العدم مؤثراً في الوجود ، مفيداً له . ولكن تجوز أن يتوقف التأثير في الوجود على أمر عدمي كما تجوز توقفه على أمر وجودي فعلى هذا جاز أن يكون مدخلية الشيء في وجود آخر من حيث وجوده فقط، كالفاعل، والشرط، والمادة ،والصورة، وأن يكون من حيث عدمه فقط كالمانع. وأن يكون من حيث وجوده وعدمه معاً كالمعد؛ إذ لا بد من عدمه الطارئ على وجوده.
The non-existent has no realization in reality, no distinction, and no subsistence; how then can it be a principle for the existence of another? Yes, it (i.e., non-existence of the obstacle) may be a revealer of an existential condition, like the absence of a door preventing entry, for it (i.e., the absence of the door) reveals the existence of a space having substance that can be penetrated, and like the absence of a pillar preventing the fall of the ceiling, for it reveals the existence of a distance in which the ceiling can move, i.e., in the extended thing which is the distance—for falling—except that perhaps one knows the existential condition (considered in the cause of existence) only through a non-existent necessary concomitant, so it is expressed by that concomitant, as in the two cited examples. So it leaps to minds that it (i.e., that non-existent) is effective in existence and considered in its cause, but it is not so. Thus it appears that the things entering into the complete cause are all existential, so it (the complete cause) is also existent by the existence of all its parts. Then the verification: that the intuitive intellect does not permit that non-existence be effective in existence, conferring it. But it permits that the effect in existence may depend on a non-existent matter, just as it may depend on an existent matter. On this basis, it is possible for a thing to have an input in the existence of another only from the aspect of its existence, like the agent, condition, matter, and form; and to have input only from the aspect of its non-existence, like the obstacle; and to have input from both its existence and non-existence together, like the prepared thing (al-mu'add), for the non-existence supervenient on its existence is necessary.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage is a sustained argument and clarification about the role of non-existence in causation. It begins by asserting that non-existence has no realization, distinction, or subsistence, so it cannot be a principle of existence. However, the non-existence of an obstacle can be a 'kāshif' (revealer) of an existential condition, as in the examples: the absence of a door reveals an open space, and the absence of a pillar reveals a distance for falling. Sometimes the existential condition is only known through a non-existent necessary concomitant, so it is expressed that way, leading to the mistaken impression that the non-existent is effective. The conclusion is that all parts of the complete cause are existential. Then the 'verification' states that pure intellect does not allow non-existence to be effective in bringing about existence, but it can be a condition for that effect. Thus, something can be a cause with respect to its existence only (agent, condition, matter, form), with respect to its non-existence only (obstacle), or with respect to both existence and non-existence together (the prepared thing), since its non-existence after existence is necessary. This refines the understanding of causality in kalam.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That non-existence (e.g., absence of an obstacle) can be an efficient cause that brings about existence.
Correction: Non-existence can only be a revealer of an existential condition or a condition for the effect, but it cannot be an effective cause. The passage clarifies that the intellect denies non-existence being effective, though it can be a condition.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Non-existence is never an efficient cause, but it can be a necessary condition for causality as a revealer or as an obstacle's absence.

?

?

How would you shift your efforts from removing the absence to seeking the existential condition (e.g., patience, knowledge, faith) that the absence only reveals?

Non-Existence as a Revealer, Not a Cause
Poses objection and answers it with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why non-existence cannot be a cause of existence and how it can only serve as a revealer of an existential condition.

Core Idea
Non-existence reveals, but does not cause, existence.

Is it possible for something that does not exist to bring something else into being? This might seem absurd, yet people often mistakenly think that the absence of an obstacle is a cause. In this passage, the author addresses this confusion by distinguishing between genuine causation and mere indication. You will see how non-existence can reveal an existential condition without being effective.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the opening objection: non-existence has no reality, distinction, or subsistence, so how can it be a principle for existence?
Notice the strong rhetorical question.
2
Understand the answer
The answer concedes that non-existence is not effective, but it may be a revealer (kāshif) of an existential condition.
This is the key distinction.
3
Examine the examples
Two examples are given: absence of a door reveals a space for entry; absence of a pillar reveals a distance for falling. See how the non-existential points to something existent.
4
Grasp the final clarification
The passage concludes that people mistakenly think the non-existential is effective because the existential condition is only known through its non-existential concomitant.
This is the common misunderstanding corrected.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الخامس - المقصد الأول : تصور احتياج الشيء إلى غيره ضروري العديمي، لا تحقق له في نفس الأمر، ولا تميز له، ولا ثبوت فكيف يكون مبدأ لوجود الغير؟ نعم : إنه (أي عدم المانع) قد يكون كاشفاً عن شرط وجودي، كعدم الباب المانع للدخول، فإنه (أي عدم الباب) كاشف عن وجود فضاء له قوام يمكن النفوذ فيه، وكعدم العمود المانع لسقوط السقف فإنه كاشف عن وجود مسافة يمكن تحرك السقف فيه، أي في الأمر الممتد الذي هو المسافة (للسقوط)، إلا أنه ربما لا يعلم (الشرط الوجودي المعتبر في علة الوجود) إلا بلازم عدمي، فيعبر عنه بذلك (اللازم) كما في المثالين المذكورين (فيسبق إلى الأوهام أنه (أي ذلك العدمي) مؤثر) في الوجود، ومعتبر في علته وليس كذلك.
The Fifth Observatory - First Objective: The conception of a thing's need for another is necessary. As for the non-existential, it has no reality in itself, no distinction, and no subsistence; so how can it be a principle for the existence of another? Yes: it (i.e., the absence of the obstacle) may be a revealer of an existential condition, like the absence of a door preventing entry, for it (i.e., the absence of the door) reveals the existence of a space with substance through which penetration is possible; and like the absence of a pillar preventing the fall of a ceiling, for it reveals the existence of a distance through which the ceiling can move, i.e., in the extended thing which is the distance (for falling). However, perhaps the existential condition considered in the cause of existence is not known except through a non-existential concomitant, so it is expressed by that (concomitant) as in the two mentioned examples. Thus it leaps to minds that it (i.e., that non-existential) is effective in existence and considered in its cause, but it is not so.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer regarding whether non-existence can be a cause of existence. The objection states that non-existence has no reality, distinction, or subsistence in itself, so it cannot be a principle (mabda') for the existence of another thing. The answer concedes that non-existence itself is not effective, but it can serve as a revealer (kāshif) of an existential condition. Two examples are given: the absence of a door reveals the existence of a space that allows entry, and the absence of a pillar reveals a distance that allows the ceiling to fall. In both cases, the non-existential (the absence) is not the actual cause but merely indicates an existential condition. Sometimes the existential condition is unknown except through its non-existential concomitant, so people mistakenly think the non-existential is effective. The passage concludes that this is not the case.

Classification of Causal Inputs
Classifies causal inputs based on existence or absence and permits dependence on non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how causal inputs are classified into three types—existential, non-existential, and both—and how this classification informs the meaning of the complete cause.

Core Idea
Causal inputs can be existential, non-existential, or both, but non-existence is never an efficient cause.

In this passage, al-Iji presents a refined understanding of causality: while non-existence cannot directly cause existence, it can serve as a condition for the cause to take effect. He classifies the ways a thing can be involved in causing another, distinguishing between purely existential inputs, purely non-existential ones, and those that require both. This lesson unpacks that classification and clarifies the meaning of the complete cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once for general understanding, then read the English translation. Notice the author's precise language.
2
Identify the three types of causal input
Look for the three categories mentioned: involvement by existence, by non-existence, and by both. Note the examples given for each.
3
Understand the clarification about the complete cause
Read the last part where the author explains what is meant by 'the complete cause must be existent'.
This is often misunderstood.
4
Reflect on the significance of allowing dependence on non-existence
Consider why the author emphasizes that non-existence can be a condition but not an effective cause. How does this shape Islamic thought on causality?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم التحقيق: أن بديهة العقل لا تجوز كون العدم مؤثراً في الوجود، مفيداً له. ولكن تجوز أن يتوقف التأثير في الوجود على أمر عدمي كما تجوز توقفه على أمر وجودي. فعلى هذا جاز أن يكون مدخلية الشيء في وجود آخر من حيث وجوده فقط، كالفاعل، والشرط، والمادة، والصورة، وأن يكون من حيث عدمه فقط كالمانع. وأن يكون من حيث وجوده وعدمه معاً كالمعد؛ إذ لا بد من عدمه الطارئ على وجوده. فما قيل : من أن العلة التامة للوجود لا بد أن تكون موجودة أريد به أن ما له مدخل بوجوده، لا بد أن يكون موجوداً، وماله مدخل بعدمه لا بد أن يكون معدوماً، وماله مدخل بوجوده وعدمه لا بد أن يوجد ثم يعدم. هذا معنی وجود العلة التامة، وحصولها المقتضي لوجود المعلول.
Then the verification: The intuition of the intellect does not allow non-existence to be effective in existence, benefiting it. But it allows that the effect in existence may depend on a non-existential matter just as it may depend on an existential matter. Accordingly, it is permissible that a thing's involvement in the existence of another is solely in terms of its existence, like the agent, the condition, the matter, and the form; and that it is solely in terms of its non-existence, like the obstacle; and that it is in terms of both its existence and non-existence together, like the preparer (al-mu'add), because its non-existence after its existence is necessary. So what is said: that the complete cause (al-illa at-tāmma) for existence must be existent means that what has involvement by its existence must be existent, and what has involvement by its non-existence must be non-existent, and what has involvement by both its existence and non-existence must first exist then cease to exist. This is the meaning of the existence of the complete cause and its attainment necessitating the existence of the effect.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage provides a classification of causal inputs based on the verification (tahqīq) that the intellect does not allow non-existence to be an effective cause of existence, but it does allow that the effect may depend on a non-existential condition. Three types are distinguished: (1) involvement solely by existence (e.g., agent, condition, matter, form), (2) involvement solely by non-existence (e.g., the obstacle or manī'), and (3) involvement by both existence and non-existence (e.g., the preparer or mu'add), which must first exist and then cease to exist. The passage then clarifies a common statement: 'The complete cause for existence must be existent' means that each part that has involvement by its existence must be existent, those by non-existence must be non-existent, and those by both must first exist then be non-existent. This is the meaning of the existence of the complete cause necessitating the effect.

Misconception: Non-existence can be a direct efficient cause of existence.
Correction: The intellect does not allow non-existence to be effective (mu'aththir), but it can be a condition or a causal input in terms of dependence.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Causal inputs are classified into three types: existential, non-existential, and both; the complete cause requires each input to be in its required state.

The Complete Cause: Not All Parts Need Be Existent
Author denies that each part of the complete cause must be existent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why rational necessity and proof do not require every part of the complete cause to be existent, and how non-existential parts like the removal of an obstacle can be included.

Core Idea
A complete cause can include non-existent parts; only some parts must be existent.

In this passage, the author addresses a common assumption about causality: that every component of the complete cause must be present and existent. He argues that this is neither required by rational intuition nor supported by proof. By examining the role of the removal of an obstacle—a non-existential part—we see that the cause's completeness does not demand simultaneous existence of all parts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the repetition of the first sentence at the end; this emphasizes the conclusion.
The passage contains an objection-and-response structure.
2
Identify the claim and denial
The claim is that each part of the complete cause must be existent. Find where the author denies this: 'that is something that the necessity of the intellect has not judged, nor has any proof been established for it.'
3
Understand the objection and clarification
Consider the objection: if removal of the obstacle (a non-existential part) is included, then the effective agent would be non-existent. Then read the clarification: 'what has involvement by its existence...'
This clarifies that the existence of the complete cause does not require all parts to be existent simultaneously.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما أنه يجب أن يكون كل واحد من أجزائها موجوداً، فذلك مما لم يحكم به ضرورة العقل ولا قام عليه برهان أيضاً. فإن قلت : لما جعل ارتفاع المانع جزءاً من العلة يلزم كون المؤثر المفيد للوجود معدوماً. أريد به أن ما له مدخل بوجوده، لا بد أن يكون موجوداً، وماله مدخل بعدمه لا بد أن يكون معدوماً، وماله مدخل بوجوده وعدمه لا بد أن يوجد ثم يعدم. هذا معنی وجود العلة التامة، وحصولها المقتضي لوجود المعلول. وأما أنه يجب أن يكون كل واحد من أجزائها موجوداً، فذلك مما لم يحكم به ضرورة العقل ولا قام عليه برهان أيضاً.
And as for the necessity that each one of its parts be existent, that is something that the necessity of the intellect has not judged, nor has any proof been established for it. For if you say: when we make the removal of the obstacle a part of the cause, it necessitates that the effective agent benefiting existence be non-existent. It is meant that what has involvement by its existence must be existent, and what has involvement by its non-existence must be non-existent, and what has involvement by both its existence and non-existence must first exist then cease to exist. This is the meaning of the existence of the complete cause and its attainment necessitating the existence of the effect. And as for the necessity that each one of its parts be existent, that is something that the necessity of the intellect has not judged, nor has any proof been established for it.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage addresses the claim that every part of the complete cause must be existent. The author argues that this is not required by the necessity of the intellect nor by any proof. An objection is considered: if we include the removal of the obstacle (which is a non-existential part) as part of the complete cause, then the effective agent that benefits existence would be non-existent, which is problematic. The response clarifies that the statement 'the complete cause for existence must be existent' means that parts that are existent in their involvement must be existent, non-existent parts must be non-existent, and parts with both must be existent then non-existent. It does not mean that every part must be existent at the moment of causation. Therefore, it is not necessary that each part be existent simultaneously.

The Objection on Making Absence of Impediment Part of the Cause
Objection and answer about including absence of impediment in the cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Islamic theologians answer the objection that making the absence of an impediment part of the cause leads to the agent being non-existent, by distinguishing between a real part and a complement.

Core Idea
Absence of impediment is not a real part of the agent but a complement.

In discussions of causality, some objected that if the absence of an impediment is considered part of the cause, then the effective agent would be non-existent, which is impossible. The answer clarifies the meaning of 'part' in this context. This passage presents that objection and its resolution.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the opening objection in Arabic and translation. Understand the reasoning that if absence of impediment is a part, the agent would be non-existent. The objector uses an analogy with digestion.
Notice that the objection assumes 'part' means a real part of the agent.
2
Understand the answer: real part vs complement
The author answers by saying 'part' here means complement (tatimma), not a real part. This saves the theory.
The distinction is crucial for causality.
3
Reflect on the role of preparation (isti'dad)
The passage also discusses the preparer (mu'idd) and how preparation is an effect of the preparer, not an independent part. This clarifies that the absence of impediment is included in the agent's count.
This reinforces that the absence of impediment is a complement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت : لما جعل ارتفاع المانع على وجوده بعد العدم كالانهضام الجيد المتوقف على عدم شرب الماء أولاً وشربه ثانياً وأما نفس الاستعداد فلا يتوقف الوجود على عدمه وإن كان مقارنا له .
الاستعداد من أنه انحصار العلة التي يتوقف عليها المعلول باعتبار وجودها وعدمها في المعد، فالكاف مقحمة بحسب المعنى، أو بالنظر إلى الأفراد الذهنية وإن أمكن أن يناقش في الانحصار بأن نفس ذلك القبيل، مع من أثر المعد . قال في حاشية المطالع : المعد هو الموجب للاستعداد التام الذي هو القوة القريبة أعني أن يتهيا القابل للمقبول تهياً كافياً لقبوله، مقارناً لعدمه، حتى إذا وجد فيه بالفعل لم يوصف باستعداده إياه، بل بإمكان الاتصاف . فإنه لازم له لا يفارقه، ويمكن أن يدفع المناقشة المذكورة بأن الاستعداد لما كان أثراً للمعد لازماً له أدرج في عداده، ولم يعده من أجزاء العلة التامة استقلالاً .
جزءاً للفاعل، كان المؤثر فى الوجود معدوماً ، وقد اعترفتم بأنه محال بديهة . قلت : ليس معنى كونه جزءاً له أنه جزء له حقيقي، بل معناه أنه من تتمته، وداخل في عداده، وهذا المقدار كاف في الاعتذار عن ترك إفراده بالذكر ويعلم من هذا أن قوله : فيسبق إلى الأوهام أنه مؤثر إن أراد به سبق التأثير الحقيقي ،فباطل وإن أراد به سبق التأثير بمعنى المدخلية في الوجود، فهو حق، ولا محذور فيه.
قوله : (ليس معنى كونه جزءاً إلخ) على هذا التحقيق أنه جزء حقيقي له، كما ذهب إليه المصنف، بل إنه تتمته فكأنه جزء منه .
قوله : ( وهذا المقدار إلخ) أي كونه معتبراً في جانبه كاف في الاعتذار، لأنه ثبت بهذا القدر التعرض له أقسام العلة، حيث أريد بالفاعل المستقل بالتأثير ولا يتوقف على كونه جزءاً حقيقة .
For you might say: Since he made the removal of the impediment (al-mani') to its existence after non-existence like good digestion dependent on first not drinking water and then drinking it, and as for the preparation (al-isti'dad) itself, existence does not depend on its non-existence even if it is concomitant with it.
The preparation: It is that the enumeration of the cause on which the effect depends, in terms of its existence and non-existence, is restricted to the preparer (al-mu'idd). So the 'kaaf' is redundant in meaning, or with respect to the mental individuals. Even if one could discuss the restriction by saying that the same kind [is] with the one who effects the preparation. He said in the gloss of al-Mutali': The preparer is the one that causes the perfect preparation, which is the proximate potency (al-quwwa al-qariba), meaning that the recipient becomes prepared for the reception with sufficient readiness, concomitant with its non-existence, until when it exists in it actually, it is not described as preparing for it, but rather as the possibility of being described. For it is necessary for it and does not separate from it. And the aforementioned discussion can be refuted by saying that since preparation is an effect of the preparer and necessary for it, it is included in its count, and it is not considered independently as part of the complete cause.
It is a part of the agent, then the effective in existence would be non-existent, and you have admitted that it is impossible by intuition. I said: The meaning of it being a part of it is not that it is a real part of it, but rather that it is from its complement (tatimma) and included in its count, and this amount is sufficient for an excuse for not mentioning it separately. And it is known from this that his saying: 'it precedes the minds that it is effective' — if he means the precedence of real effect, it is false; and if he means the precedence of effect in the sense of having an entry (madkhaliyya) in existence, it is true and there is no problem in it.
His saying: (The meaning of it being a part, etc.) According to this investigation, it is a real part of it, as the author held, but rather it is its complement so it is as if it is a part of it.
His saying: (And this amount, etc.) i.e., that it is considered in his side is sufficient for an excuse, because by this amount the mention of the divisions of the cause is established, where what is meant by the agent is the independent in effect and does not depend on being a part in reality.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage is an objection and its answer. The objector claims that if the absence of an impediment is made a part of the cause, then the effective agent would be non-existent, which is impossible by intuition. The answer distinguishes between a real part and a part of the complement (tatimma). The commentator explains that the absence of an impediment is not a real part of the agent but is included in its count as part of its completion. He also discusses the concept of preparation (isti'dad) and the role of the preparer (mu'idd), clarifying that preparation is an effect of the preparer and not an independent part of the complete cause.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the absence of an impediment must be a real part of the agent's essence.
Correction: The passage teaches that it is a complement (tatimma) included in the agent's count, not a real part.

Checkpoint

B) Part of the complement and counted with it

Key Takeaway
The absence of an impediment is not a real part of the agent but a complement, and its inclusion in the cause does not make the agent non-existent.

Objection and Defense on the Classification of Causes
Objection and answer on classification of causes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection to the classification of internal and external causes regarding genus, species, and subject, and the defense that genus and species become material and formal causes when taken as parts, and that the subject is included due to its resemblance to matter.

Core Idea
Genus and species as parts are matter and form; the subject is like matter.

This passage addresses a sharp objection to the standard classification of causes into internal (material and formal) and external (efficient, final, etc.). The objector notes that genus and species are internal but are neither matter nor form, and that the subject (mawdu') in accidents is external but is omitted. The answer provides a nuanced defense: when considered as parts (with the condition of nothing else), genus and species become matter and form in a technical sense. Additionally, the subject, though external, so closely resembles matter as a receptive locus that it is counted among the material causes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two objections
Read the passage and find the two specific objections raised against the classification: (1) genus and species are internal but not matter/form, (2) the subject is external but not mentioned.
These objections challenge the completeness of the classification.
2
Analyze the first answer
The answer gives two approaches. First: when genus is taken as a part (with the condition of nothing else), it is called matter; similarly, species is called form. Understand how universal logical parts can be seen as material/formal causes in a certain respect.
This shows that the classification is not about universals but about concrete constituents.
3
Analyze the second answer and the subject
The second approach restricts the discussion to external existence, excluding intellectual parts. Then it justifies including the subject among internal causes because it resembles matter as a receptive locus.
This clarifies the scope of the classification.
4
Review the commentator's clarifications
The commentary explains that the objection does not affect the main investigation, that genus and species are not material/formal in the substantial sense (but that is harmless), and that the subject is considered a condition on the side of the agent.
This deepens the understanding of the distinctions involved.

قوله : (قلت ليس إلخ) هذا لا ينافي ما سبق من المصنف من أنه جزء للفاعل بالحقيقة لأن مراده أنه جزء من الفاعل المستقل بالتأثير، ومراد الشارح أنه ليس جزءاً من ذات الفاعل.
قوله: (وأما الموضوع فهو مع كونه خارجاً إلخ) وهذا بعينه هو الاعتذار عن ترك ذكر المحل القابل بالقياس إلى الصورة الجوهرية، ولتقاربهما اكتفى في الاعتذار بذكر أحدهما .
It cannot be said: Genus (jins) and species (fasl) are among the internal causes, but neither of them is matter (madda) or form (sura). Also, the subject (mawdu') in accidents is among the external causes and has not been mentioned among them. For we say: When genus is taken as a part, i.e., with the condition of nothing else, it is called matter; and when species is taken likewise, it is called form. Or we say: The discussion concerns that on which external existence depends, so intellectual parts (al-ajza' al-'aqliyya) are not included therein. As for the subject, despite being external, it resembles matter completely in that it is a receptive locus, so it is made of its count, and not.
His saying: (It cannot be said, etc.) is an objection to the original enumeration mentioned, and it has no connection to the investigation.
His saying: 'And neither of them is matter or form, etc.' In it, that neither of them is matter and form in the sense of material and formal cause is denied, and that they are not matter and form in the sense of substantial matter and form does no harm.
His saying: (Also, etc.): In it, that it is from the conditions considered on the side of the agent.
His saying: (When genus is taken, etc.): Whether it is for the composite or the simple, and likewise for species. So it is rebutted what is in it that this only holds for the composite; because its genus and species are taken from the commentary of al-Maqasid as well. And the form without the simple. From the matter.
His saying: (It is called form): i.e., in comparison to matter, so it does not contradict what preceded that each of them when taken with the condition of nothing else is a part and matter for the species.
His saying: (Intellectual parts): i.e., that on which intellectual existence depends, whether they are predicated, like genus and species if composition from equal matters is allowed, or not predicated.
His saying: (I said, 'Not', etc.): This does not contradict what preceded from the author that it is a part of the agent in reality, because his intention is that it is a part of the agent independent in effect, and the intention of the commentator is that it is not a part of the essence of the agent.
His saying: (And as for the subject, despite being external, etc.): This is precisely the excuse for not mentioning the receptive locus in comparison to the substantial form, and due to their closeness, the excuse is satisfied by mentioning one of them.

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer regarding the classification of causes. The objector claims that genus and species are internal causes but are neither matter nor form, and that the subject in accidents is an external cause but is not mentioned in the classification. The answer explains that when genus and species are considered as parts (with the condition of nothing else), they are called matter and form respectively. Alternatively, the discussion concerns external existence, so intellectual parts are not included. The subject, though external, resembles matter as a receptive locus, so it is counted among material causes. The commentator then explains each lemma, clarifying that the objection does not affect the main investigation, that genus and species are not material and formal in the strict substantial sense but that is harmless, and that the subject is considered among the conditions on the side of the agent. He also clarifies the difference between the author and commentator on the meaning of 'part'.

Objection and Answer on Internal and External Causes
Objection and answer on classification of internal/external causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator defends the classification of internal and external causes against an objection concerning genus, specific difference, and the subject.

Core Idea
Genus and specific difference are material and formal causes when considered as parts.

In this passage, the commentator addresses an objection about the classification of causes. The objector points out that genus and specific difference are internal causes but are neither matter nor form, and that the subject of accidents is an external cause but was not listed. The commentator provides two key responses to preserve the classification.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Identify the two complaints: that jins and fasl are not matter/form despite being internal causes, and that the subject of accidents is an external cause but omitted.
The objection challenges the completeness and correctness of the earlier classification.
2
Examine the first answer
Notice that when jins and fasl are taken specifically as parts (bishart la shay'), they are considered matter and form respectively.
This shows they do fall under material and formal causes.
3

المرصد الخامس - المقصد الأول : تصور احتياج الشيء إلى غيره ضروري

جزءاً للفاعل، كان المؤثر فى الوجود معدوماً ، وقد اعترفتم بأنه محال بديهة . قلت : ليس معنى كونه جزءاً له أنه جزء له حقيقي، بل معناه أنه من تتمته، وداخل في عداده، وهذا المقدار كاف في الاعتذار عن ترك إفراده بالذكر ويعلم من هذا أن قوله : فيسبق إلى الأوهام أنه مؤثر إن أراد به سبق التأثير الحقيقي ،فباطل وإن أراد به سبق التأثير بمعنى المدخلية في الوجود، فهو حق، ولا محذور فيه. لا يقال : الجنس والفصل من العلل الداخلة وليس شيء منهما مادة ولا صورة وأيضاً الموضوع في الأعراض من العلل الخارجة ولم يذكر فيها؛ لأنا نقول : الجنس إذا أخذ من حيث أنه جزء أعني بشرط لا شيء يسمى مادة، والفصل إذا أخذ كذلك يسمى صورةً، أو نقول : الكلام فيما يتوقف عليه الوجود الخارجي، فلا تندرج فيه الأجزاء العقلية. وأما الموضوع فهو مع كونه خارجاً يشبه المادة مشابهة تامة في كونها محلاً قابلاً، فجعل من عدادها، ولم يعد قسماً برأسه.

The Fifth Observation - First Objective: The conception of a thing's need for another is necessary.

As a part for the agent, the effect in existence would be non-existent, and you have acknowledged that it is impossible intuitively. I say: The meaning of its being a part for him is not that it is a real part for him, but rather that it is a completion of him and included in his enumeration, and this amount suffices as an excuse for leaving it out of mention. And from this it is known that his statement: 'it springs to the mind that it is an effect' — if he means the priority of real effect, it is false; and if he means the priority of effect in the sense of having an entry in existence, it is true, and there is no harm in it. It cannot be said: 'Genus and specific difference are among the internal causes, and neither of them is matter or form, and also the subject in accidents is among the external causes and was not mentioned there.' For we say: 'Genus, when taken insofar as it is a part, i.e., with the condition of nothing else, is called matter, and specific difference when taken likewise is called form.' Or we say: 'The discussion concerns what external existence depends on, so the mental parts are not included. As for the subject, although it is external, it resembles matter completely in being a receptive locus, so it was made from its number, and it was not counted as a separate section.'

His statement: (The meaning of its being a part etc.) According to this verification, it is a real part for him, as the author held, but rather it is his completion so it is as if it is a part of him. His statement: (And this amount etc.) i.e., its being considered in his side suffices as an excuse, because by this amount establishing the mention of the divisions of the cause is achieved, where by the agent is meant the independent agent in effect, and it does not depend on its being a part in reality.

His statement: (It cannot be said etc.) An objection to the principle of the aforementioned enumeration, and it has no connection to the verification. His statement: (And neither of them etc.) In it is that their not being matter and form in the sense of material and formal cause is denied, and their not being essential matter and form does no harm. His statement: (And also etc.) In it is that it is among the conditions considered on the side of the agent. His statement: (Genus, when taken etc.) Whether it is for a composite or for a simple, and likewise specific difference. So what is in it that this only applies to the composite is repelled; because its genus and specific difference are taken from Sharh al-Maqasid also. And the form is unlike the simple. From matter. His statement: (It is called (form) i.e., by analogy to matter, so it does not contradict what preceded that each one of them when taken with the condition of nothing else was a part and matter for the species. His statement: (The mental parts) i.e., what mental existence depends on, whether they are predicamental, like genus and specific difference if composition from coextensive things is allowed, or non-predicamental.

His statement: (I said: not etc.) This does not contradict what preceded from the author that it is a part for the agent in reality, because his intention is that it is a part of the independent agent in effect, and the commentator's intention is that it is not a part of the agent's essence. His statement: (And as for the subject, although it is external etc.) This is exactly the excuse for leaving out the mention of the receptive locus by analogy to substantial form, and due to their closeness he sufficed in the excuse by mentioning one of them.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer discussion. The objection states that genus (jins) and specific difference (fasl) are among internal causes (al-illal al-dakhila) but neither is matter (madda) nor form (sura), and also the subject (mawdu') of accidents is among external causes (al-illal al-kharija) but was not mentioned in the earlier classification. The answer provides two points: First, that when genus is taken specifically as a part (i.e., conditionally without other aspects), it is called matter, and specific difference taken likewise is called form. Second, the discussion concerns what external existence depends on, so mental parts (al-ajza' al-'aqliyya) are not included. As for the subject, although external, it resembles matter completely as a receptive locus, so it was included among the material causes and not counted as a separate division. The commentary explains each part: the author's statement about being a part is not a real part but a completion; the objection does not affect the essential point; the claim that jins and fasl are not matter and form in the sense of material and formal cause is denied; the subject is a condition on the side of the agent; the classification applies to both composites and simples; and the final excuse for not separately mentioning the subject is its close similarity to the material cause.

?

?
