Why Succession Still Implies Recreation
Commentarial objection-answer on succession and recreation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objector's attempt to avoid recreation through succession fails, and how the commentator defends the principle that two independent causes cannot produce the same individual effect.

Core Idea
Succession of causes still forces recreation or changes the effect's identity.

This passage presents a sharp objection and its refutation. The objector claims that by having the second cause act immediately after the first ceases, no recreation of the non-existent occurs. The commentator dismantles this by showing that such a scenario either involves recreation or alters the effect's individuality. Let's follow the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Objection
The objector argues that if the second cause creates existence at the exact moment the first cause's effect ceases, the effect never becomes non-existent, so no recreation occurs. Read the objection carefully.
The objection is implied in the commentary's opening line.
2
Follow the Refutation: Recreation
The commentator responds that even if the second cause acts in the next instant, the effect was non-existent in the first instant, so its re-creation is still required. Grasp the logical necessity.
The key is that existence and nonexistence cannot coexist in the same instant.
3
Consider the Alternative: Identity Change
The commentator then shows that even if we grant the objector's scenario, the effect's identity changes, so the two causes do not act on the same individual. This defeats the purpose of the objection.
This demonstrates that succession does not preserve personal identity of the effect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وجد بإيجاد الثانية بذلك الوجود، ليكون التوارد على معلول شخصي . قوله : (لزم إعادة المعدوم الكلام في التوارد لا في الإعادة فلا يرد ما وهم من أن هذا إنما يتم إذا لم يجوز إعادة المعدوم، وإنما لزم الإعادة لأنه لا يجوز أن يكون وجود الثانية في آن عدم الأولى؛ لأنه يلزم وجود المعلول وعدمه معا، إذ المفروض انعدامه في آن عدم الأولى، فيكون وجود الثانية في الآن الثاني، فيكون إعادة للمعدوم . وبهذا اندفع ما قيل : إنه يجوز أن يوجد العلة الثانية في آن عدم العلة الأولى فيزول في ذلك الآن الوجود الحاصل للمعلول بإيجاد الأولى، ويحصل الوجود الآخر بإيجاد الثانية، فلا يلزم إيجاد المعدوم؛ لأن الماهية المعلولة لم تخل عن وجود قط، ولا تحصيل الحاصل إذ الوجود الثاني مغاير للوجود الأول . نعم يلزم توارد الوجودين على طريق تعاقب التصور، ولا بد لإبطاله من دليل آخر يثبت أن المعلول الشخصي إذا زال عنه وجود فعند حصول وجود آخر يزول شخصه، ويصير شخصاً آخر، فلا تتوارد العلتان على معلول واحد بالشخص.
His saying: (exists by the bringing into existence of the second with that existence, so that the concurrent occurrence is on an individual effect. His saying: (necessitates the recreation of the non-existent) the discussion is about concurrent occurrence, not about recreation, so the illusion that this is only valid if recreation of the non-existent is not allowed does not apply. Rather, recreation is necessitated because it is not permissible for the existence of the second to be in the moment of non-existence of the first, because that would necessitate the existence and non-existence of the effect together, since it is assumed that it becomes non-existent in the moment of non-existence of the first. So the existence of the second would be in the second moment, and that would be recreation of the non-existent. And by this, what was said is refuted: that it is permissible for the second cause to exist in the moment of non-existence of the first cause, so that in that moment the existence obtained by the effect from the first cause ceases, and the other existence comes about by the bringing into existence of the second, so recreation of the non-existent is not necessitated, because the essence of the effect never lacked existence, and obtaining the obtained does not apply since the second existence is different from the first. Yes, the concurrent occurrence of the two existences on the way of succession of perception is necessitated, and it requires another proof to refute it, proving that if existence leaves the individual effect, then when another existence occurs, its individuality disappears and it becomes another individual, so the two causes do not concur on a single individual effect.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer pair within the commentary. The objector claims that the second cause can exist at the exact moment the first ceases, so no recreation occurs – the effect simply transitions from one existence to another. The commentator (the author of the sharh) responds by arguing that such a scenario still involves recreation because the existence at the second moment is after non-existence in the first moment, which is i'adat al-ma'dum. Furthermore, even if one accepts that the second existence is different, then the effect's identity changes, meaning the two causes do not act on the same individual. Thus the original thesis holds: two independent causes cannot produce the same individual effect.

Key Terms
التوارد
التعاقب
إعادة المعدوم
المعلول الشخصي
الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that as long as the effect never becomes non-existent, the second cause can add a new existence without issue.
Correction: But the commentary shows that this leads to loss of personal identity of the effect, so the two causes still do not act on the same individual.

Checkpoint
According to the response, what happens to the individual effect when a second existence occurs after the first existence ceases?
A) It remains the same individual
B) It becomes another individual
C) It becomes non-existent
D) It becomes eternal

Key Takeaway
Succession alone does not rescue the claim of two causes acting on one individual effect—it either requires recreating the non-existent or changes the identity of the effect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In which areas of your life do you rely on past spiritual 'momentum'—thinking that your previous good deeds or states of faith will carry you forward automatically? This lesson argues that each moment is a new creation. How should that change your daily reliance on Allah? Commit to one specific du'a you will make each morning, acknowledging that your faith and actions are recreated by Him every instant.
?
When you sin, do you ever feel that the 'good you' from before is gone, and a new 'bad you' takes over? This lesson shows that even a good existence, when lost, cannot be simply continued by a new existence—the individual identity changes. How does this challenge you to see repentance not as a return to an old self, but as a plea for Allah to recreate you anew? What step will you take today to seek that new creation?
?
The lesson refutes the idea that two independent causes can concur on one effect. Has there been a time you attributed a good outcome partly to your own effort and partly to Allah, as if they are two causes acting together? How does the lesson's argument that only one cause (Allah) produces each moment of existence force you to reconsider your gratitude? Write down one thing you achieved this week and consciously attribute it solely to Allah's direct creation.

The Objection of Providing Survival and Its Refutation
Commentary on the objection that the second cause provides survival and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the second cause only provides survival, why the commentator argues that this does not undermine its independence, and how the possibility of survival after the agent cause is defended.

Core Idea
Independence of a cause is not required for it to produce an effect that persists.

This passage tackles a key objection in the debate on survival (baqa'): if a second cause only sustains existence, it seems dependent on the first cause. The commentator clarifies that independence is not the issue; rather, the possibility of the effect continuing after the agent cause is the real question. We'll explore the dialectic and the alternative explanation that preserves the cause's efficacy.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and initial response
Read the Arabic passage from 'قوله : ( تفيد بقاء الوجود)' to 'فلا نطول الكتاب بإيرادها وردها'. Notice the commentator's claim that the doubts are refuted by previous clarifications.
The initial response is brief; the fuller refutation comes later.
2
Understand the alternative explanation
Read from 'ولك أن تقول بعبارة أخرى' to 'وهو المبنى لتمام الدليل'. This section offers an alternative account of how the second cause works without compromising independence.
Pay attention to the definition of a perfect cause.
3
Analyze the independence objection
Identify the opponent's claim that the second cause would not be independent, and see how the commentator counters by saying independence is not the goal; we need to prove the permissibility of survival after the agent cause.
The commentator shifts the burden of proof.
4
Note the final point on recreation
The commentator notes that the impossibility of recreating the non-existent (i'ādat al-ma'dūm) has not been proven, which undermines the opponent's proof.
This is a technical point in kalām.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( تفيد بقاء الوجود إلخ) سواء قلنا : إنه زائد على الوجود أو هو الوجود في الزمان قوله : أن لا تكون علة (مستقلة لاحتياجها في إفادة البقاء إلى أصل الوجود الحاصل بالعلة الأولى، وبما حررنا لك اندفع الشكوك التي أوردها الناظرون إن تأملت حق التأمل، فلا نطول الكتاب بإيرادها وردها . قوله : ( وإن أمكن أن يوجد إلخ) فالتوارد إنما هو في اعتبار العقل فقط . قوله : (حركة الشمس ) أي بحسب الرؤية فإنها في الحقيقة لحاملها قوله : ( مغايرة للواقعة إلخ) لأن إحداهما قائمة بالخارج والثانية مركبة من حركة قائمة من حركتي الحامل الموافق والتدوير ولا قيام للحركة بالشمس حقيقة حتى يتوارد الأصلان عليها . بالشخص. ولك أن تقول بعبارة أخرى : العلة التامة تفيد نفس الوجود من غير اشتراط أن يكون الزمان الثاني أو الأول، لكن لما وجدت العلة الثانية في آن انعدام العلة الأولى بحيث لم يتخلل بين زمان وجودي العلتين زمان آخر لزم استمرار وجود المعلول وصار باقيا. وذلك لا ينافي استقلال العلة كما لا ينافي صورة التوارد إيجاد إحدى العلتين بالفعل للمعلول استقلالاً، والأخرى المعدومة بمعنى أنها لو كانت هي الموجودة بدل العلة لكفى العلة الأولى في وجود المعلول، على أنهم ادعوا عدم جواز بقاء المعلول بعد الفاعل، وبنوا ذلك على عدم جواز توارد العلتين على سبيل التعاقب بهذا الدليل الذي ذكره الشارح، فلو سلم أن العلة الثانية على تقدير إفادتها بقاء الوجود الحاصل بالأولى يلزم عدم استقلالها يرد عليه أن الاستقلال لا يهمنا، إذ المطلوب أن يثبت جواز بقاء المعلول بعد العلة الفاعلية بأي وجه كان، وأيضاً امتناع إعادة المعدوم لم يثبت وهو المبنى لتمام الدليل . قوله : ضرورة أن الحركة الواقعة بأحد هذين الأصلين ضرورة أن التغاير بين الحركتين ليس بمجرد أن المعلول الواقع بإحدى العلتين غير الواقع بالعلة الأخرى، حتى ينافي ماجوزه سابقاً.
His saying: (It provides the survival of existence etc.) whether we say it is additional to existence or it is existence over time. His saying: that it would not be an independent cause because of its need in providing survival to the original existence obtained by the first cause. And by what we have clarified for you, the doubts raised by the inquirers are refuted, if you contemplate properly. We will not lengthen the book by mentioning them and responding. His saying: (And if it is possible for it to exist etc.) so the concurrent occurrence is only in the consideration of the intellect. His saying: (The motion of the sun) meaning according to sight, for in reality it belongs to its carrier. His saying: (different from the occurring etc.) because one of them subsists in the outer, and the second is composed of motion subsisting from the motions of the carrier, the coinciding and the rotating, and motion does not really subsist in the sun such that the two principles concur on it. But you may say in other words: The perfect cause provides the very existence without stipulation that it be the second time or the first. However, when the second cause exists at the moment of non-existence of the first cause such that no other time intervenes between the times of existence of the two causes, the continuation of the effect's existence is necessitated and it becomes lasting. And that does not contradict the independence of the cause, just as it does not contradict the form of concurrent occurrence – one of the two causes actually brings the effect into existence independently, and the other is non-existent in the sense that if it were present instead of the cause, the first cause would suffice for the existence of the effect. But they claimed the impossibility of the effect's survival after the agent, and they based that on the impossibility of two causes concurring in succession by this proof mentioned by the commentator. So even if it is granted that the second cause, assuming it provides survival of the existence obtained by the first, would entail its lack of independence, one would reply: independence does not concern us, for what is required is to prove the permissibility of the effect's survival after the agent cause in any way possible. Also, the impossibility of recreating the non-existent has not been proven, and it is the basis for the completion of the proof. His saying: necessarily that the motion occurring with one of these two principles is necessarily that the difference between the two motions is not merely that the effect resulting from one cause is different from that resulting from the other cause, such that it would contradict what he permitted previously.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on two points: the objection that the second cause provides survival (baqa') of the existence given by the first, and the example of the sun's motion. The commentator argues that if the second cause provides survival, it is not independent because it depends on the first cause for the original existence. However, he then discusses that some scholars have argued that the effect cannot survive after the agent cause, and if we grant that the second cause just provides survival, then independence is not crucial. The sun example illustrates that two principles (the outer and the rotating) produce motions that are different in species, not in individual, so they do not concur on the same individual effect. The commentary concludes that the difference between the two motions is not merely due to the causes, but because the motions themselves are different in essence.

Key Terms
بقاء الوجود
العلة المستقلة
العلة التامة
إعادة المعدوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a second cause provides survival, it must be dependent and therefore cannot be a true cause.
Correction: The commentator argues that independence is not required for the cause to be effective; what matters is whether the effect can persist after the agent, and this is not disproven by dependence.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator argue that the second cause providing survival would not be independent?
A) Because survival is not a real attribute
B) Because it needs the first cause's original existence
C) Because survival requires time
D) Because the second cause is not an agent

Key Takeaway
The second cause's provision of survival does not make it dependent in a way that undermines its efficacy; the real issue is whether the effect can survive the agent, and that remains possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often fear that your good deeds will die when you stop actively performing them. But this lesson argues that survival can be provided by a later cause without dependence. How does this challenge your assumption that your spiritual survival depends solely on your own continuous efforts? Will you trust that Allah can sustain your faith even when you feel unable to cause it yourself?
?
If today you realize that your role in maintaining your beliefs is not independent, will you cling to the ultimate Cause—Allah—and commit to one action tomorrow that you do purely relying on Him to give it lasting effect?
?
Do you sometimes feel that your faith could vanish if you stop 'causing' it through rituals? This lesson shows that survival after the agent is permissible. Are you living as if your faith's permanence depends on you? How will you hand over your spiritual continuity to the One who is independent of all causes?

The Sun Example and Concurrent Occurrence
Commentary on the sun example illustrating the impossibility of two principles concurring on the same individual effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sun example used to illustrate that two principles do not produce the same individual effect, and how this relates to the concept of concurrent occurrence (tawarud).

Core Idea
Two distinct causes cannot produce the same individual effect because their effects are different in species.

The commentator uses the example of the sun's motion to clarify a subtle point: when two principles are involved, they do not actually concur on the same individual effect. The motion we see is actually composed of different motions from different carriers, so there is no genuine concurrence. This helps refute the notion that two causes can produce the same individual effect by succession.

Guided Path
1
Read about concurrent occurrence
Read the first sentence: 'قوله : ( وإن أمكن أن يوجد إلخ) فالتوارد إنما هو في اعتبار العقل فقط'. Understand that concurrent occurrence (tawarud) is only a mental consideration, not real.
This sets the stage for the sun example.
2
Understand the sun example
Read the part about the sun's motion: 'قوله : (حركة الشمس ) أي بحسب الرؤية...' Notice that the motion is attributed to the sun only visually; in reality it belongs to a carrier.
The motion is not a single entity but composite.
3
Why the two principles do not concur
Read 'قوله : ( مغايرة للواقعة إلخ)...' The commentator explains that one motion subsists externally and the other is composed of two motions (coinciding and rotating) from the carrier. They are different in reality, so they cannot concur on the same individual effect.
The distinction is crucial for the argument against tawarud.
4
Grasp the final point on difference
Read the last sentence: 'قوله : ضرورة أن الحركة...' The commentator emphasizes that the difference between the two motions is not merely due to different causes; the motions themselves are different.
This reinforces that the effects are distinct individuals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( تفيد بقاء الوجود إلخ) سواء قلنا : إنه زائد على الوجود أو هو الوجود في الزمان قوله : أن لا تكون علة (مستقلة لاحتياجها في إفادة البقاء إلى أصل الوجود الحاصل بالعلة الأولى، وبما حررنا لك اندفع الشكوك التي أوردها الناظرون إن تأملت حق التأمل، فلا نطول الكتاب بإيرادها وردها . قوله : ( وإن أمكن أن يوجد إلخ) فالتوارد إنما هو في اعتبار العقل فقط . قوله : (حركة الشمس ) أي بحسب الرؤية فإنها في الحقيقة لحاملها قوله : ( مغايرة للواقعة إلخ) لأن إحداهما قائمة بالخارج والثانية مركبة من حركة قائمة من حركتي الحامل الموافق والتدوير ولا قيام للحركة بالشمس حقيقة حتى يتوارد الأصلان عليها . بالشخص. ولك أن تقول بعبارة أخرى : العلة التامة تفيد نفس الوجود من غير اشتراط أن يكون الزمان الثاني أو الأول، لكن لما وجدت العلة الثانية في آن انعدام العلة الأولى بحيث لم يتخلل بين زمان وجودي العلتين زمان آخر لزم استمرار وجود المعلول وصار باقيا. وذلك لا ينافي استقلال العلة كما لا ينافي صورة التوارد إيجاد إحدى العلتين بالفعل للمعلول استقلالاً، والأخرى المعدومة بمعنى أنها لو كانت هي الموجودة بدل العلة لكفى العلة الأولى في وجود المعلول، على أنهم ادعوا عدم جواز بقاء المعلول بعد الفاعل، وبنوا ذلك على عدم جواز توارد العلتين على سبيل التعاقب بهذا الدليل الذي ذكره الشارح، فلو سلم أن العلة الثانية على تقدير إفادتها بقاء الوجود الحاصل بالأولى يلزم عدم استقلالها يرد عليه أن الاستقلال لا يهمنا، إذ المطلوب أن يثبت جواز بقاء المعلول بعد العلة الفاعلية بأي وجه كان، وأيضاً امتناع إعادة المعدوم لم يثبت وهو المبنى لتمام الدليل . قوله : ضرورة أن الحركة الواقعة بأحد هذين الأصلين ضرورة أن التغاير بين الحركتين ليس بمجرد أن المعلول الواقع بإحدى العلتين غير الواقع بالعلة الأخرى، حتى ينافي ماجوزه سابقاً.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The motion of the sun is a single individual effect that could be produced by two different principles concurrently.
Correction: The commentator shows that the perceived motion is actually composed of different motions from different carriers, so it is not a single individual effect that could be produced by two principles.

Checkpoint
According to the sun example, why do the two principles not concur on the same individual motion?
A) Because motion does not really subsist in the sun
B) Because the two motions are different in reality and species
C) Because the sun has only one carrier
D) Because the intellect cannot conceive it

Key Takeaway
The sun example demonstrates that two principles produce motions that are different in species, so they cannot produce the same individual effect, undermining the argument for concurrent occurrence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a blessing, do you find yourself dividing credit between Allah and the means through which it came? How does this lesson's rejection of concurrent occurrence challenge that habit?
?
If concurrent occurrence is only an intellectual consideration, then in your daily life, which 'secondary causes' do you tend to treat as if they truly share in bringing about an effect? What would it mean to consistently attribute every effect to Allah alone?
?
Consider a recent situation where you thought two factors together caused an outcome. How might reframing that as a single cause (Allah's act) change your gratitude, trust, or reliance? Are you willing to make that shift starting today?

beginner
5 min
The Rule of a Single Individual Not Having Two Independent Causes
Sharh al-Mawaqif states the rule and addresses the objection about succession.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a single individual effect cannot have two independent causes, how succession on a replacement basis is possible, and why the sun's motion example is not a counterexample.

Core Idea
One individual effect, one independent cause — no exceptions.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif establishes a key causal principle: a single individual (one in person) cannot be produced by two independent causes. It then addresses a common objection about succession and clarifies using an example from astronomy. Understanding this rule is essential for kalam discussions on divine attributes and creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the flow of the argument. Notice the rule, the objection, and the response.
The passage is dense; read it twice.
2
Identify the main rule
The first sentence states the rule: 'The one in person is not caused by two independent causes.' Understand what 'one in person' (wāḥid bi al-shakhṣ) means—an individual entity.
3
Understand the objection
The objection claims the second cause only preserves the existence conferred by the first. If so, it would not be independent. The author replies that succession on a replacement basis is permissible under certain conditions.
Succession (tawārud) means one cause after another, not at the same time.
4
Analyze the author's response
The author explains that succession is possible if the causes can replace each other. He then refutes the claim that succession is absolutely impossible by showing the logical consequence: if one cause exists and the other is absent, the effect exists from the first and would be absent from the second's absence, which is contradictory.
This reinforces that two independent causes for the same individual effect are impossible.
5
Examine the astronomical example
Some think that the sun's motion from the epicycle and deferent (two principles) shows two independent causes. The author clarifies that the motion from each principle is different in type (nawʿ), not one in person (shakhṣ). Thus, they are not the same individual effect, so the rule stands.
Distinguish between 'one in type' and 'one in person.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الخامس - المقصد الثاني : الواحد بالشخص لا يعلل بعلتين مستقلتين . يقال : إن الثانية تفيد بقاء الوجود الحاصل بالأولى؛ إذ يلزم حينئذ أن لا تكون علة مستقلة، فالتوارد على سبيل البدل جائز إذا كانت العلتان بحيث إذا وجدت إحداهما وجود الأخرى بعدها، وإن أمكن أن توجد بدل الأولى ابتداء، لا يقال : التوارد على البدء محال مطلقاً؛ لأنه إذا كانت إحداهما موجودة، والأخرى معدومة، لزم من وجود الأولى وجود المعلول ومن عدم الثانية عدمه؛ لأن عدم العلة المستقلة عدم المعلول، وما يظن من أن أصلي الخارج والتدوير يجوز تواردهما بدلاً على حركة الشمس فجوابه أن المعلول هاهنا أعني حركة الشمس واحد بالنوع، لا بالشخص ضرورة أن الحركة الواقعة بأحد هذين الأصلين مغايرة للواقعة بالأصل الآخر
The Fifth Observatory - The Second Objective: The one in person (al-wāḥid bi al-shakhṣ) is not caused by two independent causes. It is said: The second (cause) confers the continuation of the existence obtained by the first; for then it would follow that it is not an independent cause. So succession (al-tawārud) on the basis of replacement is permissible if the two causes are such that when one of them exists, the other exists after it, and if it is possible that it exists as a replacement for the first from the beginning. It is not said: succession from the beginning is absolutely impossible; because if one of them exists and the other is non-existent, from the existence of the first follows the existence of the effect and from the non-existence of the second follows its non-existence, for the non-existence of the independent cause is the non-existence of the effect. And what is supposed, that the two principles of the epicycle (al-khārij) and the deferent (al-tadwīr) can alternate as a replacement for the sun's motion, the answer is that the effect here, namely the sun's motion, is one in type (bi al-nawʿ), not in person (bi al-shakhṣ), necessarily because the motion occurring by one of these two principles is different from the motion occurring by the other principle.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage states the fundamental rule that a single individual (one in person) cannot be effectuated by two independent causes. It then presents an objection: the second cause only preserves the existence given by the first, so it would not be independent. The author explains that succession (tawārud) on a replacement basis is possible if the two causes are such that one exists after the other, and if one can replace the other from the start. He refutes the claim that succession is absolutely impossible, using the example of the two principles of astronomical motion (the epicycle and deferent) which some thought could alternate on the sun's motion. The answer is that the sun's motion here is one in type, not in person, because the motion from each principle is different. Thus, this example does not violate the rule.

Correction: The author clarifies that the sun's motion from the epicycle and deferent are different in type (naw'), not one in person (shakhs). Therefore, they are not the same individual effect, so the rule is not violated.

D) Because the two principles are not real causes

Key Takeaway
A single individual effect requires only one independent cause; succession of causes is possible only if they replace each other and the effect is not individual in person.

Independence of the Second Cause and Preservation of Existence
Commentary explaining the independence of the second cause and preservation of existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a second cause can be independent in its causal efficacy despite depending on the first cause for the original existence of its effect.

Core Idea
A cause's independence is about efficacy, not temporal or existential precedence.

This gloss from a major work of kalam clarifies a subtle point in causal theory. The commentator examines whether the second cause's need for the first cause to provide the initial existence compromises its independence. He argues that independence remains intact because the second cause acts without temporal gap, preserving the effect's existence. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and addresses common doubts.

Guided Path
1
Read the gloss on 'confers continuation'
Begin by reading the first part of the Arabic, where the commentator explains that continuation of existence is either additional to existence or identical with existence in time.
This sets the stage for the independence discussion.
2
Understand the independence of the cause
The second part argues that the cause's need for original existence does not negate its independence. The commentator claims this resolves many doubts.
The key is that the second cause gives continuation, not original existence.
3
Examine the alternative expression
Read the paragraph starting 'ولك أن تقول' which offers a clearer formulation: the complete cause gives existence itself without temporal condition; when the second cause appears at the moment the first ends, the effect persists.
This is the heart of the argument.
4
Analyze the sun's motion example
The commentator uses the sun's motion to illustrate that succession is only mental, not real—the motion is attributed to the sun only in appearance.
This counters objections about two causes alternating on one effect.
5
Address the objection about continuation after the agent
Finally, the commentator argues that even if independence were compromised, the real aim is to prove the possibility of an effect outlasting its agent. The impossibility of recreating what perished is not established.
This refutes a major opposing school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( تفيد بقاء الوجود إلخ) سواء قلنا : إنه زائد على الوجود أو هو الوجود في الزمان . قوله : أن لا تكون علة (مستقلة لاحتياجها في إفادة البقاء إلى أصل الوجود الحاصل بالعلة الأولى، وبما حررنا لك اندفع الشكوك التي أوردها الناظرون إن تأملت حق التأمل، فلا نطول الكتاب بإيرادها وردها . قوله : ( وإن أمكن أن يوجد إلخ) فالتوارد إنما هو في اعتبار العقل فقط . قوله : (حركة الشمس ) أي بحسب الرؤية فإنها في الحقيقة لحاملها قوله : ( مغايرة للواقعة إلخ) لأن إحداهما قائمة بالخارج والثانية مركبة من حركة قائمة من حركتي الحامل الموافق والتدوير ولا قيام للحركة بالشمس حقيقة حتى يتوارد الأصلان عليها . بالشخص. ولك أن تقول بعبارة أخرى : العلة التامة تفيد نفس الوجود من غير اشتراط أن يكون الزمان الثاني أو الأول، لكن لما وجدت العلة الثانية في آن انعدام العلة الأولى بحيث لم يتخلل بين زمان وجودي العلتين زمان آخر لزم استمرار وجود المعلول وصار باقيا. وذلك لا ينافي استقلال العلة كما لا ينافي صورة التوارد إيجاد إحدى العلتين بالفعل للمعلول استقلالاً، والأخرى المعدومة بمعنى أنها لو كانت هي الموجودة بدل العلة لكفى العلة الأولى في وجود المعلول، على أنهم ادعوا عدم جواز بقاء المعلول بعد الفاعل، وبنوا ذلك على عدم جواز توارد العلتين على سبيل التعاقب بهذا الدليل الذي ذكره الشارح، فلو سلم أن العلة الثانية على تقدير إفادتها بقاء الوجود الحاصل بالأولى يلزم عدم استقلالها يرد عليه أن الاستقلال لا يهمنا، إذ المطلوب أن يثبت جواز بقاء المعلول بعد العلة الفاعلية بأي وجه كان، وأيضاً امتناع إعادة المعدوم لم يثبت وهو المبنى لتمام الدليل . قوله : ضرورة أن الحركة الواقعة بأحد هذين الأصلين ضرورة أن التغاير بين الحركتين ليس بمجرد أن المعلول الواقع بإحدى العلتين غير الواقع بالعلة الأخرى، حتى ينافي ماجوزه سابقاً
His saying: (confers the continuation of the existence etc.) whether we say that it is additional to existence or that it is existence in time. His saying: that it is not an independent cause because of its need, in conferring continuation, for the original existence obtained by the first cause. And by what we have elucidated for you, the doubts brought by the investigators are repelled if you reflect properly, so we will not lengthen the book by mentioning them and refuting them. His saying: (and if it is possible that it exists etc.) then succession is only in the consideration of the intellect. His saying: (the sun's motion) i.e., according to sight, for in reality it belongs to its bearer. His saying: (different from that occurring etc.) because one of them is subsisting in the epicycle and the second is composed of a motion subsisting from two motions: the motion of the carrier and the deferent, and there is no subsistence of motion in the sun in reality such that the two principles would alternate on it. In person. And you may say in another expression: The complete cause (al-ʿilla al-tāmma) gives existence itself without the condition that it be the second or first time, but when the second cause exists at the moment of the annihilation of the first cause such that no time intervenes between the times of existence of the two causes, then the continuation of the existence of the effect follows and it becomes permanent. And that does not negate the independence of the cause, just as it does not negate the form of succession: one of the two causes actually brings the effect into existence independently, and the other is non-existent in the sense that if it were the one existing instead of the cause, the first cause would suffice for the existence of the effect. However, they claimed the impossibility of the effect continuing after the agent, and they built that on the impossibility of two causes succeeding one another based on this proof mentioned by the commentator. So even if it is granted that the second cause, on the assumption that it confers the continuation of the existence obtained by the first, entails the lack of its independence, it is objected that independence does not concern us, because the required thing is to prove the possibility of the effect continuing after the agent cause in any way. Also, the impossibility of re-creating what has perished (iʿādat al-maʿdūm) is not proven, and it is the foundation of the complete proof. His saying: necessarily that the motion occurring by one of these two principles: necessarily that the difference between the two motions is not merely that the effect occurring by one of the two causes is other than that occurring by the other cause, such that it contradicts what he allowed previously.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary (gloss) on the previous main text. It begins by explaining the phrase 'confers the continuation of existence,' discussing whether continuation is additional to existence or identical with existence in time. It clarifies that the independence of a cause is not compromised by its need for the first cause's original existence to confer continuation. The commentator then asserts that the doubts raised by scholars are repelled by this explanation. Next, it explains that succession is only a mental consideration. The gloss on 'sun's motion' clarifies that the motion is attributed to the sun only by sight, not in reality. The commentary then offers an alternative expression: the complete cause bestows existence itself without temporal condition; the second cause's effect is the permanence of existence when there is no temporal gap. This does not negate independence, as the two causes do not act simultaneously. The commentator then addresses a school that denies the continuation of an effect after the agent, showing that even if independence is compromised, the real issue is proving the possibility of continuation. Finally, it emphasizes that the difference between the two motions in the sun example is qualitative, not just a numerical distinction.

The Second Argument: Enumeration and Reduction
Enumeration of three possibilities and reduction to the first argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument that each cause must have an effect, leading to absurdity, and how it reduces to the first argument's contradiction.

Core Idea
The second argument reduces to the first because it also ends in simultaneous need and independence.

The author presents a second argument against two independent causes for one effect. He enumerates three possibilities and shows each fails. An objection leads to a return to the first argument's contradiction between need and independence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three possibilities
Read the first part of the passage to see the three divisions: either each cause has an effect, only one does, or neither does. Note why each is invalid under the assumption of independence.
2
Understand the objection
Notice the objection that each cause might have a complete influence without being a part. This seems to avoid the earlier division.
3
Follow the reduction
The reply shows that complete influence leads to independence from each cause, which implies both need and independence—a contradiction, thus returning to the first argument.
4
Reflect on the example
Consider the epicycle and deferent example mentioned: their motions differ in type, so it is not a true case of two causes on one effect, but the passage uses it to illustrate the absurdity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الخامس - المقصد الثاني : الواحد بالشخص لا يعلل بعلتين مستقلتين = ۱۲۱ شخصاً؛ لأنا نقول : استلزام عدم العلة لعدم المعلول الشخصي، يتوقف على أنه لا يجوز أن يكون لواحد شخصي علتان مستقلتان على البدل فكان إثباته به دوراً . الوجه ) الثاني : إما أن يكون لكل واحد منها أثر أي تأثير ( فكل) أي كل واحد منهما ) جزء العلة التامة ) ؛ لأن المستقل بالتأثير حينئذ هو المجموع فهو العلة التامة، وكل واحد منهما ،جزؤها، وهو خلاف المفروض ، ( أو لأحدهما ) فقط أثر، ( فهي العلة ) دون الأخرى ( أولا ) أثر ( لشيء منهما، فلا شيء منهما بعلة)، وكلاهما أيضاً خلاف المقدر، فالأقسام كلها باطلة. وقد يقال : جاز أن يكون لكل منهما تأثير تام المتنازع فيه وليس يلزم منه كون كل جزء العلة فإن قلت فيستغنى بتأثير كل واحدة عن تأثير الأخرى، قلت: هذا رجوع إلى الوجه الأول، فتأمل. ( وجوزه ) أي كما هو قوله : (أي تأثيره فسر الأثر بالتأثير؛ لأنه إذا فرض العلتين على الواحد الشخصي الذي هو الأثر كان الأثر لكل منهما قطعاً، فلا معنى للترديد بأن يكون لكل منهما أثراً ولا يكون . قوله : ( وليس يلزم منه إلخ ) لأنه إنما يلزم إذا كان لكل واحدة منهما تأثير ناقص. قوله : ( فيستغنى إلخ ) أي إذا فرض تأثير تام لكل منهما فيستغنى إلخ . قوله : ( هذا رجوع إلى الوجه الأول لأن الاستغناء عن تأثير كل منهما بسبب تأثير الأخرى ليس محالاً، لأن تأثير الأخرى فرع احتياجه إليهما، إذ لا تأثير بدون الحاجة، فيلزم استغناؤه وهو كاف في إثبات المطلوب وحينئذ يكون التعرض للترديد المذكور لغواً. فاندفع واحتياجه معاً، ما توهم من أن كون دليل مقدمة من دليل آخر لا يقتضي أن يكون الثاني رجوعاً إلى الأول. من توارد العلتين على معلول شخصي على سبيل البدل ابتداء، وأنه ظاهر البطلان . كيف ولو صح لصير إليه من أول الأمر في إثبات المطلوب من غير احتياج إلى التطويل؟ بل بخصوصية كون العلتين الخارج والتدوير، وهذا الحكم الضروري مبني على تصور الخارج والتدوير وحركتهما بكنههما ، بل قد يدعى التغاير النوعي أيضاً بناء على أن الحركة الواقعة بأصل الخارج حركة واحدة بسيطة إذا لم يعتبر حركة الأوج، وبأصل التدوير مركبة من حركتين، حركة التدوير، وحركة الحامل الموافق، وهما نوعان مندرجان تحت مطلق حركة الشمس . قوله : (قلت: هذا رجوع إلى الوجه الأول (فتأمل وجه الأمر بالتأمل أن حاصل هذا الوجه الاستدلال بلزوم استغناء المعلول عن العلة وحاصل الوجه الأول الاستدلال بلزوم اجتماع النقيضين أعني الاحتياج والاستغناء والفرق بين الوجهين في بادي النظر ظاهر لكن لما كان يرد على هذا الوجه أنه إن أريد لزوم الاستغناء من جميع الوجوه فلا نسلم الملازمة لجواز أن يكون المعلول باعتبار علية كل منهما مستغنياً عن الأخرى وباعتبار علية الأخرى محتاجاً إليه وإن أريد لزوم الاستغناء في الجملة فلا نسلم بطلان اللازم فيحتاج إلى أن يقال المراد هو الأول ويلزم مما ذكرته حينئذ اجتماع الاحتياج والاستغناء وهو محال، فقد رجع هذا الوجه إلى الوجه الأول .
The Fifth Observatory - The Second Objective: The one in person is not caused by two independent causes = 121 persons; for we say: The implication of the non-existence of the cause for the non-existence of the individual effect depends on the fact that it is not permissible for an individual one to have two independent causes on a replacement basis, so proving it by it would be circular. The second argument: Either each of them has an effect (i.e., an influence), so each of them is a part of the complete cause; because the independent in influence then is the aggregate, so it is the complete cause, and each of them is a part of it, which is contrary to the assumption. Or only one of them has an effect, so it is the cause, not the other. Or neither has an effect, so neither is a cause. Both are also contrary to what is supposed. So all the divisions are invalid. And it is said: It is possible that each has a complete influence concerning what is disputed, and it does not necessitate that each be a part of the cause. For if you say: then it is rendered independent of the influence of each by the influence of the other, I say: this is a return to the first argument, so reflect. (And he allowed it) i.e., as it is. His saying: (i.e., its influence; he interpreted 'effect' as influence because if the two causes are assumed on the one individual which is the effect, then the effect belongs to each definitely, so there is no meaning to the hesitation about whether each has an effect or not). His saying: (and it does not necessitate etc.) because it would only necessitate if each has a partial influence. His saying: (then it is rendered independent etc.) i.e., if a complete influence is assumed for each, then it is rendered independent etc. His saying: (this is a return to the first argument) because being independent of the influence of each due to the influence of the other is not impossible, because the influence of the other is a branch of its need for both, for there is no influence without need, so its independence together with its need follows, and that is sufficient to prove what is required. Then the exposure to the mentioned hesitation becomes pointless. So it is repelled, together with the misunderstanding that the proof of one premise from another does not necessitate that the second be a return to the first, regarding the succession of two causes on an individual effect on a replacement basis from the beginning, and that it is evidently invalid. How so? For if it were valid, it would have been adopted from the very start in proving what is required without need for prolongation. Rather, by the specificity that the two causes are the epicycle and the deferent, and this necessary judgment is based on the conception of the epicycle and deferent and their motion in their essence. Rather, generic difference may also be claimed based on the fact that the motion occurring by the principle of the epicycle is a single simple motion if the motion of the apogee is not considered, and by the principle of the deferent is composed of two motions: the motion of the deferent and the motion of the carrier, and they are two types subsumed under absolute solar motion. His saying: (I said: this is a return to the first argument; so reflect. The point of reflection is that the upshot of this argument is the proof of the necessity of the effect's independence from the cause, and the upshot of the first argument is the proof of the necessity of the conjunction of the two contradictories, namely need and independence. And the difference between the two arguments is apparent at first glance. But because it is objected against this argument that if what is meant is independence in all respects, we do not grant the implication because it is possible that the effect, considering causality of each, is independent of the other, and considering causality of the other, is in need of it; and if what is meant is independence in a general way, we do not grant the impossibility of the consequent, so one must say that what is intended is the first, and then from what you mentioned follows the conjunction of need and independence, which is impossible. So this argument has returned to the first argument.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument against the possibility of two independent causes for one individual effect. It begins by stating that the implication of the non-existence of a cause for the non-existence of the individual effect depends on the impossibility of two independent causes alternating, otherwise it would be circular. Then it gives the second argument: either each cause has an effect, making each a part of the complete cause (contrary to assumption), or only one has an effect (making the other not a cause), or neither has an effect (so neither is a cause). All three divisions are invalid. Then an objection is raised: perhaps each cause has a complete influence without being a part, but then the effect would be independent of each due to the other, which the author says is a return to the first argument. The glosses further explain: if both have complete influence, the effect would be independent of each, which implies both need and independence—a contradiction. The passage then discusses the example of the epicycle and deferent, noting that their motions are different in type, so it is not a true case of succession on a single individual. Finally, it concludes that the second argument reduces to the first because it leads to the same contradiction of simultaneous need and independence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The second argument is a distinct, independent proof that does not rely on the first argument.
Correction: The author shows that the second argument ultimately leads to the same contradiction of simultaneous need and independence, thus reducing to the first argument.

Checkpoint
Why does the author consider the second argument to be a return to the first argument?
A) Because both arguments use the same proof
B) Because the second argument also ends up requiring both independence and need simultaneously, which is impossible
C) Because the second argument is invalid on its own
D) Because the first argument is more fundamental

Key Takeaway
The second argument against two independent causes reduces to the first argument's contradiction of simultaneous need and independence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

This argument exposes the inconsistency: you cannot claim independence from any cause while still needing it. Where in your life is this contradiction most real?
?
The reduction to the first argument shows that any attempt to posit two independent sources of support leads to a clash between need and independence. Have you ever tried to balance trust in Allah with reliance on worldly means, as if they are independent? How does this argument challenge you to purify your trust?
?
The epicycle and deferent example shows that claiming multiple causes for one effect fails because the effects are not truly one. In your life, when you credit multiple factors for your successes or failures, are you mistakenly combining them into one effect? How can you see every outcome as solely from Allah, without denying means?

The Second Argument: Exhaustive Division of Possibilities for Two Causes
Al-Jurjani presents a divisional argument against two independent causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that exhaustively divides the possibilities for two supposed independent causes of one effect and shows each possibility is impossible, thereby disproving the claim.

Core Idea
Two independent causes for one effect? Every possibility fails.

This passage presents the second dialectical argument against the idea that an individual effect could have two independent causes. The argument works by enumerating all logical possibilities and showing each contradicts the assumption of independence. It is a classic example of qismah (division) used in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the structure: a division into three cases.
2
Identify the three cases
List the three possibilities: (1) each cause has an effect, (2) only one has an effect, (3) neither has an effect. Write them down.
These are the divisions of the argument.
3
Understand why each case fails
For each case, explain why it contradicts the assumption that both causes are independent and that there is a single effect.
The commentary will help.
4
State the conclusion
The argument concludes that all possibilities are impossible, so the original claim is false. Summarize in one sentence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني: إما أن يكون لكل واحد منها أثر أي تأثير فكل أي كل واحد منهما جزء العلة التامة؛ لأن المستقل بالتأثير حينئذ هو المجموع فهو العلة التامة، وكل واحد منهما جزؤها، وهو خلاف المفروض، أو لأحدهما فقط أثر، فهي العلة دون الأخرى أو لا أثر لشيء منهما، فلا شيء منهما بعلة، وكلاهما أيضاً خلاف المقدر، فالأقسام كلها باطلة.
The second aspect: Either each of them has an effect, i.e., influence, so each is a part of the complete cause; because the independent in influence then is the aggregate, so it is the complete cause, and each is a part of it, which is contrary to the assumption; or only one of them has an effect, so it is the cause without the other; or neither has an effect, so neither is a cause; both are also contrary to the supposition. So all divisions are invalid.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument (الوجه الثاني) against the possibility of two independent causes producing a single individual effect (الواحد بالشخص). The argument is a division of possibilities: either each cause has an effect (influence), or only one has an effect, or neither has an effect. If each has an effect, then the aggregate is the true independent cause, and each is only a part, contradicting the assumption of independence. If only one has an effect, that one is the sole cause, and the other is irrelevant. If neither has an effect, then neither is a cause. All three possibilities contradict the original claim of two independent causes, so the claim is invalid.

Key Terms
علة تامة
أثر
تأثير
مستقل
واحد بالشخص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If each cause has an effect, then they are two independent causes working together.
Correction: If each has an effect, then the true independent cause is the combination, making each only a part. Thus they are not independent.

Checkpoint
What does the second argument conclude about the possibility of two independent causes for a single individual effect?
A) It is possible if each cause has a partial effect.
B) All possible scenarios lead to contradiction, so it is impossible.
C) It is possible if one cause is primary and the other secondary.
D) It is possible only if the effect is not individual.

Key Takeaway
The second argument divides all possibilities into three, and each leads to contradiction, proving that two independent causes for one effect is impossible.

Objection and Answer: Complete Influence from Each Cause
An objection claims each cause can have complete influence, and the answer reduces it to the first argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection that each cause can have complete influence reduces to the earlier argument and does not avoid the contradiction.

Core Idea
Complete influence from each cause leads to self-sufficiency, contradicting the need for two causes.

The author presents a potential objection against the argument that two causes cannot each have complete influence on a single effect. The objection claims that each cause could have complete influence without being a part of a combined cause. The response shows that this leads to the same contradiction as before, forcing the reader to reflect on the logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its literal translation to understand the structure of the objection and answer.
Notice the author's use of 'جاز' (it is permissible) to introduce the objection.
2
Identify the objection
The objector claims that each cause can have a complete influence on the single effect, without each being a mere part of the cause. This challenges the previous argument that two causes cannot each be fully influential.
3
Understand the answer
The author responds by pointing out that if each cause has complete influence, then the effect would be independent of each cause because the other's influence suffices. This leads to a contradiction similar to the first argument, so the objection fails.
The phrase 'فتأمل' (so reflect) invites the reader to see the collapse of the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يقال: جاز أن يكون لكل منهما تأثير تام المتنازع فيه وليس يلزم منه كون كل جزء العلة فإن قلت فيستغنى بتأثير كل واحدة عن تأثير الأخرى، قلت: هذا رجوع إلى الوجه الأول، فتأمل.
And it may be said: It is permissible that each has a complete influence on the matter under dispute, and it does not entail that each is a part of the cause. For if you say: Then it is dispensed with the influence of each by the influence of the other, I say: This is a return to the first aspect, so reflect.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer. The objector proposes that each cause could have a complete influence ('تأثير تام') on the single effect, without each being a mere part of a combined cause. The author responds: if each has complete influence, then the effect would be independent of each cause because the other's influence suffices, leading to the first argument's contradiction of simultaneous need and independence. The command 'فتأمل' (reflect) invites the reader to see that this objection collapses into the earlier argument.

Key Terms
تأثير تام
متنازع فيه
استغناء
رجوع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if each cause has complete influence, then there is no need for the other cause to be part of a combined cause, thus avoiding the previous problem.
Correction: The author shows that complete influence from each cause implies that either cause alone suffices, which contradicts the need for two causes. The objection therefore collapses into the earlier argument.

Checkpoint
Why does the author consider the objection that each cause has complete influence as a return to the first argument?
A) Because complete influence implies each cause is unnecessary.
B) Because it contradicts the assumption that the effect is individual.
C) Because it leads to the effect being independent of both causes.
D) Because it implies the causes are not independent.

Key Takeaway
The objection of complete influence fails because it leads to the same contradiction of simultaneous need and independence, reinforcing the original argument.

The Mu'tazila Atom Example and Its Refutation
The Mu'tazila example of an atom simultaneously pushed and pulled is presented and refuted.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's argument for two independent causes using the atom example and the two responses to it.

Core Idea
Two independent causes cannot produce one effect; independence is conditional on solitude.

The Mu'tazila presented a concrete example to argue that two independent causes can concur on a single effect, challenging a key Ash'ari principle. The passage explains the example, the logical reasoning, and two ways to refute it. Let's examine the argument and its responses.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazila example
Read the example of an atom attached to two hands, one pushing and one pulling with equal force and speed. Consider why the atom can have only one movement.
The atom is a single indivisible substance (جوهر فرد).
2
Follow the logical steps
Notice the reasoning: cannot have two movements (impossible for two similar things), cannot attribute to just one hand (no preference), so must attribute to both, each independently producing the same movement. The conclusion is two independent causes on one effect.
This is the Mu'tazila argument for concurrence of independent causes.
3
Examine the refutations
Study the two responses: (1) Ash'ari: movement is directly from God, not from the hands. (2) Alternative answer: movement is from the combination, each hand a part of the cause, not independent, because independence was conditional on acting alone.
The alternative answer avoids the problem of two independent causes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجوزه بعض المعتزلة، كجوهر فرد ملتصق بيد اثنين، يدفعه أحدهما حال ما يجذبه الآخر على السوية في القوة والسرعة، وحينئذ لا يجوز أن يقوم بذلك الجوهر الذي لا جزء له حركتان؛ لامتناع اجتماع المثلين، بل حركة واحدة شخصية. ولا يجوز استنادها إلى واحد منهما فقط؛ لعدم الأولوية؛ بل إلى كل منهما. ولا شك أن كل واحد منهما مستقل بتحصيل تلك الحركة، فقد اجتمع على واحد بالشخص علتان مستقلتان، ورده الأشاعرة بأن حركة ذلك الجوهر مستندة إلى الله تعالى ابتداء كسائر الحوادث، ولغيرهم أن يجيبوا عنه بأن هذه الحركة مستندة إلى مجموعهما معاً، فكل واحد جزء العلة لا علة مستقلة، فإن استقلال كل منهما كان مشروطاً بانفراده عن الآخر، ولا محذور في ذلك.
And some Mu'tazila allowed it, like a single atom attached to two hands, one pushing it while the other pulls it, equally in strength and speed. Then it is not possible for that atom, which has no part, to have two movements due to the impossibility of the conjunction of two similar things, but rather one individual movement. Nor is it permissible for it to be attributed to only one of them due to lack of preference, but rather to both. And there is no doubt that each is independent in producing that movement. So two independent causes have concurred on a single individual. The Ash'aris refuted it by saying that the movement of that atom is attributed to God Almighty initially, like all other events. And for others, they may answer that this movement is attributed to both together, so each is a part of the cause, not an independent cause, for the independence of each was conditional on its being alone from the other, and there is no contradiction in that.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage presents a concrete counterexample from some Mu'tazila: a single atom (جوهر فرد) being simultaneously pushed by one hand and pulled by another with equal force and speed. The atom cannot have two movements (impossible for two similar things to coexist), so it has one individual movement. It cannot be attributed to just one hand because no preference, so it must be attributed to both, each independently causing the same movement. Thus, two independent causes produce one individual effect. The Ash'ari refutation: like all events, this movement is directly from God, not from the hands. An alternative response: the movement is from the combined action of both hands, so each is only a part of the cause; their independence was conditional on acting alone, which is not the case here.

Key Terms
جوهر فرد
مثلان
استقلال
اشتراط
علة مستقلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The atom example proves that two independent causes can produce one effect.
Correction: The alternative response shows that independence is conditional on acting alone, so in this scenario each hand is not independent but a part of a combined cause.

Checkpoint
According to the alternative response to the Mu'tazila's atom example, how should the movement of the atom be attributed?
A) To each hand independently
B) To both hands together as a combined cause
C) Directly to God
D) To the stronger hand

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila atom example attempts to show two independent causes, but both refutations deny the independence in different ways.

Two Similar Causes and the Distinction Between Type and Individual
The author distinguishes causing a type vs. an individual with two similar causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between causing a single individual and causing a single type, and why two similar causes can cause the latter but not the former.

Core Idea
Two similar causes together can produce a type, not a single individual.

The discussion of causation often considers whether two causes can combine to produce one effect. In this passage, the author specifies an important distinction: two causes that are similar in type can together cause a single type (multiple individuals of that type) but not a single individual. This clarifies a subtle point in causal theory.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the author's statement. Notice that it is a short but precise statement about causation.
The passage uses technical terms like 'مثلان' and 'واحد بالنوع'.
2
Identify the key terms
Note the terms 'مثلان' (two similar things), 'واحد بالنوع' (one in type), 'واحد بالشخص' (one individual) as they are central to the distinction.
Understanding these terms is essential for grasping the argument.
3
Understand the distinction between type and individual
Realize that 'one in type' means sharing the same species or category, while 'one individual' means a single particular entity. The author allows two similar causes to cause a type (i.e., multiple individuals of that type) but not a single individual.
This is a key nuance in kalam causal theory.
4
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself why this distinction matters. It shows that two independent causes cannot produce the exact same effect (same individual) but can produce effects of the same kind.
This prevents a potential confusion in arguments about causality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المثلان، فهما واحد بالنوع فيجوز تعليله أي تعليل الواحد بالنوع بمستقلتين، على معنى أن فرداً منه
As for two similar things, they are one in type, so it is permissible to cause a single type by two independent causes, meaning that an individual of it
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage makes a distinction: two similar causes (مثلان), like two identical movers, are one in type (واحد بالنوع). It is permissible for them to jointly cause a single type (واحد بالنوع), meaning an individual of that type can be caused by two independent causes. This is different from causing a single individual (واحد بالشخص), which was the main subject. The passage is incomplete but clearly distinguishes between causing an individual and causing a type.

Key Terms
مثلان
واحد بالنوع
واحد بالشخص
تعليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if two causes are similar, they can jointly produce a single effect (individual).
Correction: The passage clarifies that two similar causes can produce a type (multiple individuals of the same kind) but not a single individual.

Checkpoint

A) Causing a type is permissible with two independent causes, while causing an individual is not.
B) Both are equally impossible with two independent causes.
C) Causing an individual is permissible with two independent causes, but a type is not.
D) There is no difference; both are impossible.

Key Takeaway
Two similar causes can produce multiple individuals of the same type, but cannot combine to produce a single individual.

Proof that One in Person Cannot Have Two Independent Causes
Presents a Mu'tazili objection and two Ash'ari responses on one movement from two causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazili argument for two independent causes on a single atom and the Ash'ari rebuttal that all motion is from God or that the causes are only parts of a single cause.

Core Idea
One movement cannot have two independent causes; divine causation resolves the puzzle.

In this passage, al-Sanusi presents a challenging argument from the Mu'tazila about causality. They claim that if a single atom is pulled equally by two hands, the resulting movement must come from both, making two independent causes. The Ash'ari response, however, shows that all motion is from God, preserving the principle that a single effect has a single cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the English translation. Notice the hypothetical scenario and the logical steps.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the Mu'tazili scenario and reasoning
Find the example of the atom attached to two hands. Understand why two movements are impossible and why the movement cannot be assigned to one hand alone.
The key point is 'la ulaawiyya' (no preference).
3
Understand the Ash'ari rebuttal
The passage gives two responses: the Ash'ari one that God creates all movement, and an alternative that both hands together are a single cause.
Notice how each response preserves the principle that an effect has one cause.
4
Reflect on the lesson
Consider why divine causation is a stronger rebuttal than the alternative answer. Think about how this argument affects our understanding of causality.
This ties into the broader Ash'ari doctrine of occasionalism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعليل الواحد الشخصي بعلتين مستقلتين ( بعض المعتزلة، كجوهر فرد ملتصق بيد اثنين، يدفعه أحدهما حال ما يجذبه الآخر على السوية في القوة والسرعة )، وحينئذ لا يجوز أن يقوم بذلك الجوهر الذي لا جزء له حركتان؛ لامتناع اجتماع المثلين، بل حركة واحدة شخصية. ولا يجوز استنادها إلى واحد منهما فقط؛ لعدم الأولوية؛ بل إلى كل منهما . ولا شك أن كل واحد منهما مستقل بتحصيل تلك الحركة، فقد اجتمع على واحد بالشخص علتان مستقلتان، ورده الأشاعرة بأن حركة ذلك الجوهر مستندة إلى الله تعالى ابتداء كسائر الحوادث، ولغيرهم أن يجيبوا عنه بأن هذه الحركة مستندة إلى مجموعهما معاً، فكل واحد جزء العلة لا علة مستقلة، فإن استقلال كل منهما كان مشروطاً بانفراده عن الآخر، ولا محذور في ذلك.
Explaining the one in person by two independent causes (some Mu'tazilites, like a single atom attached to two hands, one pushes it while the other pulls it equally in strength and speed), then it is not permissible for that atom, which has no part, to have two movements; due to the impossibility of the meeting of the two similars, but rather one personal movement. And it is not permissible for it to be attributed to only one of them; due to lack of preference; but rather to each of them. And there is no doubt that each of them is independent in producing that movement, so two independent causes have come together upon one in person. And the Ashʿarites refuted it by saying that the movement of that atom is attributed to God Almighty initially like all other events, and for others they may answer it by saying that this movement is attributed to both of them together, so each one is part of the cause not an independent cause, for the independence of each of them was conditioned upon its being alone from the other, and there is no problem in that.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage is an argument and refutation regarding whether one thing in person can be caused by two independent causes. The Mu'tazilites present a case: an indivisible atom pulled equally by two hands. Since two identical movements cannot coexist, there is only one movement. It cannot be from one hand alone because there is no preference, so it must be from both. Thus two independent causes produce one movement. The Ash'ari rebuttal is that all movements come from God, not from creatures. An alternative answer is that the movement is from both together, but each hand is only a part of the cause, and its independence is conditional on being alone—so the causes are not truly independent. The author concludes there is no contradiction.

Key Terms
الواحد بالشخص
علة مستقلة
اجتماع المثلين
الأولوية
الأشاعرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Mu'tazili argument successfully proves that two independent causes can produce one effect.
Correction: The Ash'ari response shows that the movement is from God (the true cause) or that the hands together form a single cause, so the independence of each hand is conditional on being alone.

Checkpoint
Why do the Mu'tazilites argue that the atom's movement must be from both hands?
A) Because two movements cannot coexist in one atom
B) Because there is no preference for one hand over the other
C) Because the hands are equal in strength
D) Because God creates the movement

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazili example of two hands pulling one atom fails because divine causation is the true source of all movement, or because the hands together form a single compound cause.

Rule for Two Similars: Independent Causes for Individuals
Defines the permissibility of independent causes for two similars of the same species.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that two similar individuals of the same species can each be caused by independent causes, and that this does not imply the specific nature itself is caused by multiple causes.

Core Idea
Two similar things of the same kind can each have its own independent cause.

This lesson examines a key rule in causality: while a single thing cannot have two independent causes, two things that are identical in species can each be explained by a separate cause. The author clarifies a common misunderstanding and illustrates it with an example of relations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the author's precise wording: 'يجوز تعليله بمستقلتين' and the clarification that follows.
The passage is compact; read slowly to grasp the distinction.
2
Identify the rule and its limitation
Focus on the phrase 'على معنى أن فرداً منه يكون معللاً بعلة مستقلة' and the negation 'لا على معنى أن الطبيعة النوعية توجد...'. Understand that the rule applies to individuals, not the specific nature as a universal.
The common mistake is to extend the permissibility to the specific nature itself.
3
Analyze the example
Look at the example of the relation (المخالفة) between blackness and sweetness. Notice that the two relations are similar in nature but reside in different subjects, allowing each to have an independent cause.
This illustrates the rule concretely.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المثلان، فهما واحد بالنوع فيجوز تعليله أي تعليل الواحد بالنوع بمستقلتين، على معنى أن فرداً منه يكون معللاً بعلة مستقلة، وفرداً آخر منه مماثلا للأول يكون معللا بعلة أخرى مستقلة أيضاً، لا على معنى أن الطبيعة النوعية توجد في ضمن الأفراد عن علل متعددة إذ ليس في الأعيان إلا الأشخاص كما مرت إليه الإشارة ) كالمخالفة فإن مخالفة السواد للحلاوة، مثل مخالفة الحلاوة للسواد)، فإن هذين المعروضين، وإن كانا متخالفين في الماهية إلا أن عارضيهما متماثلان فيها.
As for the two similars, they are one in species, so it is permissible to explain it, i.e., explaining the one in species by two independent causes, meaning that an individual of it is explained by an independent cause, and another individual from it similar to the first is explained by another independent cause as well, not meaning that the specific nature exists within individuals from multiple causes since there is nothing in existence except individuals as has been indicated to by the reference, like the relation, for the relation of blackness to sweetness is like the relation of sweetness to blackness, for these two subjects, although they are different in essence, their accidents are similar in it.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage clarifies a key distinction: while one thing in person cannot have two independent causes, two things that are similar (مثلان) and belong to the same species can each be caused by independent causes. The author emphasizes that it is not the specific nature (الطبيعة النوعية) itself that is caused by multiple causes, but rather the individual instances. An example is given: the relation (مخالفة) of blackness to sweetness is similar to the relation of sweetness to blackness. These two relations are in different subjects (blackness and sweetness) but are themselves similar in essence. Each relation can have its own independent cause, whether that cause is the subject alone or the subject with something else. The point is that when multiple individuals share an identical property, each individual's property can be explained by a different cause.

Key Terms

الواحد بالنوع
الطبيعة النوعية
المخالفة
علة مستقلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the permissibility extends to the specific nature itself
Correction: The text limits it to individuals; the specific nature as a universal is not caused by multiple causes.

Checkpoint
What does the example of the relation of blackness to sweetness illustrate?
A) That two independent causes cannot produce the same type of relation
B) That two similar relations in different subjects can each have an independent cause
C) That blackness and sweetness are identical in essence
D) That the specific nature of a relation cannot be caused by multiple causes

Key Takeaway
Two similar individuals of the same species can each be caused by an independent cause, but the specific nature itself is not caused by multiple causes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see two people with the same habit or flaw, do you automatically assume the same cause? How might this lesson about independent causes for individuals change how you judge others? Will you now consciously consider that each person's actions may stem from a different cause?
?
This lesson emphasizes that the specific nature exists only in individuals. In your own life, do you tend to treat people as categories (e.g., 'all impatient people are alike') and ignore their unique circumstances? What would it take for you to see each person as an individual with their own independent causes?
?
The example of blackness and sweetness shows that relations can be similar but arise from different subjects. Think of a relationship in your life that you assumed was the same as another similar relationship. Have you overlooked its uniqueness? How can you adjust your perspective to honor the independent causes in that relationship?

Two Independent Causes: Clarifying 'Mustaqillatayn'
Al-Jurjani glosses the term 'mustaqillatayn' as different in type.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the commentator glosses 'mustaqillatayn' as different in type and how this affects the causality of individual particulars.

Core Idea
Two independent causes of the same effect must differ in nature.

Al-Jurjani's gloss on 'mustaqillatayn' sharpens the thesis: an individual cannot have two causes unless they are different in kind. This lesson unpacks that clarification and its implication for causality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the key terms: الواحد بالشخص, علة مستقلة, مستقلتين, تماثل المعلولين.
Pay attention to the gloss: 'ay mukhtalifatayn' (i.e., different in type).
2
Understand the need for the gloss
Why does the commentator clarify that 'independent' means different in type? Without this, one might mistakenly think any two independent causes could cause one effect.
3
Grasp the logical conclusion
The outcome: similarity of two effects does not force similarity of their causes. This is a key point in debates about causality.
Reflect on how this connects to the broader discussion of causality in Islamic theology.
4
Self-check with the quiz
Answer the checkpoint question to confirm your understanding of the gloss.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الخامس - المقصد الثاني : الواحد بالشخص لا يعلل بعلتين مستقلتين
قوله : (مستقلتين) أي مختلفتين فيكون حاصل المسألة أن تماثل المعلولين لا يستدعي تماثل عليتهما.
The Fifth Observation - Second Purpose: The individual particular cannot be caused by two independent causes. His saying: (mustaqillatayn) i.e., different in type, so the outcome of the issue is that the similarity of the two effects does not necessitate the similarity of their causes.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage defines the problem and clarifies a key term. The author (al-Iji) states that an individual particular cannot be caused by two independent causes. The commentator (al-Jurjani) explains that 'independent' here means different in type, not just any two causes. The conclusion is that the similarity of two effects does not imply their causes must be similar. This sets up the discussion about whether a species can have multiple causes.

Key Terms
الواحد بالشخص
علة مستقلة
مستقلتين
تماثل المعلولين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'mustaqillatayn' means any two independent causes, not necessarily different in type.
Correction: The commentator explicitly says 'ay mukhtalifatayn' meaning different in type, so the two causes must differ in kind.

Checkpoint
What does the commentator mean by 'mustaqillatayn' in this passage?
A) Two independent causes of the same type
B) Two independent causes different in type
C) Two sequential causes
D) Two simultaneous causes

Key Takeaway
The individual particular cannot have two independent causes unless they are different in type, and similarity of effects does not necessitate similarity of causes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a success, do you mentally list multiple independent causes (e.g., your effort, a friend's help, luck)? How does this lesson's assertion that one effect cannot have two independent causes of the same type challenge you to see Allah as the singular ultimate cause, and how will you intentionally redirect your gratitude to Him alone starting today?
?
Think of a person you judge negatively. Is your judgment based on assuming that because their action resembles a bad action (similar effect), the cause (intention) must be similar to yours? How can the principle 'similarity of effects does not necessitate similarity of causes' free you from false assumptions and move you to compassionate inquiry instead of condemnation?
